THE RED PAGE.
The Future of the Australian Com-
monwealth.
I establishment of the Commonwealth of
ustralia has been repeatedly described as
e advent of a new nation. But the use
the word nation in connection with that
ither metaleptical, or has a prospective
to a future consummation of the work
ie Australian people and the Imperial
nt of the British Empire have, so far,
>erformed in uniting the inhabitants of
ralian continent and the island of Tas-
one political organisation of the federal
hat work has been accomplished under a
nee of the sovereignty of the Imperial
mt of the British Empire over the terri-
iprised in the Commonwealth ; and if the
tion is used in a limited sense to designate
used community in occupation of a denned
and in possession of large powers of
tonomy, but without reference to the
of the sovereign power from which all
authority exerciseable in the community
y proceeds, then, undoubtedly, a distinct
nay exist in a state of subjection or sub-
on to an external sovereignty. But the
mts of a province or appendage of
are not transformed into a distinct nation,
ull and perfect meaning of the word, when
s invested by the sovereign power of chat
with a large measure of autonomy it
to all their local affairs and interests, but
subject to the paramount legislative and
ve and judicial authority of the sovereignty
has conferred that measure of autonomy
iem.
ie absence of any genealogically ana his-
y distinctive origin, the only foundation ot
rate nationality, in the full sense of the
is political; and from the strictly political
>f view separate nationality does not exist,
Drdance with the proper use of the word,
it separate sovereignty. The forces which
:e separate sovereignties in separate eon-
neous and homolingual communities are
r distinguishable in character and influence
the separate descriptions of geographical,
teal and political. These several forces
;e reciprocally upon one another, and their
ictivity and product are largely affected by
tal physical environment of the community,
tinctive characteristics have already begun to
r in the Anglo-Australian who is bom of Aus-
a parentage upon Australian soil, and lives
i life upon it, although the factor of a separ-
olitical sovereignty is as yet absent. lhe
ary of the Commonwealth of Australia con-
es a total physical environment sufficiently
so from the physical environments of
aopulations of Great Britain and Irelan
-operate effectually with the social forces
dually at work in the Commonwealth,
separate social segregation, to produce a
ict type of human life and character. And
ccessive generations, born upon Australian
,nd nurtured under Australian influences a
actively Australian type of human character
become more and more emphatic, and will
ly distinguish the native Australian from his
anguineous and homolingual contemporaries
urope and America.
it if this distinctively Australian type of
an character is to come to a natural maturity,
is to make its largest possible contribution to
multiform civilisation of the world, it
its full and perfect expression m a dista t
onal life, which cannot be fully evoived with-
complete political autonomy and its attendant
Abilities and burdens. If the younger and
populous and less powerful of two consan-
aeous and homolingual communities is subject
he sovereignty and ultimate control
he older and larger and more powerful com-
nity, the social and political ideals and aims of
sovereign community will dominate and mould
, social and political life of the dependent com
nity to a degree which will always make it, to a,
ger or lesl extent, a social and political
itation of the dominant community , and
that extent will obstruct the natural evolution
the social and political ideals and aims and
ititutions in which an independent nationality
iuld find its natural and legitimate expression.
the Commonwealth of Australia remain
: ever an appendage of the British
npire, it will never contribute to the
Xy of the world the distinctive ideals and
Movements which a distinctively Australian
•tion would add to the common stock of human
•perience and accomplishment; because so long
fSSSfa-» app<™i*s» •‘rss&rs
hrntv it will never reach that maturity o
itiohal life which requires for its evolution a
■alisation of national individuality and a con-
dousness of capacities and opportunities which
nly complete autonomy can perfectly awaken.
But it must be clear to every one who, without
ny warping prepossession of his p’ mmom
xamines the present relations of the Common
wealth of Australia to the Imperial Parliament and
o the British Crown, that those relations cannot
iontinue after the population and power
Commonwealth have increased to an extent which
Mil make it fully able to resist, without Imperial
assistance, any attempt by a foreign enemy to in-
vade its territory or to destroy its commerce. The
large increase in the future in the proportion of
the* population of the Commonwealth which will
be born upon Australian soil will be attended by
a large increment of a definite Australian senti-
menf in the Commonwealth, which will resist, as
♦neongruous with the local interests of the Com-
monwealth, and with its capacities of self-govern-
ment and its powers of self-protection, the
paramount legislative authority of the Imperial
Parliament, and the power of the Crown h> dis-
allow any legislation of the Parliament of the
Commonwealth and any amendment of the Con-
stitution of the Commonwealth which has received
the ratification of the votes of a majority of the
electors of the Commonwealth. A prudent disuse
of the royal power of veto upon the legislation ot
the Commonwealth, and an equally prudent con-
sultation of Australian opinion and sentiment in
regard to all proposed legislation by the Imperial
Parliament which will affect the Commonwealth,
may prolong the connection of the Commonwealth
with the Empire for a long period. But the time
will inevitably come when Australian sentiment
will demand the abolition of the subordination
of the Parliament of the Commonwealth to the
royal power of veto upon its legislation, ana
the paramount legislative power of an externa
sovereignty; and the abolition of that subordina-
tion will proclaim the advent of an Austra lan
sovereignty and full and complete Austra lan
nationality.
The advocates of that nebulous conception of
the future relations of Great Britain and her
dependencies which they describe as lmperia
Federation may continue to refer regretfully to the
separation of the United States of America
from Great Britain, but a strong preconception in
favor of a particular period in the past history ot
the world could find plausible reasons to regret the
dismemberment of the empire of Charlemagne,
and the evolution of the separate nations which
subsequently occupied the territories which 1
comprised. When the advocates of a perpetual
union among all the component communities ot
the British Empire employ the word “federation
to describe a connection which will continue tie
present paramount legislative power of the Britis 1
Parliament and the disallowing prerogative ot tne
Crown in regard to the legislation of all the
subordinate Parliaments in the Empire, they
make a total misuse of the word “federation as
a recognised term of political nomenclature, and
thereby unconsciously conceal from themselves
the true character of the connection which they
contemplate. A federation of all the component
communities of the British Empire, in the tiue
meaning of the word, would mean the abolition ot
the present legislative power of the British i arlia-
ment over such dependencies as Canada and Aus-
tralia, and the erection of another legislative
authority through which such dependencies would
share in the possession and exercise ot the
sovereign power of the empire. But a political
union of that character would mean the incor-
poration of the United Kingdom of Great Britain
and Ireland in an organisation in which its his-
toric institutions would be intensely incongruous,
and in which the divergent industrial and com-
mercial interests of the several members ot the
union would create recurring antagonisms tending
to disruption, if they were made subject to
a common legislative control. If, on the
contrary, the British Parliament is to re-
tain its present paramount legislative authority
over the whole Empire, then Canada and Aus-
tralia will remain provincial dependencies ot
that Empire, without participation in the pos-
session and exercise of sovereign power and with-
out any international recognition as nations among
nations.
The propagandists of a perpetual connection
between Great Britain and her consanguineous
and homolingual dependencies assert that
all the necessary foundations of a poli-
tical unity are found in their racial
origin, their unity of language and the
similarity of their political institutions and
methods. But these alleged foundations of
political unity do not obliterate those differences
in geographical position and physical environment
which create in widely-separated territories, with
diverse climates and productions, divergencies m
industrial and commercial pursuits and interests
and antagonistic immigrational relations with
other countries ; and any attempt to regulate all
such divergent interests and antagonistic relations
in a manner that may seem beneficial to the major
part of the Empire must produce periodical dis-
content and more or less resistance m particular
portions of it. . ,
T-he Commonwealth of Australia has already
had an example of the manner m which the
allegation may be made that the interests of the
whole Empire may be affected, and their supre-
macy may be invoked, for the purpose of over-
riding the legally and constitutionally-expressed
wishes of the majority of the AustralianP^P l ®'
There was not a provision of the Bill to consti
tute the Commonwealth of Australia which had
undergone a more exhaustive discussion in each
of the three meetings of the Convention of 1897,
and in the previous Convention of 1891, than that
which prohibited appeals from judgments of the
High Court of the Commonwealth to the Judicial
Committee of the Privy Council m cases in which
an interpretation of the Constitution of the Com
monwealth was involved. Yet, when the Bill was
under discussion in the Imperial Parliament, the
Secretary of State for the Colonies openly allied
himself with the defeated and discontented
minority in Australia, and, as has been graphi
cally and correctly stated by an independent
observer, “appealed to its representatives to
cable to him the opinions of public men, of
influential representatives of the mercantile com-
munity, and a section of the newspapers; and,
having obtained them, he did not scruple to use
them as a set-off against the declared wishes of
the responsible Governments of a.majority of the
colonies.” —(Atlantic Monthly, March, 1901, p.
40G ) The Colonial Secretary, m his blindness,
persuaded himself that he. was using the solid
phalanx of obedient imperialists behind him to
maintain the unity of the Empire;; bu ihe builded
better than he knew, and exhibited to the Aus
tralian people a pregnant object-lesson illustrative
of what they may expect in the future from their
subordination to the Imperial Parliament.
Another pregnant object-lesson of the same
character was the disallowance by the Crown,
upon the advice of the same Colonial Secretary,
of an Act passed by the Parliament of Queensland
to prohibit the employment of colored an
industry which was to be subsidised by the State.
This instance of imperial interference with Aus-
tralian legislation, and the reason given, by the
Colonial Secretary in justification of it, viz., that
the Crown could never consent to the exclusion of
any of its subjects from any portion. of ite
dominions, demonstrates clearly that the geo
graphical position of the Commonwealth of Aus
tralia necessitates the acquisition ofanindepend
ent autonomy by the Commonwealth, for' the
future protection of its social and industrial
interests against the influx of Asiatics, m regardg
whom legislative exclusion frem other SjrtooOh*
Empire is not equally urgent. Ihe increase oi
facilities of intercourse and communication among
tee inhabitants of different portions of the surface
of the elobe,. by which it is frequently alleged
that the*practical difficulties in the way of a closer
political uidon of Great Britain and her dependen-
cies will be removed, will make more imperative
to the Commonwealth of Australia the acquisition
of an independent autonomy for the regulation of
its relations with its Asiatic neighbors ; because
the diminution of the natural protection provided
by geographical distance against Asiatic immigra-
tion will make more necessary a pohtatal protec
tion against it. The people fnd Par ment ot
the Commonwealth have declared emphatically
for a White Australia, and if they and thenr sue
cessors are faithful to that declaration toe con
tinuance of the Commonwealth as an Integra
portion of an Empire which contains m l io
colored subjects of diverse races will become every
year more nearly impossible.
Tf the sole purpose and consummation of all
social organisation and poetical je
satisfaction of the physical appetite, and necesa
ties of the human animal, the sentiment ot
Let national individuality might be regarded . *»
a useless excrescence, or, at best, as a* ten f■ y
and imperfect factor in social evolution. Bute
perfect efflorescence and fruition of all social
organisation and political activity are found m the
various tvpes of human excellence which they
have evolved. “The glory that was &reece and
the o-randeur that was Rome, as they were exni
bited in the realm of political activity are pr
served for the world m the record, oftdelvesa
deeds of men like Lyctirgus 1 brides, BpaWmon
da« Cincinnatus, Fabius Maximus, and the t o
Scipios. The glory and the grai}deur °f tl e
national life of France are enshrined m the lustr
of such names as St. Lojns, GhanceHor L Hopita ,
Oolieny Sully, Richelieu, Mirabeau, Carrel, and
Gambetta. The national greatness of England is
SSI in such builders of Edward I
Drake, Pym, Cromwell, Vane, Comers,
I am, and Gladstone. The, frgition of. t
al life of the Anglo-American Repubhc is
in Washington, Franklin, the two Adamses,
son, Hamilton Maddieon, Livingston
,a,ll Webster, Sumner, and Lincoln, ana
co-workers in the task of building up their

icial existence. Only ae the eons end

; the provincial and subordinate position which
ada has occupied within the British Empire

jublic ; and a like impossibility of such .career®

’he vital root of the personal force and m-
ince of the moulders and directors of the des-
ies of nations is found in that patriotic emotion
ich only attains its highest strength and endur-
:e and its greatest clearness and directness of
ion, when the possessor of it realises .that the
tere, within which he acts and his influence
;ends, embraces the totality of the uation s
erests and all its international relations,
is experience is clearly impossible in a
pendent and subordinate community, and
pseudo-patriotism that endeavors to embrace
widely-extended Empire which includes dif-
ent races of men and distinctly different civilisa-
ns, and divergent, if not antagonistic, industrial
d commercial interests, will be nothing more
in a corroding passion for aggrandisement and
mination. Such pseudo-patnotism can work
lv evil for humanity, and if there is any sub
intial foundation for the hope of a continual
ogress in the welfare of the human race, it must
found in a continuation of the processes by
lich the previous stages in the evolution of
rilisation have been attained. Ini the' realm of
ilitical activity this process has been the division
the population of the world into separate
■gregates located in diverse physical environ-
ents and evolving, m association with distinct
ilitical organisations, distinct types of human
laracter aid capacity. Unity of nee and lan-
will doubtless at all times produce
references in the sympathies and 1
t nations; and the sympathy and affection of
a independent Australia would always flow out

American Republic in preference to all the other
ations of the world ; and the influences of all the
uman qualities and national characteristics which
ave made England great and glorious woMd not
ie less beneficent for humanity if they proceeded.
Lm the activities of three or four
tations which traced their descent from the
ame original stock. Three or four such
lations would exert a more powerful and
nore controlling influence upon international
relations than that which would be exercised by
m extended and mighty Empire whose inter-
aational policy was directed from one controlling
rentee ?t is beyond dispute that the combing
voices of England and America m any inter-

of t larger British Empire, of which
the United States were so many provincial

Fus-land America, Canada and Australia speak-
£as separate nations, and with separate and

far more powerful and beneficent influence than
the “fetation of a world-wide empire, towards
which the other nations of the worid be
constantly prone to adopt an attitude of jealousy,
suspicion and resentment.
If the foregoing observations and deductions
are well-founded, it is very evident that the
wisest statesmanship in relation to such depend-
encies of the British Empire as Canada and
Australia on the part of the Imperial Parliament
and the Ministers of the Crown m England is to
refrain from any attempt m the name of imperial
interests to obstruct the evolution of their separ-
ate nationalities, and rather to assist m the
consummation of the glorious work which the
Mother Country has so far accomplished in the
erection of the foundations of a family of nations,
which are destined to encircle the earth as so
many homes and fortresses of the highest type of
political excellence and social well-being.
CIVIS AUSTBALLENSIS
The Purpose of Art.
As artists, Rembrandt and Rossetti stand
at the opposite poles. The one was a
master; the other never got beyond the
stage of being a student, and an experimenter.
The one was an apostle of realism, the realism
which Zola interpreted in literature ; the other of
a fervid religious romanticism. Yet both were
great influences in Art-the Dutch realist and the
English-Italian pre-Raphaelite —and a true appre-
ciation and fair judgment of the relative values of
I the ideas of Rembrandt and Rossetti, as expressed
or sought to be expressed in their works, would
give a full answer to that vexed question : What
is the Purpose of Art ? Rossetti strove as valiantly,
if not as skilfully, as Rembrandt to champion an
ideal utterly antagonistic to that of the Hol-
lander ; and perhaps more of the truth was with
the inferior painter. ~ . . _
Rembrandt aimed, above all things, at realism.
All notions of idealism he rejected utterly.
Soldiers, beggars, burghers, women, he painted as
they were, not sparing a shadow, a crease, or a
wrinkle. He refused to believe that classic
nymphs were in any degree different from the
Dutch vrows who stood as his models, and whose
care-worn, toil-worn faces were matched with
almost grotesque and painful realism by care-
worn, toil-worn bodies, wrinkled with child-bear-
ing and the coarse labor of the field and cottage.
His “Diana Bathing” is a Dutch woman who has
had at least 10 children, and at least 40 years of
hard-working life ; she bathes in a stream, not in
a large wooden tub, but that is the o y
concession to idealism. As a picture of
some Prau Stoffel it would probably be
absolutely lifelike, if that Frau could be
imagined attempting so picturesque a bath, or
indeed any bath at all. This and other Pictures,
in which no grossness of physical detail, however
unpleasant, is spared, showed Rembrandt s reso-
lution not only to be real, but to carry on an
aggressive campaign against idealism. Simp
realism would have contented itself with P. amtl ”»
suitable subjects in real fashion, and leaving the
domain of conventionally-ideal nudes alone,
that did not satisfy Rembrandt. He must put on
his canvas bearded angels, an alarming y *
tomical Adam, and various t e
models for whom obviously sold fish on the quays
of Amsterdam. It is not unreasonable to divine
in that a deliberate campaign against conventional
ideals in painting, against the idealistic bcauty of
nude studies, against the suppression of the un-
pleasant by artists. Similarly Zola, castmgthe
gage of defiance at the romanticists, and P lea dmg
for realism, deliberately deals with the disgusti g,
saying with Whitman — .
I am determined to tell, you with courageous, clear voice,
to prove you illustrious
How superb and how divine is your body, or any part
Rossetti, with Madox Brown, was theapostle of
the pre-Raphaelites who did. so much to infuse
new life into English art His ar^ de *
exact antithesis to that of Rembrandt. Inaseries
of pictures and designs, beginning with the gi
hood of Mary Virgin” and “Ecce Ancilla
Domini” and ending with (say) The Blessed
Damozel,” he pleaded powerfully forareturno
the symbolism, the imaginativeness, the poetry aim
the religious spirit of painting before Ra
His women are practically all of the one type-
the type of the Easter lily and the seraph-
showing a pure sensuousness, tinged most ofte
with tinder melancholy. Very seldom do they

MINING ENGINEER.
“Ontario,” Nicholson Street,
Gentlemen, —
Burwood, N.S.W.,
March 8, 1900.
“ On the Ist Sept., 1898, I became over-boated,
then suddenly exposed to a piercing cold wind, I
caught a chill, the result being that I was deprived
of robust health, and for about seven months my
life was despaired of. During this time X had been
through the hands of eight medical men in private
and public hospitals. The majority of them held
out no hope, some of them declaring that I had
kidney, liver, heart and lung troubles. In the last
Hospital, my medical attendapt said I had fright’s
Disease of the kidneys, that he could do nothing
for me, and that I had better be taken home, as I
could not recover. Rather a dark prospect this,
considering that, in addition to the troubles
enumerated above, I had dropsy to a fearful extent.
I had been tapped in the lower part of the abdomen
five times, several gallons of water being drawn off,
but all to no good purpose. In a few days after
being tapped, I swelled up in my legs, abdomen,
and Stomach, larger than before. The dropsy
began to make its appearance in my hands and face.
My body sw r elled to ; that degree that I sickened to
look at myself, and many times wished I could die.
Friends who visited me sympathised and bid me,
as they believed, a last farewell. But an angel of
mercy came in the shape of Warner’s Safe Cure.
A lady .friend strongly recommended me to give up
hospitals, doctors and'medicines, and take only
Warner’s Safe Cure. On the 3rd April, 1899, I
began to take Warner’s Safe Cure. As soon as I
began to take this medicine my appetite began to
return. At the end of four weeks, when I had
drank four bottles of Safe Cure, the water began to
come away. I reduced in circumference around
the abdomen from 54| inches to 41-| inches, and my
legs reduced in proportion. I felt like a new man.
My spirits returned. I began to get regular sleep,
still reducing in size until not a vestige of lung,
kidney, liver or dropsy trouble could be seen or
feft. lam now in the best of health, and am firmly
convinced that Warner’s Safe Cure lias, in God’s
been the means of restoring my lost health
■Slid -strength. I would gladly write, talk, or sing
the praises of Warner’s Safe Cure, but will con-
clude by stating that I shall be only too happy to
answer any questions, either by letter or otherwise.
I feel I owe a duty to the world at large in making
known the almost miraculous cure effected in my
case by Warner’s Safe Cure. I also feel a deep
debt of gratitude to the discoverer of Warner’s Safe
Cure, and trust this humble, unsolicited testimonial
may be made public for the benefit of suffering
humanity. lam well-known all over the Australian
Colonies* and my old mining Contemporaries will
rejoice to learn of my restoration to health.”
S. E. RICHARDS,
Mining Engineer.
Messrs. 11. 11. Warner & Co., Ltd.,
Melbourne. Vic.

Morbid Months of Misery.
“A DESPAIRING WRECK OF HUMANITY.”
A Great Friend iri Time of Need;
TJje Case of W[r. H. B 'I^NETTi
(by a special reporter.)
It is worthy of note that the interviews
which have from time to time appeared in
these columns are singularly free from mere
theorising about the subjects dealt with,
and are entirely confined to practical, though
none the less interesting, details, an addition
to which is made by Henry Barnett, of No.
212 James-street, Leichhardt.
“To show what important events meo
times arise from trivial causes,” said the
gentleman mentioned, “Imust inform you
that one day I scratched my elbow with a
piece of zinc, which was hanging loosely in-
side a waggon. It was nothing to grow
alarmed about, I thought, and I took no
notice of it until the next day, when my
arm appeared to be slightly inflamed, but I
.did.not take any precaution to stop it get-
ting anv worse. Not till a w eek afterwards
did I bother myself and by that time my
arm was so much swollen, and the pain was
so intense, that I could not go any longer
without seeking some advice.”
“ Where did you go for it?” the repor-
ter enquired.
“ To the hospital, and on three successive
visits my arm was operated upon. It was
then that I realised the great disaster which
had overtaken me, as the doctor said I had
just come in time to save losing my life from
blood poisoning, so by that you will know
that I was in a dreadful state.”
“ Were you able to attend to your busi-
ness ? ” ’ '
“Work of any description was beyond
my powers for nearly four months, for my
constitution was fairly broken up and! was
almost helpless. ■ This being the first illness
I ever had, you may depend that I felt my
misfortune very keenly, and the thought
that I would never be able to use my arm
again nearly drove me frantic. I sat and
brooded over my troubles till my mind
lapsed into a chronic state of despondency,
and although the doctor said I had come
‘ just in time,’ I did not feel so sure about
it, as I felt so generally weak and wretched
that I doubted if I could survive the terrible
calamity which had occurred. For five
days and nights I did not eat a morsel of
food, although I tried hard enough to take
a little nourishment, but I could not swal-
low what I put to my mouth, and this, to-
gether with the agonising pains I was
suffering, made a thorough wreck of me.
My cruel tortures would not allow me to
sleep, neither did T know which way 1 to lie
for ease, so that when daylight came I was
glad to get up in spite of having scarcely
sufficient strength to walk about, for if I
did \saks an attempt my- head turned so
giddy that I suus had to sit down again.
My wife was kept going t?sght and day bath-
ing and bandaging my arm, hut even the
great care I received and the doctor’s medi-
cines combined could not succeed in elimi-
nating the poison which prevented the
wound from healing. I was completely at
the mercy of my‘original complaint, which in
time made my recovery more improbable,
because of the ruin it wrought on my in-
ternal organs, and as ailment after ailment
was? piled up I saw that my prospects were
more gloomy than ever. My nerves were
always on the quiver, which showed that
the strain on my system was too great for
them, and I felt particularly nervous on
hearing any noises, which also grated on my
ears and made me extremely irritable. I
could see that it was no good taking any
more doctor’s medicines, as I was getting no
benefit from them and they made me feel
sick; besides, they did not help me in re-
gard to my appetite, so I decided on pur-
chasing some Clements Tonic.”
“ How did that idea originate ? ”
“ Well, I had taken Clements Tonic to
give me an appetite once before when I was
feeling a bit jaded, and it made me eat like
a horse ; and now that I was so feeble, and
run down as low as I could be, I thought
Clements ' Tonic might do the same again.
And upon my word, it did ; but for a while
I was sorry that Clements Tonic made rtie
eat so heartily, as my digestive organs were
in such a shockingly bad state that all food
disagreed with me. My stomach always
got filled with wind, and my chest pains
were so weighty that I could scarcely get
my breath, while the awful pains 1 had
hitherto been maddened with were added
to by- something going wrong between my
shoulders. The taste in my mouth and the
furred condition of my tongue testified to
the disordered state of my liver, but the
aches in my limbs and across my loins told
the direful tale that my vitality was per-
fectly exhausted. When I come to look
back upon those weeks of abject torture and
misery, when I could not even read for the
swoll n feeling in my eyes, with no anima-
tion in my body, and with no hope left, I
consider it a merciful of
Providence which influenced me to perse-
vere with Clements Tonic, by the aid of
which came a delightful experience that
broke the morbid monotony of all those
months. When the first bottle of Clements
Tonic was finished I was sleeping more
soundly than I had done for some time past,
the reason being that there was a decrease
in the pains which had kept me awake so
consistently, and how thankful I was for
the rest, too ! To find that I could obtain
relief from Clements Tonic gave me a fresh
interest in life, although I did not antici-
pate throwing off my afflictions altogether,
as they had held out so obstinately against
other medicines ; but I was grateful for all
mercies, and when the discomforts following
my meals were lessened you can imagine
my great surprise. Little by little my
strength returned, and the vitalizing pro-
perties of Clements Tonic came in evidence
by the gradual healing of the wound in my
arm, and now there is nothing but a scar to
show where it was opened. I had lost a
considerable amount of flesh during my
illness, but in a few weeks it was all re-
stored, so it is needless to say that Clements
Tonic put my digestive organs in thorough
order, besides iufusing so much power to my
nervous system that you could not have got
a tremble out of my nerves no matter how
vou might have tried. Before long I had
shaken off every pain that had tormented me,
and with the full complement of strength
which Clements Tonic restored to my sys
tern, I went back to work none the worse
for-the terrible trials I had undergone. .
“ Are you sure you were quite cured ?
“I ha\e not had the least thing wrong
with me since, and I feel as hearty as though
my constitution were made of iron, so
Clements Tonic did its work well. 1 was
a despairing wreck of humanity when it
was called into requisition, and for saving
me as it did I owe my life-long thanks to
Clements Tonic—an admission which you
can publish in any way that may . do. good.

A lady who had had Bronchitis was left
with a severe cough which nothing seemed
to relieve. She has written a letter to tell
about the treatment which finally restored
her to health
“ 46, Elmhurst Boad, Forest Gate,
“ London, England,
. “ Sept. 7th, 1900.
Dear Sirs, —Having derived great benefit
from Scott’s Emulsion, I think you may be
interested to hear the results of my ex-
perience with your preparation. When I
commenced the use of Scott’s Emulsion I
had been suffering from bronchitis, which
had left me with a severe cough, and
nothing I tried did me any permanent good.
After X had taken Scott’s Emulsion about a
fortnight my cough had very materially
MRS. E. THOMPSON.
(From a photograph.)
improved and in a short space of time
ceased to trouble me any more. Not
only was my chest greatly strengthened
by the use of Scott’s Emulsion, but my
general health was very much improved.
I have recommended Scott’s Emulsion to
my friends who have been troubled with a
cough or chest ailments, and they also used
it with great benefit. —Faithfully yours,
“ (Signed) (Mrs.) E. Thompson.”
One of the most important truths which
is brought out in the foregoing letter is the
benefit which Scott’s Emulsion is to the
general health. This well-known standard
remedy is a specific for all throat and lung
affections, but its greatest advantage is in its
power to enrich the blood without digestive
effort, and to furnish the entire system
with the nourishing properties of cod-liver
oil. The improvement in the general health
after taking Scott’s Emulsion can hardly
be over estimated and it is of great import-
ance, because the general health determines
to a great extent diseases and chronic
ailments. Scott’s Emulsion aids digestion,
and stimulates the appetite for food. It is
the best form of cod-liver oil that is offered
to the public, being the most palatable, and
most readily absorbed into the blood.
It is well worth being careful to pur-
chase only the genuine Scott’s Emulsion,
which may be distinguished by our trade
mark of a man holding a big fish over his
shoulder.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent-street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Crucified Venus.
The goddess hangs upon the cross
Where she has suffered since the hour
When, breathless to behold their loss,
The gods grew jealous of her power.
Their temples held no docile throng,
Their altars claimed no sacrifice,
When Venus raised her siren song,
When Venus swung her passioned eyes.
They sought to still her bosom's swell
And dull the fervor of her eye;
That man might stay beneath their spell,
They met, and held that she should die.
With nails they pierced her dainty hands,
They raised her on the cross above
A barren waste of stones and sands,
In hope that thus they’d murdered Love.
The home of newt and toad and snake,
Of slime-clad creatures loosed from Hell—
Unmindful that her eyes would make
A Paradise where’er they fell.
Before her glance the poppies blew,
The sunlight clove the leaden air,
The vines entwined the cross and grew
A chaplet round her amber hair.
She will not die; immortal, calm,
She wields her sway, supreme, apart;
No nails can hold her rosy palm,
No blade can still her torrid heart.
No pain can rob her magic store
Of love and beauty, glowing free
For man to stand and gasp before
Her pink, pulsating nudity.
New faiths, begot of greed and hate,
To end her reign in terror try;
Calm-eyed, contemptuous, elate,
She sees the gods grow sick and die.
The mushroom deities, that load
The world with lies in accents smooth,
March on and fade along the road
That leads from Buddha down to Booth.
She knows her power, she sees their plight,
Their feeble gibes, their rotten rods;
She smiles upon their feeble spite—
The one immortal of the gods.
Weak, desperate, undone, afraid,
To-day the bitt’rest blows they deal,
With pride the meek alone parade,
With Hate that only weak men feel.
A swarm of puling priests they breed
To strangle beauty, love and mirth,
To torture those who boast her creed,
To raze her temples to the earth.
The temples pass, the groves remain,
The groves remain for youth and maid;
Even the threats of Hellish pain
Serve not to make their hearts afraid.
With frothing lips and quaking knee
The self-anointed crew entreat —
And, in the dark, when none can see ,
They crawl to worship at her feet.
She will not die; immortal, calm
She wields her sway, supreme, apart,
No nails can hold her rosy palm,
No blade can still her torrid heart.
And so, with jest and ribald shout,
Baulked, impotent their will to do,
Each dawn the dull sects drag her out
And nail her to the cross anew.
W.D.
Lais.
Fierce, pale and languorous; warm and passionate,
Filled with unquenchable ardour, perilous
To weak or strong who near me, full of pain
To those who love—l only am delight
To men who seek me lightly, gold in hand.
What do I care for flower of faithfulness!
My one desire to satiate my wild,
Insatiable and never-cooling fire
With fierce caresses and with mad excess.
And all men love me (Love, how joyfully !)
My supple and soft curving perfectness,
My firm smooth breast on which are boldly set
The twain snow-clad volcanoes, crowned with fire,
Dawn-coloured, full of danger and delight.
My warm, encircling arms bring loss of thought—
Only strong striving sense remains, wherein
The struggle for supremacy, ’twixt pain
And pleasure, strange and swift, holds aching
sway.
The high delirium, succeeded soon
By slow, long calm of smooth, sweet, dusky hours,
Through which I murmur with a voice that
dreams
By dark, cool vales with fern on either side—
A sleepy stream of sound—with here and there
A flash of dainty wit or sudden pause
Of utter silence, while the airs float by
My scented temple, and relieve the heat
With some suggestion of the breezy bay.
Yet comes a time when each man hateth me,
Loving those strange, unknown, pure women, far
From me in thought, whom never I may meet
With eyes that understand the other’s heart—
Since each must lack what fills the other’s life.
I sometimes sigh that I may never know
Ihe peace and gentle cares that use their hours,
Consideration sweet that husbands give,
And clinging little hands that claim their love.
Yet who would change the turmoil of my life,
Its passion, flaming colors, strange, fierce flowers
That grow in graphic nights, for long, pale days
And lily-blooms and love —that surely dies ?
iNot I not Lais ! I have pride and power,
And wealth and beauty, and all men worship me.
S.A.

WILL ANDRADE,
BOOK and MAGIC ARCADE .
201 Bourke Street, Melbourne
A Maoriland publican spoke in a Court
recently and moved the Bench almost to tears by
recounting the difficulties he had to contend with.
First of all, he complained that every policeman
on the “bate” was practically a pensioner, and
immediately the supply of free drinks, smokes,
and comfortable suppers showed signs of falling
off raids became painfully frequent. Some of the
police also borrowed small sums of money. If he
got up at 2 a.m., in response to repeated knock-
ings, and admitted a “traveller,” he was sure to
find that the traveller was an amateur detective,
sooled on by theW.C.T.U. or some other pestifer-
ous organisation. If he lay in bed and declined
to open the doer and admit the amateur detective,
an information was laid under that clause of the
Licensing Act which provides that all travellers
must be .accommodated. He had to know all the
prohibition-order people in the whole district, and
occasionally he was fined for refusing an unpro-
hibited person who resembled somebody that was
prohibited, and who, for all he knew to the con-
trary even now, might be the real prohibited
person he said he wasn’t. Then informations were
being continually laid against him by bona-fide
travellers who demanded liquor on Sundays, and
every time he took the bona-fide traveller’s word
that he was bona-fide he fell in, and had his
license endorsed, and was fined for Sunday
trading. He complained bitterly that people who
had filled up with bad grog in the neighboring
prohibited district crossed the imaginary line, and
lay down in his bar parlor, and got drunk on his
premises without having one solitary drink there.
How a policeman wanted his license cancelled
because he had served a woman of “doubtful
character,” which is the newest crime in Maori-
land ; and he declared that unless the police
branded women of doubtful character he had not
sufficient acquaintance with women of doubtful
character to be able to tell them at sight. He
finished up by remarking that he wouldn’t take
up the court’s time any longer. The verdict was
a matter of indifference to him. A woman whom
he refused to serve on account of her apparent
doubtfulness had just got a verdict of £250 against
him for slknder, and he was on his way to the
Bankruptcy Court.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.

“ Tahune Linah ” concerning a naval
relic (B. 28/6/’O2)
The Tas. gunpowder-hulk, La Mutine, alias Aladdin,
was sold by the Defence Dept, of the Commonwealth —
not by Tas. Govt. Some little time ago the old ship took
it into her head to leak, and the united effort of Tas-
mania’s standing army was unequal to the task of freeing
her. This was the main reason for the sale. The appear-
ance of the soldiers at the pumps would have broken the
heart of an old shellback. So far as the history of the
vessel goes, Jeffrey, in his very readable book, “A Cen-
tury of our Sea Story,” throws some light on the
error in imagining the Aladdin was the original La
Mutine captured at Santa Cruz in 1797. This vessel was
sold out of the service in 1801. In 1846 Capt. MacArthur,
of Hobart, went to London and bought from
Bennett, a London shipowner, the barque Aladdin,
being assured by the vendor that this was the original
Mutine which carried despatches from Nelson at the
Battle of the Nile to Naples. According to Jeffrey this
was Mutine No. 3. No. 2 was built at Bideford in 1806.
No. 3—the vessel under discussion—still quoting Jeffrey
—was built at Plymouth dockyards in February, ’42.
Bennett bought her after she had been condemned, and
registered her as the Aladdin. The name has been perpetu-
ated, and Mutine No. 7 has been added to the Navy list
recently. Further proof of Aladdin’s English origin is
furnished by the fact that her bolts are stamped with a
broad arrow. The Australian shipping register shows
she was built at Plymouth in 1825, so there is some dis-
crepancy between this date and Jeffrey’s. Mutine
No. 2 took part in the bombardment of Algiers
in 1816. A Mr. Brown of Hobart now dead—
dipped into old records, and years ago gave it out
that the Aladdin was the Mutine of Algiers—nine years
before she was built, according to her register. It seems
that the practice of re-naming vessels is responsible for
the mistakes made, and sentiment filled many gaps in her
story. Re cannon-ball. The writer was offered what
was claimed to be the identical article for 55., but didn’t
take any. At present time, have several cannon-balls
taken from a vessel of no recorded history. Cannon-balls
are “ off,” so far as historical merit goes. However, the
Aladdin was an “ old-timer,” and her true history as a
warship and whaler would, no doubt, make good reading.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,

scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now

No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

to The ' Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.

paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

Social, or other A Hides, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches

clippings. All communications will be regarded •as

are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, JULY 26, 1902.
A Great Surprise from Tasmania.
Among all the different proposals for the
reform, or alleged reform, of the State Con-
stitutions, the brightest, so far, is that of
Tasmanian Premier Lewis. Probably Lewis
didn’t intend to be bright, but, by accident
or otherwise, he got there. Tasmania is a
very small State with a very small State
Parliament—3B members in the Assembly
and 19 in the Council. Just now there is a
mania for reduction, and Tasmania is jeered
at for having one member to every 3000
people or thereabouts. The reform agitation
caught up Premier Lewis and swept him off
his legs, and he began to devise schemes for
further reduction. He thought out the
possibility of an Assembly of 20 members
and a Council of 10, in which latter body, as
the occasional absence of nine members must
always be reckoned upon, one would pre-
sumably be a quorum. And an Upper
House, where the whole legislative body
could be shouted for at a cost of two-and-six
looked so ridiculous, and so perilously like
an approach to the Turkish system, with its
hereditary Legislature of one man, that Lewis
has decided to propose that the two scraps
shall sit together. He can’t quite get rid
of his deep-rooted worship for the dismal old
House of Property, so he doesn’t go so far as
to propose that the combination shall be one
House, in the ordinary sense of the term,
with one franchise. He suggests that 20
members shall be elected on the Assembly
franchise and 10 on the Council franchise,
and these 30 shall meet in one Chamber and
try to look as separate as they can. Never-
theless, Lewis, the last man from whom such
ii thing could be expected, has the honor to
be the first Australian Premier of the pre-
sent day to propose doing away with the bi-
cameral fetish. If he carries his point, his
curious double-barrelled House of Parliament
will almost certainly settle down, some day,
into the single-barrelled condition, and then
a great deal will have been done towards the
simplification of the legislative machine.
* * * •
So far an exceeding conservatism, an un-
reasoning adhesion to old forms—in many
cases imperfectly understood, and a regret-
table inability to leave the old furrow have
characterised all attempts at State reform.
The Australias were, like England itself,
governed at one time by a single House —
the only thing that was the matter with it
was that it was the wrong kind of House.
The United States began with one House,
and the other was only added because the
intense jealousies of the small States made
them insist on having a House where all
States were equal, while the larger States
insisted on having also a House in which
their greater populations would be adequately
represented. The empire’s bi-cameral sys-
tems grew out of the idea that a certain titled
class had an inherent right to legislate—-
hence the necessity of a House to put it in ;
or out of the idea that the Crown’s nominees
should sit by themselves, so that they could
not be voted down by the common, pestilent,
objectionable delegates of the mob, or have
to associate with farmers and similar cattle ;
or out of the superstition that a small wealthy
class should have special elected represen-
tatives apart from the vulgar herd. The
habit lingers, even, as in N.S.W., when the
wealthy class has ceased to be specially re-
presented, and there are no royal nominees
and no hereditary nobility. When all the
reasons for an Upper House are gone, and
its power is gone, and no one respects it any
longer, it stays on like the fifth wheel of a
coach or any other lingering relic.
» * • *
In Canada, six out of the eight divisions
have one State House of Parliament only.
In the United States the two-House system
still hangs on, but in an increasing number
of the States both Houses are gradually
becoming little more than an excrescence,
while the power drifts into the hands of
popularly-elected Conventions—literally one-
House Parliaments elected at irregular
intervals. It is the popularly-elected Con-
vention which drafts a new Constitution,
and the two-House State Legislature has no
voice whatever regarding its acceptance. In
most of the States the new Constitution
may be legally put in force on the authority
of the Convention only, but, as a rule, it
is also put to the popular vote. And a State
Constitution, so-called, is really a huge code
of laws. Anything outside of it the State
Legislature can touch or alter, but in
many cases there is little of any great
importance outside it. In many cases
even the blessed privilege of running into
debt is prohibited to the Legislature. As
regards 39 States out of 45, the miserable,
bi-cameral Legislature is only allowed to
meet once in two years, and in 29 of the
States it is only allowed to sit for a certain
time (from 40 to 90 days) when it does meet.
In most of the States it requires an absolute
majority of both Houses before any measure
can be passed. In a number of them, the
Constitution comes up for revision by a
popularly-elected Convention at stated in-
tervals. The Referendum is applied to a
multitude of subjects, and has been a popular
institution for over 60 years, and the Initia-
tive exists in a few places. The Upper
Houses of the U.S. Legislatures represent
no special class ; they are merely survivals.
All these things are bad for the bi-cameral
tradition, as it is supposed to exist among
English-speaking peoples.
* * • *
The great bulk of British colonial Legisla-
tures consist of one body only—the trouble
is that, in most cases, it is a nominee body,
or else partly nominee and partly elected
on a narrow suffrage. When popular
suffrage arrives, however, the old nominee
body is retained, and the result is that the
live snake drags his dead, cast-off skin behind
him, where it is neither use nor ornament.
The venerable and decayed excrescence will
be abandoned some day, but the process is
very slow. But even Britain, despite its
love of tradition, has blessed intervals of
reason. When it created the London County
Council, to rule a population 20 times that
of Tasmania, no one dreamt of putting a top-
heavy upper deck on it, and it does its work
quite as well without. And despite the
tired old theory about the value of a Second
Chamber in “ checking hasty legislation,” no
one has propounded the theory that the
British possessions which are run by one
House are any more hasty than those with
two. Upper Houses, as a matter of fact, are
divided into two classes; those that are
powerful and are hated as obstacles to every
kind of progress, and those that are power-
less, like the Legislative Councils of N.S.W.
and Maoriland, and are only despised. They
may be also divided into necessary evils,
which are few, and unnecessary evils, which
are many. The Federal Senate is probably
an evil, but it was necessary as a concession
to the weaker States. Some day when
Australia gets a more complete form of union,
when its debts, railways, and educational
systems are taken over by the Common-
wealth Government, when its population is
more evenly distributed, and its unwieldy
States are sub-divided, the country may be
able to do without its necessary evil.
* * * *
If Premier Lewis is able to carry out his
scheme he will, at least, have achieved some-
thing original. A purely-elective House,
with two suffrages, is quite out of the
common order of things. But there is
always the strong probability, almost the
certainty, that the suffrages will ultimately
boil down to one. Meanwhile, the one
House will do good service in the way of
destroying a pernicious old legend by showing
that its lonesomeness doesn’t lead to the
much-feared “ hasty legislation.” JSTo better
place than Tasmania could be found for re-
introducing the uni-cameral system and
breaking up the legend, for the idea of any
Tasmanian Legislature being hasty under
any circumstances is as absurd as to
suspect a sheep of conspiracy, or a
caterpillar of ferocity, sedition, and habitual
drunkenness. Anyhow, Premier Lewis is
hardly a free agent. The public calls, or is
alleged to call, for reduction of members. An
Upper House of 10 members, when one of
them was in the chair, and two were ill with
influenza, and two more had paired, would be
a gaudy sight while the other five went goutily
on to a division and two tellers were appointed
each side. And an Assembly of 20 members
would only muster about nine and the
Speaker on quiet nights, and when the
House went into committee, and the Speaker
retired with dignity, and the Chairman of
Committees took the chair, and the unruly
member had been suspended for the rest of
the sitting, there would be only the four
Ministers and their one follower, and the
Leader of the Opposition and his one ad-
herent left. The pomp of Responsible
Government can’t be kept up under such
conditions, any more than one man can form
himself into a hollow square and mobilise
himself as a reserve at the same time. Either
the forms of Responsible Government or the
bi-cameral system must needs go overboard.
The Unemployed.
An unemployed conference has been sitting at
Sydney, about the saddest thing in conferences
any city could produce. In N.S. Wales an incre-
dible proportion of the population has been sup-
ported for the last few years by the expenditure
of loan money ; probably the proportion thus
employed and their families and dependents now
amounts to over one-tenth of the population.
And yet there are unemployed. There are State
labor bureaux. State relief works (paid for out of
loans), State shelters, homes, labor farms—in fact,
there is almost everything that can be imagined
for the poor in the way of useless or almost useless
work, the payment for which is left, as far as
possible, to posterity. The great art of digging a
hole and then filling it up again has been carried
almost to the last possible extreme on their
behalf. Almost everything in the shape of
an lOU that could be done for the un-
employed has been done, or is being done.
And still there are unemployed. The conference
of these unfortunates has just laid it down
as an axiom “that an Act should be passed
through the Legislature establishing the right of
all male residents to employment.” The Bul-
letin fails to see that the Act would do any good
unless accompanied by a scheme for supplying the
employment ; the mere legislative statement that
a man is entitled to work amounts to no more, by
itself, than the statement that he is entitled to be
Emperor of China. Yet as regards the right
itself, The Bulletin has no doubt whatever. It
is the business of every Government to so adjust
things that every person who wants to work shall
be able to get work. Otherwise, it is a failure.
Of course, no Government can be so mathemati-
cally accurate that nobody shall ever be unem-
ployed. But it should be able to adjust things so
that no capable person need be out of work long
enough to sink into actual want—that there shall
be no chronic unemployed.
» * * *
Obviously there are only two possibilities in the
case. To make more work the country must
either produce more raw material for export—that
being about the only thing it exports, or it must
produce more manufactured goods for its own use,
and consequently import less. It can’t have any
great increase of manufactures without importing
less; if the foreign supplies remain the same and
the local supplies greatly increase the only result
will be an accumulation of goods on hand that no
one will have money to buy. To try to get over
the difficulty by producing more raw material for
export has been tried for many years, and has
failed. N.S.W. has aided, bonused and fostered
its “ great producing industries ” with subsidised
railways, roads, low freights, cheap land, cheap
money and all manner of aids, and whereas the
exports of locally-grown commodities, apart from
gold—the export of everything else that could be
dragged off the surface, or the places
beneath the surface, of the land —brought in
£lB 12s. lOd. per inhabitant in 1883, it brought in
less than £l3 per inhabitant in 1900. The differ-
ence—practically £6 annually per inhabitant, or
£24 per annum (9s. 2d. per week) for every family
of four—accounts for a fearful amount of
want and misery. The struggle to solve
the unemployed problem by the increase of
production for export has been long and terribly
costly, and it has failed with a great and abject
failure. Drought, poor land, the immense distance
to the British market, and the low prices obtain-
able there, have beaten the producing industry
badly. The extra employment, if the experience
of the last 20 years counts for anything, can’t be
obtained under present conditions in that
direction, unless it is employment of a wretchedly
ill-paid character.
¥ * * *
The other resource is for N.S.W. to manufacture
for itself a few millions of the £27,000,000 worth
of goods it now imports. The work is available,
the market is available—it is right here at our
doors. The labor is available; the unemployed
are always with us, and their increase is the most
hopeless problem of the day. Already manufac-
tures are worth a great deal more in cash value to
N.S.W. than agriculture is, but even now there
are not nearly enough of them to absorb the sur-
plus population, and not one-third enough to
supply the local demand. There has been a
surplus population for many years—a population
supported out of loans; and local industries have
always been in an insufficient state. Manufactures
constitute theonlybranchof N.S.W. industry which
shows any serious possibility of expansion nowa-
days ; since 1892 they have increased in value as
much as the pastoral, agricultural, dairying,
mining, timber, and fishing industries put together.
They have had, all along, a certain amount of Pro-
tection ; with a little more the results would
probably have been better still. That is the only
direction in which there is any visible hope for
the unemployed. Belief works, or public works
that are of a semi-relief character, are merely
temporary aids that sink the country deeper in
debt. They leave a worse trouble behind in the
shape of more interest to pay. They have a
pauper atmosphere about them which is had for
the self-respect of the community. And they tend
to make the Australians a race of pick-and-shovel
men—like the Kaffir in South Africa, or the old-
time nigger in the Southern States—a class which
only knows the coarsest and least remunerative
variety of work—which is half-brother to the
horse, and nearly related to the crane and the
sand-scoop.
• * • •
Even the most virulent Freetrader, if he were
crowded into a corner and put on his solemn oath,
would probably admit these propositions :
(1) If (say) £10,000,000 worth of the goods that are now
imported were manufactured locally there would be more
employment.
(2) If £10,000,000 worth less goods were imported there
would be less money sent away in payment for imports.
(3) Seeing that the United States can export nearly
twice as much as it imports it doesn’t necessarily follow
that by reducing imports N.S.W. would also reduce ex-
ports.
The Ereetrade argument, however, is that if
imports were compulsorily reduced by a tariff
which, in some lines, was absolutely prohibitive
(no Australian State has tried a prohibitive tariff-
yet) the local products which took the place of
these imports would cost more, therefore the pro-
ceeding isn’t worth while. And if wages'were
cut down so low as to make it quite certain that
the local workman, in the early stages of a new
industry, before he had become an expert, and be-
fore economy of production had been brought
to its highest pitch, could hold hi 3 own, even
against the fierce cutting of prices that the
importer always resorts to when he sees a market
slipping away from him—well, that isn’t worth
while either. Relief works, built out of loan
money, can always, under aFreetrade, or anything
approaching a Freetrade, system, beat manu-
factures badly. Manufactures have to pay their
way from the start; relief works are nearly all
subsidy. Even in the matter of railways, which
are sometimes relief works and sometimes not,
N.S.W. can show a number of lines on which the
subsidy more than equals the total earnings. If
the Freetrade creed refused to subsidise railways,
relief works, and agricultural and pastoral indus-
tries, as well as manufactures, it would at least be
consistent. As matters stand it demands that the
unsubsidised factory shall hold its own in the
labor market against other industries on which the
refreshing breeze of competition doesn’t blow, and
pay vastly heavier railway freights, and, if it is
in a municipality, which is usually the case, pay
its own local rates for roads and so on, and also
pay its share towards maintaining the roads of
the agriculturist who, in N.S.W., is almost
always outside of local government, and isn’t
locally taxed to maintain his own roads. New
South Wales to-day is probably, in the strictest sense
of the term, almost the most pauper-ridden white
man’s country on earth—reckoning the poor in its
asylums and gaols, the unemployed, and the vast
mass of people who are maintained out of loan
funds on works which are not expected to pay,
and which are, at least in considerable part, of a
relief character.
* * * *
There is no visible way by which N.S.W. can
manufacture £10,000,000 more goods per annum
within a very short time, and thereby find work
for another 60,000 hands, unless £10,000,000 of
imports are shut out by a more prohibitive tariff
than any Australia has yet possessed. The State
certainly can’t afford to buy £9,000,000 worth more
goods, and its present imports as well; therefore
it can’t make any great increase in local produc-
tion while the imports continue on the present
huge scale. And any attempt to make duties
high enough to seriously reduce imports is de-
nounced by the N.S. W. delegation in the Federal
Parliament on the ground that it will “reduce
imports ” and “ destroy revenue.” The Free-
trade Party claims that duties should be so
low as not to interfere with imports, and then
they will produce revenue. It also says that
it is the buyer—the N.S.W. citizen—who pays
the duty, be it high or low ; on which basis, when
a million pounds worth of goods dutiable at 6 per
cent, are imported, he pays £1,000,000 for the
goods and £50,000 for the duty. It might, per-
haps, be better for him to make the £1,000,000
worth of goods (which would give employ-
ment to about 5000 or 6000 people) and
have the £1,000,000 in wages, and pay the
£50,000 in direct taxation, but the Freetrade
party says it wouldn’t. While N.S.W. supports
that party it is difficult to see what practical con-
solation it can offer to the unemployed, who say,
quite rightly, that it is the duty of the Govern-
ment-to find them work. The Government has
made great and costly efforts to foster the agricul-
tural and pastoral industries, and Nature has so
far got ahead of it that they produce hardly any-
thing more in the gross, and vastly less per in-
habitant, than they did 20 years ago. The
strongest Government can’t struggle against the
fact that, whereas each acre under cultivation in
N.S.W. in 1882 produced £74s. sd. worth of crop,
it only turned out £2 Bs. Bd. worth in 1901.
Mining is under a cloud, and supports a diminish-
ing population. The State can’t make all the un-
employed policemen. It can’t put them on the
land unless it gives them the land and seed and
tools, also rations to support them till the crop
arrives ; and apart from the fact that a man who
knows nothing of agriculture would fail even under
these conditions, all this is a system of bonuses
and artificial coddling which is quite hostile to the
Freetrade tradition. It can’t borrow £10,000,000
every year to keep them going on public works-
even the Owe’Sullivan has his limitations. To
absorb the unemployed in manufactures it is
necessary to nearly double the present N.S.W.
output in one act, and that can’t be done
without shutting out an enormous quantity of
imported produce, and N.S.W. has sent to
the Federal Parliament a body of men whose
votes steadily block every attempt to shut out im-
ports. If the unemployed would pledge them-
selves never, under any circumstances, to ask for
more than 18s. a week, no doubt sufficient manu-
factures to absorb them might start with very
little Protection —cheap labor, if it is only cheap
enough, and reasonably skilful at the same time,
doesn’t require Protection. But, as things are, a
dead wall rises up in front everywhere.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Victorian Reform Bill.
Premier Irvine, of Victoria, has issued his con-
stitutional Reform Bill. It isn’t an ideal measure,
but if Irvine stands by it—if he insists on the
whole Bill and only allows alteration where it
further liberalises the measure, he will be the
greatest Parliamentary reformer that Victoria has
yet possessed. Irvine is a cold, emotionless man,
as free from heat or rash enthusiasm as a tomb-
stone on a cold night, and he probably cares about
as little for reform as anybody else in the Legis-
lature. But circumstances have deposited the
reform movement, like a deserted infant, on his


bleak and unpromising doorstep, and in his cold,
bleak way Irvine looks, so far, like doing his duty
by the infant. The leading features of his scheme
are these:
Seven Ministers.
Assembly of 56 members.
A Minister may sit temporarily in the House to which
he does not belong' for the purpose of explaining or
speaking upon a measure.
Council of 28 members.
Every male or female ratepayer to have a vote at
Council elections.
Every male ratepayer to be eligible to sit in the Council.
If either House refuses to pass a Bill which has been
twice passed by the other, both Houses may be dissolved
simultaneously.
If the newly-elected Houses fail to agree there may be a
Joint Sitting of the two Houses, and its vote shall decide.
There is no provision for payment of M’s.L.C., so
the chances of the Legislative Council becoming a
chamber of a red revolutionary character are
remote. _ But still the new Council, if Irvine
carries his measure, will be an enormous advance
on the old one. Hitherto, by reason of the
property qualification, only about 1000 or 2000
persons have been eligible to sit in the Victorian
Upper House —all rich landlords, and almost all
Tories of the most bigoted variety. Under the
new order of things 130,000 men will be eligible,
and if Victoria can’t find 28 reasonably liberal,
intelligent, progressive men among that mul-
titude, even without payment, it must be, to
a great extent, Victoria’s own fault. The
foul insult by which nearly 99 per cent, of the
people who make the Council are declared to be
political lepers and outcasts, unfit to sit in the
House of their own creation—by which the thing
that is made disqualifies the maker-—is to be
removed. That is to say, it is to be removed if
the Irvine Government stands firm. The As-
sembly will probably pass the measure as it is.
The Council will probably pass the clauses which
cut down the size of Parliament—the mere money-
saving clauses —and will q uite as probably reject
the proposed amendment of its own constitution.
And, if Irvine accepts half the Bill, the chance of
getting the other half —the Council-reform half,
which is incalculably more important than any
mere reduction of members—may not come again
for 20 years. But if the tombstone man insists on
the whole Bill or none at all—if he tells the Council
that it must accept the reform idea or take the respon-
sibility of causing the loss of the reduction clauses
also, and expose itself to the fury of Kyabram
and the cold blast of B. Goddard —then he, most
likely, can carry his point. It is mostly a ques-
tion of Irvine’s sincerity. The Reform Bill itself
doesn’t prove anything absolutely definite in that
direction. Any man can introduce a valuable bill
and then drop the most valuable part of it. Sup-
posing Irvine sees the matter through, he will be
Victoria’s greatest reformer on very easy terms.
He came into office when every political circum-
stance was shunting him violently along the road
to reform. He can bring about a vast political re-
generation with less difficulty than any of his pre-
decessors. The demand for economy, and the pre-
cedent of the newly-established Federal Senate, as
showing what kind of Upper House Australia
really demands, are in his favor. The great and
indignant Age is with him. The Kyabram pro-
gramme is with him—at least in part —and B.
Goddard is howling like a demon in the back-
ground. The Hour has come—and the Icicle.

The Senate.
When the Federal Senate has finished making
"suggestions” for the reduction or abolition of
customs duties, and the House of Representatives
begins to consider these suggestions, a great many
interesting things may happen, though, on the
other hand, it is much more likely that nothing in
particular will happen. As a rule nothing in par-
ticular happens. The Senate has no power to
alter money Bills ; it can only suggest that the
House of Reps, should alter them. (“Suggestion”
is very good when both parties are nearly of the
same way of thinking, but if one party is a Holy
Roman teetotaller and the other is an Orangeman
who keeps a public-house, it isn’t likely that any
suggestions one party can make to the other,
either about drink or religion, will lead to agree-
ment.) If the House of Reps, refuses to agree
with the Senate’s suggestions the Senate can either
abandon the suggestions or throw out the Tariff Bill
bodily. In the present case the Senate’s ideas and
the ideas of theßepresentatives are so widely diver-
gent that it becomes a question as to which House
will rule—the Lower House which represents the
people on a population basis, or the Upper House
in which one man’s vote in Tasmania counts for
eight times as much as one man’s vote in N.S.W.
If the House of Reps, doesn’t insist on its tariff,
then the Senate has asserted its dominant position
in matters of finance, and the Treasurer should
sit in that House instead of in the Representa-
tives, and the Financial Statement should be
delivered there, and the fiction that the Lower
House—the People’s House as distinguished from
the House of States —has chief control of money
matters should be abandoned. The Senate’s
amendments are calculated to greatly reduce
the producing power of a tariff which is already
too small for the States’ requirements, and if the
Senate is allowed to cut down revenue, and yet
leave to the other House the work of making the
diminished revenue cover requirements, the
position of the Representatives will be ridiculous
and wholly undesirable. Of course, there is the
curious and difficult situation that if the
House of Representatives insists on its tariff, the
Senate may reject the whole measure, leaving the
Commonwealth with no tariff at all, and before the
deadlock clauses of the Constitution can be put in
force—these clauses move very slowly—the two
years within which the Constitution says that a
tariff must be passed will have expired. And
nobody knows exactly what will follow. But
if the House of Representatives surrenders its
rights because the Senate can bring about chaos in
the attempt to usurp an authority to which it has
no just claim, the House of Representatives
will make a mistake which it may take a
century to repair. Its business is to assert its
claim, as the House which most fairly represents
the people, to predominate in matters of finance.
If the Senate likes, in defence of an illegal demand
for financial predominance, to bring about one of
the biggest deadlocks in history, and thereby
incur a measure of public obloquy which may lead
to its own destruction, the House of Representa-
tives has no call to sacrifice itself in order that it
may save the Senate from publicly disgracing itself.
If it becomes a question whether the Senate shall
make itself an object of execration through
attempting to do wrong, or whether the other
House shall save it from execration by allowing it
to do wrong without protest, there should be no
room for doubt or hesitation in the matter. There
can be no deadlock if the Lower House allows the
Upper one to have absolute control, but peace
would be too expensive at the price.

A Harassed Community and a Tram Co.
Melbourne’s policy of leasing its tramways to a
company for a long term of years is bearing fruit.
The fruit consists mostly of the apple of discord
hung on the tree of recrimination. When the
tramways were constructed, the cable system was
about the most up-to-date in the world ; now it is
out of date except in precipitous localities, where
the cable car’s capacity for taking hills that neither
the steam nor the electric vehicle can look at
still keeps it to the front. The lines then planned
out were supposed to be all that Melbourne could
possibly want for a generation, andtheco. undertook
to work these lines for a certain term under certain
conditions ; now Melbourne finds that it has out-
grown the limited system, and a multitude of new
lines are wanted. When the agreement was first
made, the system of charging everybody 3d., and
carrying him five miles or 100 yards, as the case
might be, seemed reasonable enough ; the idea of
cutting up the immensely long threepenny sections
into short penny ones only grew into popularity
in later years. So now things have settled down
into a sort of dialogue between Melbourne and
its Tramway Co., the method of the dialogue
being after this fashion :
The Harassed Commmunity : “As owners of the tram-
ways we would like to convert them into an electric
system at our own expense. It won’t interfere with your
profits in any way.”
The Teamway 00. : “It wouldn’t cost us anything l , of
course, but it would put us to some trouble. And our
lease expires in 14 years. The present system will last
our time. Of course, if you gave us an extension of our
lease it would he different.”
Haeassed Community : “We want to extend the
system.”
Teamway Co.: “We won’t work any extensions unless
you give us an extension of our lease. You can’t work
them separately with much profit; extensions that would
pay handsomely when worked as parts of the city system
would be a poor investment, or even a dead loss, when
worked as 10 or 12 disconnected suburban scrap-lines.
This immovable attitude on our part is always liable to
lead to an extension of our lease.”
Haeassed Community: “If we extend your lease the
same trouble may arise again before the second lease is
expired.”
Tramway Co.: “ Then we would want you to keep on
extending our lease. You can’t extend our lease too
often.”
Haeassed Community: “We want the threepenny
sections subdivided into penny sections. Threepenny sec-
tions are out of date and inconvenient—so much so that
the old dead-and-gone omnibus has been resurrected on
the penny principle and is running against the tramways.
It is a disgrace to Melbourne that what was once the most
up-to-date tramway system on earth' should become so
antiquated that this antediluvian vehicle can come
back to compete with it.”
Tramway Co. : You can make any new arrangements
you like when our lease expires 14 years hence. You
can’t expect us to worry much about the up-to-dateness
of things when we have only 14 years to run. It would
be quite different if we had an extension of our lease.”
Harassed Community : “ And if we give you an exten-
sion on condition that you accept these improvements,
what guarantee will we have that the service won’t
become antiquated again? New improvements are
always being devised.”
Tramway Co. : “ Every time there is a new improve-
ment that you want us to take up, just come along and
see us about an extension of the lease.”
Harassed City : “ Look here—what will you take to
get out?”
Tramway Co. : “ About twice as much as the thing is
worth.”
That imaginary dialogue pretty nearly represents
the situation. The negotiation was never put
into actual words, but the general understanding
is to the above effect. So the State is seriously
considering whether it will build thirteen
detached suburban electric tramways or street
railways, which will have to be run as separate
enterprises at probably 50 per cent, more cost
than if they were connected with the city trams
and worked as one business. And Minister of
Railways Thomas Bent is “seeing gentlemen”
about “ concessions. ’ ’

The New Ireland.
The cables from S’Africa —in spite of the existing
censorship—are now obliged to announce the
existence of grave discontent among the Boers in
the Transvaal and the Orange State. In Vryheid
district the. inhabitants objeet to being transferred
to Natal. Around Bloemfontein the stalwarts of
the Boer cause are striving to keep alive Repub-
lican ideas by the wearing of the old Boer
flag and by making life a well-deserved misery
to the villainous and traitorous “Burgher
Scouts” who changed sides during the late
war. One commandant at least has been
urging his men to keep in mind their national
existence as Transvaalers. So much comes
through, in spite of the censorship ; and there is
no news at all of any Boers singing “ Rule
Britannia.” It is to the last degree unfortunate
that this should be so. Having admitted the
force of necessity, and surrendered their national
flags, the Boers would best study their own
interests now by submitting to the inevitable, and
trusting to the early grant of Home Rule as pro-
viding them with an independence in all but
name. The Boers are not, however, (as the Jingoes
have just lately been misrepresenting them), a
highly-educated, philosophical, unprejudiced, and
exceedingly generous people. A good sturdy
race, truly, but not the combination of magna-
nimity, wisdom, and foresight which the
Jingo of June, 1902, would have us believe.
It is inevitable, therefore, that there should be
bitterness, and hatred, and conspiracy on their
part against the British, that they should remem-
ber the horrors of the concentration camps and
the glories of many sturdily-fought battles, and
should still cherish, with that tenacious firmness
which is the chief characteristic of Dutch races,
hatred of the victor and hopes of a separate
national existence. They would be more than
human in charity and in wisdom if it were
otherwise. England has taken on her shoul-
ders a new Ireland, and must go through, in
Africa, with all the terrible work of 1798 on the
Irish hills—with what result it is impossible to
say. By a miracle of patience and firmness the
result may be honestly declared happy—a couple
of centuries hence.
Micawber Australis.
The following sentences are taken from the lead-
ing article in the sober-minded and perfectly
serious Brisbane Courier (11/7/’O2). Speaking of
the two successive half-million deficits it says:
We only mention this to show the difficulties which
have to be faced. But the Government has a duty to
perform. Ends must be brought together. Shale it be
DONE BY STERNLY ECONOMISING OR BY BORROWING?
The last sentence isn’t intended as shrieking
comedy—the Courier doesn’t frivol in its leader
columns. In England, or in any sanely financed
community, such a remark would probably appeal
to the public sense of humor ; and grave and
eminent financial authorities would laugh
hilariously as they saw the editor go by, because
every time they saw him they would be reminded
of the joke of the century. The editor who calls
borrowing a making of ends to meet —who seriously
discusses whether the floating liability left at
the close of the year should, or should
not, be manfully paid off by turning it into a per-
manent liability—who propounds it as quite a
possible alternative that the country should get
out of debt by getting into another debt of the
same size—such a person is hardly possible in any
English-speaking country bar Australia. In Aus-
tralia he is more than possible, and more thari
probable; he is a permanent institution. The
habit of handing out the I 0 U, and remarking
that that liability, thank God, is at last settled,
has taken such deep root that an Australian
journalist can ask sternly, in big type, whether
his State is going to let things drift, or manfully
borrow enough to pay its debts, and he can do it
without raising a smile. About £9,000,000 of
Australia’s permanent or floating debts were
accumulated in this way. Australia is quiteaccus-
tomed to the situation.
