The Quiraing- ?
Warships were sent in search of the steamer
Quiraing, only when she was 28 days out from
Newcastle to Dunedin, coal-laden, with 22 souls
(but no fashionable passengers) aboard. The
hesitancy and delay in taking any expensive action
in regard to the missing vessel is in accordance
with all usual precedents when only the lives of
seamen are concerned. When the Quiraing was
“missing” some days* the steamer South Aus-
tralian was sent to Dunedin to fill the coal
charter which the Quiraing had undertaken
(i that couldn’t wait), and, incidentally, to keep a
look-out for the missing vessel. That that was
only an incidental part of her mission is shown by
the fact that the South Australian left Newcastle
on July 9 and reached Port Chalmers on July 15.
A coal cargo, it is plain, must not be seriously
delayed. It was not until July L 9 that the
decision was come to to send two warships in the
track of the Quiraing. It would be interesting to
know whether the real reason for this vague, half-
hearted effort is the fact that there are no passen-
gers aboard; or the conviction that the Qui-
raing has not been disabled but has foundered.
On this point a cable from Maoriland, published
in the S. M. Herald, says :
Mr.KiNG, who, until the present trip of the Quiraing,
had charge of her engine-room, stated in an interview to-
day that her engines were first-class, and he thought that
a breakdown in that quarter was most improbable,
though he admitted that her propeller might have gone
amiss. The Quiraing was a good sea boat, but he did not
like the feel of her when she was loaded down as she was
sometimes.
And a description of the Quiraing (Sydney
D.T.)reads:
The Quiraing was imported by the old A.S.N. 00. for
the Queensland trade, and after that company amal-
gamated with the Queensland Shipping 00. she was sold,
and employed in the island and other trades. She is an
iron screw steamer, with a net register of 653 tons, and
gross measurement of 1166 tons, having a length of
220 ft. 2in., beam of 31ft. 9in., and depth of 19ft. 9in.
She was built in 1882, and is at present owned by a
Brisbane syndicate, being this voyage under charter to
Mr. Sneddon, of this city.
The Quiraing, it is stated by the S.M. Herald
of June 23, carried 1100 tons of coal cargo out of
Newcastle.

Hopetoun’s Pedestal.
“ In the good old days, in the days of old,
It was crozier of wood and bishop of gold ;
But now we have changed that law so good
Into crozier of gold and bishop of wood.”
Such a change our ex-Governor-General proposed
to make for us. “I intended to set up a pedestal.”
A better knowledge and apprehension of affairs
would have realised that the Commonwealth is
the pedestal. That uplifts the Governor in
the eyes of the world, and he can no more add on
a foot to the height of it than a cubit to his own
stature. The pedestal contemplated would have
been an absurdity. Powder and lace and buttons
and frills, however artistically piled up, would
not have saved it from contempt. An intelligent
democracy has no use for those commodities. The
Governor himself is an anachronism, not perhaps
to be dispensed with for a while, and to be treated
with a proper respect. But a royalty-apeing.
Oriental monarch, no ! Australia has not herself
to blame for the wrong notion which has got
about. It came with Carrington to N.S.W.
And Carrington came in a queer time. When
his appointment was announced, that gentleman
in Australia who knows most about these things
observed, “ Thank Gop ! Government House
will at last be clear of bailiffs. ” Actually, so poor
had Colonial Governorship become. With him
the chimneys smoked, and the cellars hummed
again. Carrington made a bit of a precedent,
though he didn’t talk about a pedestal. “At
Home” they did not understand the precedent
very well. It appeared that the main desire
of Australians was for a peer, and a young,
bright, free-spending peer. Truth is, "before
Carrington, we had peers and sons of peers,
a half-a-dozen of them, and do not hold the
memory of one to-day in less than contempt.
Australians want a man for a Governor, and
Hopetoun made his little bit of popularity or
fame when he tried to play the man—when knock-
ing about in free intercourse with all sorts of Aus-
tralian people, trying, as it seemed, to get the
hang of Australian affairs, the bent of the Aus-
tralian mind, and not when trussed up for the
delectation of Toorak. We did not hanker after the
moleskins and felt hat again. The jackarooing was
done with. But matured intellect, with the
advantage of experience gained, was expected,
and instead of that there came the ex-Lord
Chamberlain with tlie pedestal. And the puzzle
which even the farewell letter does not explain is,
why was it expected that Australia should pay
for the pedestal ? Australian democracy will
recognise no perpetual obligation to keep up a
cheap imitation of royalty. Let lords keep their
pedestal; and we will keep our cash.

Mr. Chamberlain Takes a Back Seat.
It is an important development for Australian
nationalism that Mr. Chamberlain has had to
give place to Mr. Balfour in the English Cabinet
re-arrangement Lord Salisbury's exit from the
political stage has long been foreseen, and Mr.
Chamberlain used to assume, with or without justi-
fication, the position and privilege of the heir-ap-.
parent to the Premiership. That gave him great
authority. Now he is simply a Cabinet member
with no promotion even remotely in sight, and
connected in public rumor with hostility to
the Court (which to a Conservative politician
is a most damaging position). Bo it is
safe to say that Joseph Chamberlain will not
be so bumptious in the future as in the past.
A few months ago he advocated an Imperial
Federation drawing “ the colonies ” closer to their
mother-in-law, though in doing so he had to
directly flout Lord Salisbury. That is not
likely to be repeated, for, if attempted again, it
would probably lead to the retirement of Mr.
Chamberlain ; a politician who has had to sub-
mit to being passed over must tune his song to a
humble lay afterwards. All this will be of dis-
tinct benefit to Australia. The supremacy, of Mr.
Chamberlain in the English Cabinet would have
involved constant Imperialistic encroachments'
on our self-governing rights. These might—
possibly would—have been submitted to'; or
they might have ‘precipitated a separation
for which the time is perhaps hardly ripe.
In either case they would have led to
definite injury. Mr. Balfour, who now riles
in the Conservative camp, has a strong sympathy
with the ideas of the Liberal party on “the,
Colonies”—that they should, as far as possible, be.
left to themselves to develop in their own way.
Every student of international history will agree
that that is the best, both for Australia and for
England. It leaves us to work out our destiny in
our own way, and helps to make it possible that,
when the inevitable national separation does
come, it will be an entirely amicable one.
Thb dinner given by King Edward to 550,000 of -
the London poor is a whole volume of common- •
tary on what poverty means in England. When
one-tenth of the population of the largest and
richest capital on earth are so poor that they are .
glad to accept a gratis meal it looks bad for the
richest capital on earth. All the glitter and ;
pomp and affluence of the west end of London,
the magnificence of the “stately homes of
England,” the splendid hospitality and lavish
charity of its rich landlords, the gorgeousness of
its Hopetouns and Beauchamps, and much larger
magnates—all these are only possible through the
abject poverty of the huge majority of the'
population. Even in a rich country there isn’t
so much money that any man can even be as
rich as Hopetoun (who is still a comparatively
humble sample of the British plutocrat) without
many others living all their lives on the confines
of starvation to make up for it. Of every 1000
persons in the United Kingdom, 939 die practically
penniless—that is to say, the most they own is a
few poor sticks of furniture worth a pound or two,
and their well-worn clothes. An appalling pro- ‘
portion of the people of the large cities (about
one-fourth in some cases) live in one-room houses—
a whole family in a room. In Scotland, it is reck-
oned that 22 per cent of the whole population—
city and country —lives a whole family in a room.
The people who are supposed to live permanently '
on the confines of starvation —who practically
never see more than one meal ahead, and whose
one meal is often a long distance ahead—are
commonly estimated at 8,000,000. One square
meal for half-a-million of them at Coronation-time
is something, certainly, but it’s a long time
between Coronations.
♦
The present strike of coal-miners at Outtrim
(Vic.) affords the strongest argument possible for ■
compulsory arbitration. The Unionists objected .
to three men as “ undesirable ” mates and refused
them admission to the Coal Miners’ Union on the •
ground that they showed themselves traitors to
Unionism by taking work at the McEvoy mine, at
Eldorado, during a strike. The coal co., per
contra, says the three are sober, honest, competent
men who have never worked at “scab” rates, and
that the miners, in refusing them admission to
their Union and in throwing the mine idle
by declining to work alongside them, are dis-
tinctly infringing trade-union rights. Now, if the
company’s version is correct , it could take its case
to any Arbitration Court with the certainty of the
Unionists being ordered to resume work and
admit the three undesirables. And, in addition,
the coal co. would recover heavy damages from
the Union. So the Employers’ Federation, which '
persistently rails against compulsory arbitration
as an unnecessary interference and a piece of
socialistic legislation, must either consider it
mostly has a bad case or else it doesn’t believe in
law’norder, and prefers strikes, lock-outs, and
similar violent methods.
♦
Nobody recognises better than the Labor parties
do that any fool and any fraud can throw money
around by the handful, and that really the first
thing to see to is, that the man gets the money >
honestly. So they gibe at the “ philanthropist
who first racks or robs the poor and then scatters
a percentage of his pence among them, with the-
reporters looking on. But these same Labor
parties have not even yet fully applied the same
wise rule to States and Governments. To many
of the members of these parties (for there is a
steadily-growing company of exceptions) it is
enough that a Govt, or a Minister spends—the
O’Sullivan is a fine fellow, and the best Works
Minister N.S. Wales ever had, because lie
scatters the most coin ! Yet that is the part
that any fool or any fraud could do —the first
thing to see to here, just as it is in connection with
the private scatterer, is the source whence the
money came. And if that won’t stand investiga-
tion, then the bigger the scatter the greater the
folly or the fraud.
+
Tasmania’s “reform” movement is becoming the
boss joke among the humorous reform movements
of the Commonwealth. Tasmania has four
Ministers at £750 each, which is cheap enough.
It pays its members only £lOO a year. It pays
its civil servants a little over half Australian rates.
It is governed at less than half the cost per
inhabitant of Queensland, less than one-third that
of Westralia, and about 22 per cent, less -cost per
inhabitant than Victoria. It is by far and away
the most cheaply-governed State in Australasia ;•
it beats Great Britain hopelessly in economy, and
seems to represent the irreducible minimum. And
seeing that nearly half its expenditure is for
interest on its public debt, the amount left to
economise on is only about £400,000 a year. Its
unproductive debt—the debt for which it has to
provide interest solely out of taxation—is larger,
in proportion to population, than that of any'
country in Europe, and it pays this interest out
of a revenue which is a marvel of smallness.
Yet there isn’t one reformer in the island
who admits that the only way to make ends meet,.
now that the inter-State duties have been deducted,
from an income that was almost invisible before, is
to impose more taxes. Poor Premier Lewis, who
has already put a quart into a pint pot, is violently
abused for extravagance because he doesn’t erowd
two quarts into a gill. Tasmania’s land produces
twice as heavy crops as the soil of N.S.W., and
pays half as much tax, and the reform party is half
inclined to demand that even the Jd. in the £ be
taken off. Tasmania’s Treasurer, nowadays, is a
man who gets £750 a year and is expected to pay
eighteen pence worth of liabilities out of 6d. and
have 9d. over. And yet poor Treasurer Bird
hasn’t been driven to drink. Almost any other
man, under like circumstances, would take his
grey hairs to the public house and have a great
and terrible Tasmanian drunk—which is two
small beers.

Victoria’s Inspector-General of Prisons, Mr.
Evans, though excessively gradual, certainly
begins to arrive. According to his latest annual
report, just presented to the House, he has estab-
lished a special division for those who are on the
border line of criminality,, where they are worked
separately in healthy, agricultural toil. Also he
strongly objects to the habit of some Judges of
awarding long terms of ‘ ‘ solitary ” just prior to
discharge. He even goes so far as to say that
confinement makes prisoners morose
and -vindictive, and leads to unsettlement of
Working habits.” That, however, is about as far
as he has yet got. He congratulates himself on
the fact that there Were 34 less confinees at the
end of the year than at the beginning, and

» ■
The returned Broughs, come to play their
last Sydney season, had a great reception, with a
note of sadness in it, at Sydney Royal. A certain
pathos attaches to the last of anything. The
“Last of the Mohicans,” viewed with the sunset
of his race upon him, looks less dirty than the
second last Mohican and not so huge about the
feet as previous Mohicans. The “Last Days of
Pompeii,” and the “ Last of the Barons ” and the
“ Last of the Saxon Kings ” and the “ Last Man ”
are all tearful figures. Even the Last Day is
expected to be a heavy function. The Broughs
opened with a new play called “When we were
Twenty-one,” which has a dreary sound, and sug-
gests a thin, old man in a brown suit pondering by
the dying embers about his lost youth. The play
is less dismal than the name might indicate, but
it is an uneven production which fades a
good deal towards the end. Four middle-aged
men—the doctor, the colonel, the baronet, and
Brough, but principally Brough—have brough t up,
in a joint sort of way, the son of a deceased friend,
and this son is a Lout. He lives mostly on
Brough, and he is engaged to the girl that Brough
ought to be engaged to—a girl who looks nice, but
has all the elements of a Cat. When he comes
home with vine-leaves on his hat and his dress-
suit up about his ears, he is hustled to bed with-
out Brough knowing of his idol’s fall; and when
he drop 3 his nice girl and becomes engaged to a
large and notorious music-hall female of the most
terrible description the friends grieve over him,
and Brough decides to save him. The female
agrees to beoome notorious along with Brough for
one month in consideration of £lOOO in order that
her shocking character may become apparent to
the young man. It is only after the delicate
negotiation lias been concluded that Brough
learns that his adopted Lout married the
uproarious female that morning, and was thrown
out by her two hours later when she found
he had no money. Certain gentlemen whom
she had previously cleaned out had told
her in strict confidence that _ his wealth
was enormous, and when she discovered her
error she had driven him off the premises, had
thereafter raged through the hereditary flat of
her ancestors like a shower of wild cats. Brough
finds sorrowfully that he has been paying his
adopted Lout’s wife to become more notorious
than she is already, if such a thing can be.
* * * *
So the adopted Lout again comes home
with his suit up about his ears. He has a
vague idea that Brough has ruined his life, but
after awhile he decides to clear his character by
going to the war and killing somebody. It is
always understood, on the stage, that the remedy
for a blighted life or a blotted character is to go
to the war and kill somebody. The dead body
removes suspicion, and retrieves the past, and
settles up everything comfortably. He is to get
a divorce from the violent female when he comes
back. The violent female has already bolted with
another man. Brough decides to personally
marry the girl who nags at him and is a Cat, and
things straighten out till they are about as
straight as a cow’s horn.
• • # •
Mrs. Brough has a poor part and a very
brief appearance as the violent female. She isn’t
seen throwing things about or ejecting her hus
band. Miss Temple, as a French maid who says
“damn,” gathers up the ruins, and explains in a
terrified whisper how madame had wiped up the
premises and cleaned the stove with monsieur,
and how madame is now an enraged tigress per-
forming in the jungle of the next room. Both
ladies do their little parts well, but the parts are
not worthy of them. Brough is the principal suc-
cess of the play, and is a consistent success all
through. He even gets through the delicate nego-
tiation where he pays the lady to become notorious
with him without the negotiation appearing need-
lessly unpleasant. All through, Brough has the
heavy part of a grieved father trying to reform
an adopted Lout, yet he never becomes pon-
derous. Ernest Yere, who has done much
good work with this co., is a consistent and
rather interesting Lout; and clever Miss Brenda
Gibson plays the girl as prettily as circum-
stances allow. When she rounds on her .worried
benefactor, the dramatist destroys public sym-
pathy with her. He creates an impression that
she is liable to become an embittered Oat Misne
grows old. A sleepv mother, represented by Miss
Susie Vaughan, is a"gem among kindly old ladies,
and Leslie Victor does a casual fat man in excel-
lent style. One decided failure among the smaller
characters may be allowed, by reason of his
obscurity, to pass without identification. On the
whole, if the play was only half as good as the
company there would be little to complain or.
But the brilliance of Act 11. isn’t maintained.
The Lout isn’t a pleasant character, even when he
repents and decides to go to the war and kill
somebody. And the temporary Mrs. Lout is
utterly depressing.
* W Ilf *
*’ A Little Ray of Sunshine,” produced on
Saturday, at Sydney Palace Theatre, for the first
time in Australia, is another example of the success
which may be won in comedy by a playwright
abandoning the classic conventional lines and
giving himself something of the license which the
melodramatist is allowed by custom. Theoreti-
cally, a comedy should be a piece of “life
natural and probable in all its incidents. “ A
Little Ray of Sunshine,” like “Sweet Nell,,
which has just been such a marked success, doesn t
trouble too much about that theory, and cheer-
fully violates many of the rules for standard
comedy-making, when such violation is good for
the fun or the pathos. So it doesn’t stand on the
same artistic plane as such plays as “ Quex,”
“The Liars,” and “ The Tyranny of Tears ; ” it
is, in fact, of the Christmas-supplement order of
art. And for that very reason it has
won a wide popularity in England, which
should be repeated in Australia. The fun
of it is bright and wholesome, the story such
a one as appeals to the best in human nature, and
leads to cheery laughter and to a feeling of sensi-
sibility which is all happiness. Christmas Eve,
and the young squire is to be married on the
morrow; but a shadow is over his happiness in
the shape of an exigent money-lender, to whom he
has had recourse, to save from dishonor his friend
and future brother-in-law, young Markham.
And this shadow suddenly grows and darkens
the whole prospect. Enter the long-missing
Lord Markham, back from Australia with the
wealth of a Croesus and the desire to come
as an unexpected ray of sunshine, and mas-
querading, therefore, as anybody but him-
self. Hilarious fun through his being mis-
taken for the money-lender’s man, the baker’s
man, the cook’s uncle, various wedding guests;
and then disclosure of his real identity, all
the clouds rolled away and a full burst of
sunshine for Xmas. The fun throughout is
excellent; bewildering are the complications of
humorous situations, and the incidental pathos is
as salt with walnuts, good flavoring and whole-
some withal.
• * « *
Mr. Thornton takes the chief burden upon
himself as Lord Markham, and gives a character
sketch which equals in merit anything that he has
before done in Sydney. Both as a merry old man
carrying onjahuge joke at everybody’s expense,
and as the more serious and dignified saviour of
the family, his acting is excellent in a part which
makes exceptionally heavy demands. On the
whole, “ the male support” shows an even stan-
dard of high merit—the most successful being
Mr. Joseph Wilson and Mr. John Denton; Mr. R.
Erskine and Mr. Fred Cambourne play small
parts very well. In the acting of the women
characters there is a good deal of weakness, but
Miss Phoebe Mercer and Miss Katie Lee make
the most of their opportunities, which are not
very great.
** # *
Major Lenehan, late commanding officer
of the historic Bushveldt Carbineers, opens a lec-
turing season in the Centenary Hall, Sydney, on
Tuesday next. Mr. Leneban’s first lecture,
“War—Grim and Gay,” deals with the humors
and tragedies of the march to Pretoria, and the
sorry career of the Bushveldt Carbineers —about
whom, it is understood, he has an account to give
differing very materially from the impressions of
the general public.
** * *
“The Great Ruby” drags on its gilded
career at Sydney Lyceum, but the gallery declines
to take seriously the numerous top-hatted or long-
trained personages who barely keep the wolf from
the door on two or three thousand a year. Even
the stalls are unresponsive to this claim for sym-
pathy. The spectacle of the other noble person-
age, who tries to borrow half-a-crown from a wise
daughter who won’t lend the coin, appeals more
to the human sympathies of the audience, and the
ultra-stylish lady who goes out with the only £lOO
she has in the world to back the wrong horse is
also pleasing till she appears in evening dress with
18-year-old arms. She is supposed to be a middle-
aged lady, worried about her hotel bill and the
difficulty of dressing well on nothing a year, and
her cheerful, youthful, careless plumpness doesn’t
assort with her alleged age and the haggard life
of care she is supposed to lead.
** * *
“Hands Across the Sea” concludes the
Woods-Williamson season in Sydney, with the
exception of a benefit performance of “ Pygmalion
and Galatea ” on the last night. The impossible
ship still permits the various characters to walk
about on the ocean ; though this miracle is dis-
tinctly old-fashioned and unworthy of a bright,
up-to-date company. Another remarkable piece
of scenery is a red staircase that obtrudes its
glittering impossibility right to the footlights.
Jacob’s angels couldn’t have sprinted up that
staircase without breaking their celestial shins.
** * *
“ Mathis,” a new opera by Ernest Truman
and Leumane, is to be played for six nights
at Sydney Criterion, beginning on Saturday.
Slapoffski speaks well of the new opera, which is
promising, and this country’s old bass idol,
Edward Farley, is in the cast, which is more than
promising.
*» * *
The return of Paul Cinquevalli filled
Sydney Tivoli with a cheering audience on Mon-
day. The greatest juggler of modern times comes
with a fresh budget of feats—mostly in the
“strongman” line—but does nothing to eclipse
in power of entertainment, his old marvels.
Cinquevalli is most attractive in. his “little”
tricks —juggling with airy trifles in a fashion
infinitely neat and absolutely certain. Compared
with his billiard-ball, cue, and hat performances,
the new buggy-balancing act does not seem
at all marvellous ; it is a thing which San-
dow or any other strong man might, learn
to do with a little trouble. But in the
balancing of small things, Cinquevalli is certainly
“ the incomparable.” His juggling with a cannon-
ball also gives wonderful proof of the extent to
which the human eyes, muscles and nerves can be
trained to achieve the apparently impossible.
Cinquevalli’s humorously stolid assistant contri-
butes more than ever to the success of his master’s
act; he is a genuine comedian, and would be
worth a big salary as a clown. Mark Anthony,
entertainer, is another fresh arrival —a good
singer, a good imitator, and a fine story-teller.
Mr. Tom Woottwell has another budget of songs,
and Byron and Fagan contribute a new sketch as
smart as the last one. Other good “turns’’are
provided by the Williams Sisters and Miss Jennie
Opie.
#* * *
If Melbourne has begun to tire of musical
comedy the change of feeling is not yet percep-
tible. On Saturday there was a great house at
Her Majesty’s for the first production of “A
Circus Girl.” The demand for standing-room set
in long before 7.45 p.m. When the curtain
actually rose, as per advt., and having found
space, the seatless hundreds held their ground
until after 11 o’clock. “A Circus Girl ” differs
from the average play of its kind in having more
narrative, and fewer irrelevant specialities. It
doesn’t lose all sense of responsibility as soon as
the characters change their garments and proceed
to the business of the second act. Something
rather similar to this was played at the
Princess’s years ago under the title of “In
Town,’’but whereas the other narrative introduced
its characters within the confines of a London
hotel, and then transferred them all to the green-
room of a giddy theatre, this story places them
outside a cafe on the Boulevarde, Paris, before
shifting them to the ante-room of a circus, and
elsewhere. There are three scenes to the second
act of the spangled fiction. Also, the cast of per-
formers includes a peaceful white gee-gee, which
is supposed to oarry Miss Young around the
arena, the scene of action being shut out from
view by an aggravating pair of curtains whenever
an alleged performer goes forth from the front to
do a “turn.” “ A Circus Girl,” though smartly
written, is not so almost persuasive as “In Town ”
was. It strikes one as being a travesty of the
earlier farce. The elderly duke and duchess
and their daughter, who went behind the
scenes at the Frivolity Theatre!in “In Town,”
are represented here by Sir Titus (Bantock)
Wemyss, Lady Wemyss (Blanche Wallace),
and Carrie Moore, as the sentimental offspring.
Florence Young, in the name-part, is a somewhat
rakish reminder of Decima Moore as the priina-
donna whom the old Duke (now Sir Titus) was
privileged to pursue with bouquets and doubtful
intentions. Kenningham and Harold Thorley
are young men adorers of the two principal
girls, but neither of them has a commanding part
like Captain Coddington in “In Town.”
* * * *
And there are others. Hugh Ward wears
the fur-trimmed coat and weather-beaten nose of
a circus-proprietor, a gaudy relic of the grand old
school that always curled its declining hair and
went to bed in Hessian boots. Miss Ghiloni
appears as Madame Drivelli, wife of the above.
She ought to be a somewhat-aged person, turning
the scale at 16 stone, but unfortunately manager
Williamson hasn’t provided the co. with a stout
lady comedian of uncertain age, suitable to such
parts as Madame Drivelli and Carmenita in “ A
Runaway Girl.” So Miss Ghiloni paints lines on
her face, and struggles hard to seem different from
herself. George Lauri is a waiter at the cafe, en-
gaged to marry Maud Chetwynd, who walks on a
wire at the circus, and looks the daintiest
of “treats” in pink, also in blue. A Terrible
Turk is attached to the circus for the pur-
pose of wrestling with all coiners, and Lauri
accepts his challenge, having been deluded by
a mis-statement of the Turk’s length, width and
thickness. On reaching the circus he discovers
that police-officer Kelly, of Melbourne, is the
mountain of flesh he has undertaken to shift.
Then Lauri descends to strategy. He pours
champagne—awful hippodromic champagne—into
Kelly before the event, and five quarts of the
deadly lotion suffice to rob the monster of his
terrors. Kelly when last seen is lying flat in the
arena, with Lauri standing on the place where he
put the champagne. Subsequently, Sir Titus
gets fired, in effigy, from a cannon by his future
son-in-law, but he returns to earth with holes in
his apparel, and helps to wind up the plot.
* * » •
The forces at Her Majesty’s—especially
the orchestra—failed to make the very best of
“ A Circus Girl ”on Saturday night. They gave
a good enough performance in the bulk, and
there was bulk to spare. A company weaker in
some parts and stronger in others would have
given the new show a better send-off. Neverthe-
less, it went off with an air of importance, as
though it had a stake in the country and felt sure
of getting influential support.
** * *
The drama with a real mangle and a
spurious railway train in its lurid constitution is
still ministering to the wants of an easily-excited
populace at the Royal. Probably it will mimister
for another week after this. Then “ The Worst
Woman in London” will take the place of “The
10.30 Down Express” and invite all the good
females of Melbourne to behold what sort of a
sinner they might be if they tried. Lovely woman
in general has a lively craving for the knowledge
of exactly how bad she isn’t.
#* * *
Melbourne theatre-managers surely don’t
need any more frosty seasons to convince them
that their houses, under present conditions, are
lumbago-traps for three months of the year, and
that the public, in a general way, is not taking
any more lumbago at the price. Melbourne grew
up under the impression that it was a mild-winter
city, like Sydney, where one can sit in a mode-
rately well-filled theatre in July without shiver-
ing But, of late years, the truth about the tem-
ferature has displaced the oheerful old fallacy.
'erhaps the houses were comfortable enough when
they were lighted by gas. Now they are electn-
cally lighted, and the people don’t roll up to keep
one another warm, as of yore, and the need for
heating arrangements is a public grievance. As
for the Town Hall, with its draughty entrances
and cold corridors, the place is insufferable. Tha
performers are as bitter in their complaints as tha
scanty audiences. What time they are not trying
to console their fellow-sufferers in front, they sit
in the ‘ ‘ artists’ room ” and sneeze d—n !
** * *
The amateur “ Runaway Girl” perform-
ance, in Melbourne, brought tears to the eyes of
two or three patriotic medical men, who had come
to judge it on its physical points. The nicely-
brought-up daughters of Toorak, South Yarra,
and St. Kilda were under observation as future
mothers of the bong-tong. Were they built for
brilliant maternity successes ? The faculty shook
its head in despair. No limbs to speak of; all
right as regards faces, feet and ankles, yet very
saddening in the more important matters of
shoulders, hips and limbs —especially the latter.
Stout limbs the exception, slim arms and legs
the rule. Shapely enough, but lacking the
dimensions that a medical eye would wish to dwell
upon. Small-boned, alas! M.D. and Surgeon
sorrowed together, during the interval, wondering
what the young men thought of it. Apparently
the young men—the future husbands and fathers—
were not as disappointed as they ought to have
been. Youth is thoughtless, and Limbs don’t
enter very much into its calculations. The boy
adores a sylph ; the veteran admires “an angel
who has something to sit down upon.”
** * *
The “ Chinese Honeymoon ” has arrived
at its fourth and penultimate week in a satisfac-
tory state of health. May Beatty’s Fi-Fi grows
broader, patchier, merrier, though her low-
comedy business is beginning to bruise the feelings
of the More Refined. The red-whiskered Mr.
Pineapple of Edward Lauri continues every night
freshly to try and pump up a laugh among the
audience. By sheer hard work he manages to make a
passable part of that silly bridegroom, who escapes
to sundry arms from the lynx eyes of his hysteri-
cal new-half and her dashing quartet of maids.
The heavy baritone whistles to his Juli-an-io at
the balcony window with growing clearness, but
the girl in the circle badly wants him to ring off
and sing his cuddlesome serenade in the approved
fashion. Princess So-So is still so-so. A slight
huskiness of late sends her out of time, and her
tired air grows apace.
* » * *
The days of “ The Honeymoon ” are num-
bered because “Sweet Nell” is coming back to
the Princess’s on August 2nd. Sweet Nell, her
unprecedented hat, her harpsichord, likewise her
King and His Majesty’s. self-contained dog were
a,t the very zenith of their popularity, as the advt.
would say, when they left Melbourne in April
last. Since then they have been yearningly spoken
of by everybody who knew them, and by many
who missed the chance of making their acquaint-
ance, therefore the date of “Nell’s” return is
noted on the tablet of the local mind in red pen-
cil, and the eye of the local multitude appears to
be looking ahead.
** * *
Last Saturday’s special feature at the
Opera House turned out to be Lydia Yeamans-
Titus, instead of the Florenz party. Of all the
people who have ever happened in a Rickards
programme when somebody else was expected,
Madame L. Y.-T. is undoubtedly the brightest. In
fact, she is brightest singer, mimic, and essen-
tially feminine humorist who ever happened any-
where in the district under any circumstances.
Her sweet, penetrating, educated voice is like
the original youthful voice of Maggie Moore,
with all the little bits of grit taken
from it. Her imitation of a child singing
a song is Celebrated because it is wonderful,
absolutely wonderful, and the humor of her en-
tertainment in other respects is as pure as her
singing. Rickards’s just now is the Home of
musical talent, also the Temple of polite comedy,
and a Palace of pictures on a sheet. Professor
Wrigley, who plays the clarionette, xylophone
and all kinds of wind music, has replaced the
Concertina Sultan. The clever Jackson Family
is back again after a few months interval, and
one of the damsel Jacksons looks as though she had
grown an inch taller meanwhile. Holdsworth, the
illustrated songster, warbles tales of sadness and
joy, and Tom Nawn continues to dream about a
Genie.
# * *
At the Town Hall (where Gerardy lately
caught Melbourne in a cold, soulless mood) there
will be a re-appearance of that fascinating artist,
Mdlle. Antonia Dolores on Saturday next (26th).
The simple intimation that Dolores is going to be
there should suffice to fetch an army of admirers,
and when it is further stated that she will give
only four more recitals in Melbourne prior to
leaving the country for an indefinite period, the
admirers can ask for nothing further in the way of
inducement. Dates fixed for the Dolores’ fare-
wells are 26th, 28th, and 30th July (matinee), and
2nd August.
A quite unaccountable outbreak ot new
dramatic enterprises is promised for Saturday.
At the Bijou an interesting production of “Cyrano
de Bergerac, ” with Henry Lee in the name part,
and Miss Waldorf as leading lady. At the Queen’s
Theatre, where the Victoria Hall used to be, Miss
Ada Willoughby and co. are announced to play a
hilarious comedy called “The Wrong Mrs.
Wright,” which is very like the name of George
Willoughby’s comedy.
** * *
Dan Barry and Co. committed “ A Gilded
Sin ” at the Gaiety Theatre—a sort of an out-
growth from the Bijou—last Saturday evening,
and did not escape the approbation of a numerous
and vociferous audience. “The Sin” is pardon-
able in its cheerful parts. One very forgivable
item in the trouble is Miss Gladys Grant, who,
with a fresh, musical and sympathetic voice and
a youthful and pleasing appearance, should have
future opportunities of sinning histrionically in
a much less desperate degree. Some more or less
complimentary remarks on Daniel B. and his
venture are held over until next week, when the
drama will be changed.
* * *
At Adelaide Royal, Hawtrey’s co. has
come in from an up-country tour with “ The Lady
of Ostend,” by Burnand. As might be expected
from the London Punch man, the humor is rather
thin at times. But the exposure of an erring hus-
band by a casual cinematograph record is right up
.to date. The said husband lies hysterically to
his wife, and altogether there is considerable fun
in the three-act farce. At the Tivoli, the excel-
lent D’Arc marionettes have faded away, and
Rickards has returned to his own again, with
manager Marcus back in command, a»d a variety
co., headed by the Sidney Drews,- who are to
‘‘ inaugurate a new era in vaudeville in Aus-
tralia. ”

The antidote to cyanide poisoning, as supplied
by Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, has
been approved by the Victorian Mines Department as
being the only real antidote known. All who use
cyanide should keep one or two of these on hand. The
price is 10s. each."

The Hannan’s Oroya Gold Mining Co., Kal-
goorlie, has lately placed an order with the Austrai.
Otis Company, South Melbourne, for a double drum first
motion winding engine. The cylinders are 18 inch
diameter by 36 inch stroke, and are fitted with balanced
piston valves. The drums are Bft. in diameter and fitted
with indicators. The engine is completely, controlled
from the driver’s platform. In all respects it is similar to
the engines working so satisfactorily at the Golden Horse-
shoe Mine, Boulder; Long Tunnel, Victoria; and the
Oobar Chesney Mine, N.S.W.*

present is a time of stress becomes very evident. During
the past half-year every item in the balance-sheet shows
a reduction, and the totals are £500,000 less than they
were last December. Australia is tolerably hard-up when
even so solid an institution as the Commercial of Sydney
shows such symptoms.

The banking profits represent ss. lOd. per cent, on the
total shareholders’ funds with which the half-year com-
menced, as compared with 10s. 4d. per cent, earned
during six months to December, 1901. On capital alone
the banking profits are sufficient to pay a dividend of 7s.
per cent. To make the earnings equal 5 per cent., it
Would be necessary to write down the capital from
£1,168,041 to £81,720 and write off all reserves.
Though the nominal reserves have largely increased, and
the interest burden is considerably reduced by the repay-
ment of deposits, there is no sign of any increase in profits
—in fact the earnings for the half-year just ended are the
worst since June, 1899.
A considerable amount of progress is certainly
being made in the repayment or repurchase of the recon-
structed deposits. The figures, since the A.J.S. Bank
began to show the reconstructed deposits separately,
have been as follows :
Due on Reconstructed Paid off during
Deposits. Half-year.
Dec., 1899 .. £5,784,223 ..
June, 1900 „ 5,457,327 .. £326,896
Dec., 1900 .. 5,390,005 .. 67,322
June, 1901 .. 6,318,901 .. 71,104
Dec., 1901 .. 5,269,030 .. 49,871
June, 1902 .. 4,939,221 .. 329,809
This represents a reduction of £845,002 in 2J years.
The new business of the A.J.S. Bank makes
very little progress. New deposits (those that are not
reconstructed) stand at £1,123,994. Since the bank began
to show them separately the highest figure they have
reached has been £1,200,773 at December, 1899, and the
lowest £1,076,556 at June, 1901. The principal asset is
described as “ Bills discounted, advances, and all other
debts due to the Bank, and, other assets at old valuations,
£6,360,790.” New advances (which are presumably good)
and old assets left over from the days before 1893 (which
are admittedly very doubtful) are thus lumped
together. It is impossible to say how much of the
mass consists of old, doubtful assets, but they
may represent £5,000,000 or so. There is nothing
to show what progress is being made with their
realisation. Of the last call £6473 is still unpaid, and it
has stood at exactly the same figure since June, 1899.
No attempt is made to forfeit any shares for unpaid calls.
There is nominally £391,505 of capital still to be called up,
and if any part of this could be called up and devoted to
the repurchase of the 3J per cent, inscribed stock deposits
(obtainable at less than 10s. in the £), it would be money
invested at 7 per cent; £lOO,OOO thus invested would
relieve the A.J.S. Bank of an interest burden of £7OOO a
year, which ought to more than double its profits. Yet
no attempt is made to call up anything. The A.J.S.
never offers any estimate of its losses or any theory as to
when it expects to get on a sound footing again. It
never writes off any capital- in reduction of losses.
It is the most absolutely silent bank in Australia—the
Sphinx with £1,168,041 paid up. The directors never give
themselves away to a serious extent by rash statements
at the half-yearly meeting, about their hopes, fears, ideas
or intentions. In a country with anything like a decent
Banking Act, or any respectable system of State audit,
such an institution would be impossible. It doesn’t even
pretend that the figures in its balance-sheet show any-
thing like its real position ; these assets “ at old valua-
tion ” may mean anything; nor does it mention how old
that valuation is. The writer knows of very few countries
where a bank could go on living on these terms, but in
Australia almost anything in the way of drift and casual-
ness is possible.

The Queensland National Bank’s balance-sheet
would convey to a person unacquainted witn t
history of the institution not the slightest idea of the
position. The bank, as far as can be esti .
after the last reconstruction, about £1,040,000 to private
depositors which was repayable gradually out of profits
without interest. This large liability has t 0 b f P™'* ,® d
for before the bank can be a really solvent institution
again, and it didn’t, and doesn’t, appear in the balance-
sheet at all. Likewise the assets which did appear in the
balance-sheet at December, 1897, were admittedly -
valued to the extent of something like £500,000, and this
also had to be provided for out of profits. There was such
exceedingly great doubt about an item described ( C
December, 1897) as “ Debts in suspense pending realisa-
tion of securities £742,835,” that the old losses which had
to be provided for out of new profits might easily
prove to be more than the £1,540,000 estimated
above —on the other hand they might chance
to be less. In the course of five years, the Queens-
land National Bank has made visible progress to this
extent:

Of course, the items above shown are to some extent
duplicated; the large repayments made to the Govern-
ment were not made wholly out of profits, but probably
to a large extent out of the proceeds of the realisation of
the “ debts in suspense.” Still, the Q. N. Bank has
realised £244,764 of visible profits in the five years, and,
with repayments and accumulated reserves, it has pro-
bably reduced its total shortage by a sixth. And as it
gets its house in order, and realises its non-productive
assets, the earnings, despite the present set-back, ought
to increase. During the past half-year “ Debts in Sus-
pense ” have been reduced by £15,000. Any real pros-
pect of dividends seems to be still many years ahead, and
there is always the possibility of a bank in this state,
especially one which does business in a badly depressed
country, which is going straight towards public msol-
vency, meeting with another big disaster before the
effects of the old disaster are cleared off. Barring that,
the outlook has some elements of hope—remote elements.
Leaving out of consideration the reconstructed
deposits and the mass of more or less dead securities that
are in process of realisation, the live part of the Q.N.
Bank has progressed thus during last year :

* * * •
Victoria has floated another £1,000,000 loan in
London. Not having gone in madly for an Owe’Sullivan
public works policy, it was able, unlike N.S.W., to wait
till the market improved and so gets rather better terms.
Its 3 per cent, securities apparently realise between
£94 and £9s—perhaps £2 more than N.S.W. secured two
months ago. And even then it loses £50,000 on the deal—
that is to say it borrows at a lower rate than its credit
can stand, and £50,000 of the interest is paid out of
principal and added to the permanent debt.
* * * •
The Commercial Bank of Australia (Mel-
bourne) is still another institution which shows diminished
earnings for the half-year just closed. The proposed
writing off of about £1,100,000 of the ordinary capital has
been formally authorised by the shareholders, but pend-
ing certain legal formalities the capital still appears in
the balance-sheet at the old figure. The adjustment will
be made before next balance-sheet is issued. When the
writing down is effected it was estimated six months rgo
that there would still remain a deficit of £1,076,334 to be
gradually liquidated out of earnings, and as the paid-up
capital, even after the writing down, will be about
£2,217,000, the Commercial Bank of Australia has about
£1,100,000 of capital over and above all losses and liabili-
ties. This is as good a position as depositors could ask
for. This half-year, for the first time, the directors
explain the exact position as regards their famous
Assets Trust Co. During the six months to 30th
June the bank made £67,852 profit, and the Assets
Trust showed £22,418 loss. Therefore the net profit
of the whole institution was £38,434. Hitherto the
bank has transferred, as a rule, £30,000 each half-year of
its profits to the Trust, but the directors obstinately
refused to state whether this was the amount required to
meet the Trust’s current loss, or whether it was in-
sufficient to meet the loss, or whether it covered the loss
and left something over to write down 1 the capital value
of the assets. Apparently, however, it may be assumed
that £30,000 half-yearly was just about sufficient to meet
the loss, and the position may be roughly estimated as
follows (except as regards last half-year it is only an
estimate):

On this rough estimate £21,821 has been paid off the
deficit out of profits since June, 1896. But things are
really better than they look, for the figures represent that
the Commercial Bank of Australia scarcely held its own
till June, 1900, while since then it has paid £23,944 off
the deficit out of earnings. And if the directors would
publish, even now, a statement of the exact loss made by
the Assets Trust each half-year sinoe that unfortunate
side-show came into existence, it is just possible that
things would look a little better than this.
The half-year’s net profits are about £3 11s. 6d.
per cent, on the written-down capital, or, rather, on the
capital as it will probably stand when the writing-down
is completed. The principal figures compare with those

During the year the liabilities of the Assets Trnst have
been reduced by £44,433. The bank’s reserve of cash
and liquid securities represents over 39 per cent, on all
its liabilities to the public, which is a remarkably
strong reserve. Altogether, the Commercial Bank of
Australia, despite its deficit of £1,000,000 or so, looks more
promising since it faced the position and revealed the real
state of things with regard to its Trust. From a
depositors’ point of view, with about £1,100,000 of capital
intact, it is as strong as could be asked for. As to the
ordinary shareholder, his position is too poor to be worth
talking about at present, and his next dividend is
probably due about the third Goronation from now.

The earnings represent £3 2s. lOd. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds, as at the commencement of
half-year. It is a very low rate, and if it represents all
that the City Bank of Sydney (a non-reconstrueted bank),
is capable of earning, then a considerable writing-down
of the assets appears to be called for. There is a general
impression, however, based on a bygone statement by the
chairman of directors, that the bank has old losses
(amount unknown) to provide for, and that the formal
accuracy with which it professes to earn practically the
exact amount of its dividend every half-year arises from
the fact that the balance (amount unknown) is applied in
reduction of these losses. In the absence of any theory
about the amount of the alleged losses, or the amount of
the alleged earnings that are applied in reduction thereof,
there is also a complete absence of any theory as to when
the process, if there is such a process, will be completed.
It is an unsatisfactory and hazy state of inference, but in
a country like Australia, where there are banks whose
balance-sheet values are known to differ from the real
values by a million or two, a mere small uncertainty like
the above is a trivial matter.
The figures in the City Bank of Sydney’s bal-
ance-sheet remain nearly stationary, as usual. Deposits
(£1,125,836) are nearly £39,000 more than they were 12
months ago, and about £22,000 less than they were six
months ago. The reserve of cash and liquid securities is
£338,438 (cash £288,438, and Government securities
£50,000), which is equal to rather over 28 per cent, on all
liabilities to the public. Note circulation (£68,044) is
£9000 less than it was a year ago, while advances haye
increased by £33,000. There is very little information in
the City Bank’s balance-sheet, and the institution doesn’t
even profess to be doing anything serious in the way of
re-building the reserve fund which it wrote down heavily
some years ago. The latest quoted sale of its shares
(£8 paid up) was at £4 7s. 6d., at which price they yield
the purchaser 7£ per cent. That plainly hints that the
public doesn’t value its City Bank very highly. If there
is anything to clean up—and in view of the earnings it is
hard to see how it can be otherwise—it would surely be
better to do it at once, and write values down to bedrock.

The Queensland National Bank also shows a falling away,
and though the Royal Bank of Queensland shows an
increase in gross profits that apparently only arises
through the great increase of capital; the earnings per
cent, on the shareholders’ funds are less than they were
six months ago. Still, the Bank of North Queensland is
able to pay its regular little dividend, and make a reason-
ably substantial addition to its reserves, which are all in-
vested in State securities.
Apart from the reduced earnings, things look
reasonably well. The principal items compare with the

suggests that the management aims at stability in the
first instance.
* • • •
For the year ended June, 1901, Queensland had
a deficit of £528,188. For the year ended June, 1902, it
had a deficit of £431,939. For the year that will close at
June, 1903, Treasurer Cribb originally anticipated a deficit
of £247,788, but he contemplates measures by which he
may reduce it to £100,000 or less. Even this means a
shortage of over £1,000,000 in three years, added to
previous deficits of £1,575,000, which were long ago
added to the public debt and quietly put out of
sight. This is over £5 per inhabitant owing for
deficits alone—more than the total debt per inhabi-
tant of Austro-Hungary, Sweden, Norway, Switzerland
or Denmark, and about equal to that of the United States.
Yet the proposal to square the finances by a land-tax or
absentee-tax is repudiated with as much scorn as ever.

As part of the decrease under the heading “ cash and

which is solid enough for all purposes. _ All the same, the
reduction in the bank’s holding of public securities is not
a good point. The decrease in deposits is solely in the
matter of Government deposits; those of the public have
expanded slightly. Australian Governments are pretty
hard up these times, and haven’t much money to deposit
anywhere.

The persistent misconduct of Gentleman One in
bolting with or losing the trust funds is at last bringing
to Gentleman One his natural reward. The N.S.W.
Government proposes following the Maoriland example
by creating a Public Trustee’s Department, and other
States will follow suit one by one. And, later op, it way
be found necessary to prohibit the private trustee alto-
gether, and absolutely take away from Gentleman One
and his kind a branch of business which has proved very
remunerative in the past, and which, but for the fleeting
character of the legal tribe, might have continued so in-
definitely. The lawyers have killed the goose that laid
their golden egg.

evidence on the Greater Sydney scheme, almost the

dwells there any length of time becomes contaminated.
Now “Australia’s Greatest Financial Authority is in-
fected.

Further finds of rich. sulphide ore at Lobb’s
Hole, Kiandra (N.S.W.), reported. Every vein found so
far on this field has bulked from 28 to 30 per cent, copper.
The men with the luck to strike the mother lode will have
a real bonanza.

Australia, has been selected to assist Hr. Rich in examin-
ing the Great Cobar mine on behalf of the London option-
holders. They are to be accompanied by an accountant
from one of the big financial houses. It is expected that
the trio will leave London this week.

SOCIETY
According to Lord Hopetoun’s last sad
farewell to his Australian people, that slim gentle-
ma j ,! ad set lnrrlself the task of moulding and
.modelling the Australian Governor-General for
all time. He intended to form the lines on which
the office was to be run till the end of time, to
erect all precedents, and fashion the customs and
manners, and dot the “i’s” and cross the “t’s”
of the Federal gubernatorial business now and for
ever, amen. He regrets his failure in this matter
with quite a pathetic little break in his voice.
But there was too much frill on his shirt; his
scheme wanted too much postilion and outrider,
i nd R h k ' sto °kinged flunkey and gold lace and
brass band ; and so he stepped down and made
room for someone who would start the game on
simpler and cheaper lines, rather than go back
and set up shop in a humbler way. The
letter explains much. Nobody thought there
was method in Hopetoun’s mildness, and that
all the time he was trying to stamp his genteel
lineaments on our Constitution. His lordship was
flattered by the papers into considering himself
quite a powerful sort of Johnnie, whereas at his
best he was a weak, wire-drawn nobleman with a
nice smile. Had the papers let him alone, he
might have been content to be a fragile aristocrat
with an engaging smirk, and would have been as
good a Governor-General as Australia expects to
get from Mr. Joseph Chamberlain.
ITS MEANING.
DALLIDALLY (a chronic procrastinator): “I
dreamed last night that I — er — ah—proposed
to you. I wonder what that is a ‘sign of?”
MISS LINGERLONG (desperately): “It is a sign
that you have got more sense when you are
asleep than when you are awake.”
The Straits Times, a Singapore news-
paper, on the Australian Governor-General inci-
dent :
The question arises—Why should a rich country like
Australia grudge its Governor-General an adequate
Salary ? The answer is, that the Labor party—savagely
anti-imperialist and Republican in its tendencies, and
endowed by heritage, in many instances, with a genea-
logical tree that has its roots in Botany Bay—controls
the Commonwealth Parliament and goes in for petty
economies and gigantic jobs.
There is one radical mistake in this. A man who
was wise enough to have a convict for a grand-
father doesn’t become a Labor member in Aus-
tralia ; he is far more likely to draw thousands a
year in rents from the city property which his
ancestor got for being a reasonably good Convict
(the unreasonably bad convicts were not allowed
to leave any posterity), and sit in one of the Tory
Upper Houses.
The Melbourne press announcement that
Lord Hopetoun wished to call himself Marquis
of Linlithgow was made in defiance of a desire
that the new title should be kept dark until the
King had signified his approval, a question of
etiquette being involved. The next day, the dear
departed was waited upon by municipal and other
sycophants, several of whom called him “Marquis
of Linlithgow,” whilst one goatish person fra’ that
county slobbered poetically over the admirable
choice of a title. And it was beautiful to see the
perturbed Wallington dodging about behind the
wincing Hopetoun and trying to catch the mis-
guided slobberer’s eye.
* • » *
Governor Rawson was at the commercial
travellers’ dinner in Sydney on Saturday nigh c,
and left early on account of illness in his home.
It is a pity His Ex. did not happen to leave
before the political orations were made. It was
in shockingly bad taste for the speakers to intro-
duce party politics at a social function to which
the Governor was invited.
* * * *
The Clarkes are certainly the most retiring
gubernatorial family Victoria has yet known.
Were it not for occasional pars, in the “society ”
sections of the weekly press His Ex.’s women-folk
would scarcely ever be heard of. As for the
Governor himself, he bobs up at an occasional
assemblage of a dull and strictly moral character,
and speaks a few elevating sentiments in a small
voice, and the whole business is reported in the
obscure backblocks of the dailies under no-account
headlines. In editorial offices Gov. Clarke is
evidently of about the size and significance of a
half-pint of peanuts. The Argus, at least, ought
to know better. Even its readers will presently
be asking what’s the use of a Clarke who is prac-
tically only an Imperial clerk.
♦ * * •
Little Chief Justice Way, S.A.’s Lieu-
tenant-Governor, although Chancellor of the
University, is far from popular with the students,
who immercifully chaffed him at the laying of the
foundation-stone of the medical school this month.
Sammy lost his temper under the ordeal, which,
however, seemed on this occasion to be rather
enjoyed by Gov. Tennyson and other distinguished
onlookers. The C. J. lacks dignity, and has a lot
of little ways (not children, but manners) which
tend to make him ridiculous.
* * • *
Some Melbourne people who dearly love
to get their names into the papers, are just now
amusing themselyes with .the silliest sort of
philanthropic scheme that ever commended itself
to the wealthy unemployed. Inspired by Royal
and Mayoral example in the dear Homeland they
are taking up subscriptions for a dinner to the
Melbourne Poor, or some of them. One dinner,
whereat the guzzled crowd will be expected to
rejoice over their brief respite from “rats inside,”
on the understanding that the strange sense of
fulness is due to His Gracious Majesty. At time
of writing £35 “only” had been contributed
towards the blow-out fund, but more was expected.
If the Poor of Melbourne were consulted in
the matter, they would vote unanimously for a
rational distribution of the One Dinner fund in
relief doles of, say, 10s. per dole on the lottery
system, but the mere raising of coin for practical
charity purposes is not an attractive thing. The
promoters of Good Causes expect a special advt.
as a reward for their enthusiasm nowadays, and
the advt. must be of a fashionable character. A
reputation for having supplied a hungry mob with
one severe.dose of dyspepsia is the most fashion-
able notoriety in the market this Coronation year.
It entitles the favored individual to stretch forth
his hands over the temporary dining-table and
say, “ Bless you, my Feeders ! ” in a royal purple
tone.
* * * *
Commandant General Hutton swooped
down on Tasmania t’other day and straightened
up the local millingtary with a few well-chosen
expletives. That little job through, he took a look
round, and was promptly captured by the pushful
curator of Hobart Art Gallery, who is ever lying
in wait for titled personages. Next day the local
paper put it: —
The General, who is an authority on art, expressed
himself as very pleased with the Art Gallery collection,
and considers Mr. Piguenit the leading landscape painter
of Australasia, an honor Mr. Piguenit richly deserves.
It is kind of the General to so freely pass his
authoritative word for painters, but much of that
sort of thing will surely undermine the reputation
of his Blanky Oath. A military commander who
doesn’t keep his 8.0. above suspicion is altogether
too commpnplace for the Commonwealth.

The daily papers don’t seem to tumble to
the fact that the cabled report from S’Africa that
Dan Kelly and Steve Hart, two members of the once
notorious Kelly gang of bushrangers joined one of the
Australian contingents under assumed names, and after
taking their part in the fighting returned to Australia
is obviously intended as a satire on the methods
of some of Australia’s gallant soldiers. It was a
smart satire, and it has got right home.

TRYING TO FORGET.
JONES: “ What do you think of that cigar I gave you?”
SMITH : “ I don’t think of it at all — l’m trying to forget it.”
Senator Matheson, a wise man out of the
West, is hotly opposed to the 25 per cent, duty on
“brum.” jeweller-y, and in the House drew a
pathetic picture of the poor woman robbed of her
little adornments by a shameful tyranny. “To
the poor woman cheap jewellery is an absolute
necessity of life,” exclaimed Mr. Matheson pas-
sionately. “No sooner does she get a spare
shilling than she runs off to purchase a personal
adornment to attract the men. The women
wanted the franchise to be in a position to deal
with these very questions.” The spectacle of the
poor washerwoman robbed of her chance of fasci-
nating a man with, the baleful glitter, of a nine-
penny diamond, or forced to remain single because
of the shameful tax imposed upon matrimony by
the increase of 9d. a gross in brass wedding-rings,
so worked upon the philanthropic senator that his
speech was presently lost in a scream of indigna-
tion, and he collapsed in a state of temporary Free-
trade paralysis.
* • » •
Premier See has introduced the Eddy
Orphanage Bill, which provides for the establish-
ment of an institution for the care of fatherless
children of N.S.W. railway employees. The
funds have been raised by subscription, chiefly
from the railway staff, and the orphanage is to be
a lasting memorial of the deceased Commissioner.
Mr. Hollis, Labor Member for Newtown, and him-
self an ex-railway man, has formally protested
against the passing of the Enabling Bill. Hollis is
a man who takes a lively interest in charitable
effort, and he points out that throughout the
civilised world the system of incarcerating children
in institutions has been universally discarded in
favor of the family life obtained for the little ones
by boarding them out. Of course the bill will
pass, the subscribers can do what they like with
their own money, and, if they have decided on an
orphanage, they will most likely have it. All the
same, institution-life never yet did a child any
good.

* * * »
The teachers in charge of over 300 of the
State schools in S.A. receive under £lOO a year.
There is even an assistant inspector of schools
(male) at £270 a year ! The Department appar-
ently reckons that youngsters drift into teaching
without, as a rule, quite knowing what they are
doing, and that, after a few years of it, being then
good for nothing else, they must stay on, even if
they are only offered two meals a day.

There is a movement to shift Sydney Zoo
to Parramatta. Parramatta, the Geelong of
N.S.W., is the sort of place where wild beasts
would probably feel at home.
Fifty or 60 or more
years hence a new trouble
will fall upon Australia—it
will begin to be haunted by
the old inhabitant who was
the first baby born in Aus-
tralia under Federation.
The eastern States were
federated earlier than the
middle and west; the union
began with the first second
of the year 1901, and, owing
to the difference of time,
the new year in Australia
begins in the east and
crawls westward like a
caterpillar ; therefore, as a
matter of strict accuracy,
the first baby was presum-
ably an Eastern baby. But
there is no official record to
show which baby it was,
and long years hence it
will have multiplied by
dozens ; it will be of both
sexes; it will be white,
black, and brown ; and it
will be bitterly indignant
because its word is doubted.
Likely enough, the first
Federal baby is already
dead, but that will make no
difference. The real infant
should have been officially
stamped.

How’s this for a tem-
perance sermon ? A swaggie
recently, in the dark,
walked into the Campaspe,
off a bridge, and was
drowned. The body was
found lying across a log, in
mid-stream, the only part
out of the water being the
right hand, which still
grasped an unopened bottle
of beer. A bottle of same
was found in each side-
pocket of the coat—un-
broken—though the man’s
spine was fractured and his skull smashed by the
fall.
* » * *
Never be too civil. In a Victorian bush
town, recently, when theP.M. was paying a fort-
nightly visit to the Police Court, a neatly-dressed
young man of 25 took off his hat and said, cheer-
fully, as the buggy drew up, ‘ ‘ Good morning, Mr.
—-—-.” The P.M,, thinking he was a local
resident, of substance, advanced, and shook hands
with him cordially. In the third case called on,
the civil young man was arraigned for cutting
timber without a license. “ Ah,” remarked the
P.M. to a brother-Justice, “the young gentleman
I spoke to outside the Court. What is he?”
“ Don’t know what he is now,” replied the beak,
“ but a short while back he was boots at Smith’s
pub.” The P.M. was reckoned a lenient man,
who, in such cases, usually inflicted a half-crown
penalty. “What!?” he exclaimed, wrathfully.
“And the fellow had the impudence to shake
hands with me ! Fined £5 ! ”

HIS EXPERIENCE.
JUNIOR: “Do you think drink shortens a man's days.”
SENIOR: “I do. I was teetotal for a month once, and it seemed
like two years ”
A case was heard at Grafton (N.S.W.)
this month, when a charge of alleged cruelty to a
mite of seven years was preferred ag linst a
couple, man and wife, to whose care she had been
entrusted by her relatives. The police brought a
strong-looking case, but defendant’s lawyer, with-
out going into the evidence, successfully objected
that there was no “proof” that the Act under
which proceedings had been initiated extended to
Grafton. It is evident that someone blundered in
allowing the case to be squelched for want of
formal evidence which could easily have been got
ready.
* * * *
At last Adelaide Cremation Society has
advanced so far as to call tenders for the erection of
its furnace on the site granted by the Govt, near
North Terrace Cemetery. Millionaire Barr Smith
is one of the most enthusiastic members of the
society. The N.S.W. Cremation Society still
nominally exists, but it has not yet managed to
get an oven.

Melbourne Hospital has gained a most
unsavoury notoriety over the case of James
M‘Kean. M‘Kean, who was an old Imperial
pensioner, was left at the hospital by a woman
who couldn’t explain what was the matter with
him, except that he was very ill and was spitting
blood. Then she went away—probably having
her own business to look after. No doctor
attended the old man. He was simply left
lying about for a little time and then handed
over to the police, and next day he
inconsiderately died in the gaol hospital, which
fact led to fuss and inquiry. If hospitals get into
the habit of having their patients arrested in this
off-hand fashion for being illegally on the premises,
or something like that, then the popular idea of a
hospital wants revising. It used to be a place
where the sick were taken in. If it is to be a
place whence the sick are thrown out, then it
wants a chief bruiser and wrestler in charge
instead of a doctor.
* * « *
A candid plaintiff in a Melbourne Court
lately explained some of the amusements of his
“push.” It was originally a football club, but
developed into a push, and it used to meet on
some vacant land behind Collingwood Town Hall.
They would sit along the rails and “ number off.”
Sometimes they would sit on a rail from 6 till
12 p.m. They discussed football matches and had
arguments. Most of them did nothing. The
gentleman was suing for wrongful arrest for
vagrancy, and, as most of his mates had been
more or less frequently convicted, his case broke
down badly. But sitting on a rail for six solid
hours and arguing the point —what joy !

In Vic. railway refreshment rooms you
may obtain a shilling standing-up meal —fried
chops, steaks and sausages, and mostly a lot more
is cooked than is eaten. The joker knew this
and solemnly said to the waitress, “ Miss, my
doctor has ordered me to eat oidy chops, steaks
and sausages that have been fried several times; can
you give me anything of that sort ? ” The young
thing looked joyful. “ I can, sir ! ” she replied,
breathlessly. “Well,” he said, coldly, “give me
cold corned beef, please.”
“ Adelaide ” sends a
Fable for Wives:
An enthusiastic Female
Cricketer, having Won the
Toss in a game of Single
Wicket, joyfully went In to
Bat. For awhile she Batted
carefully, but afterwards she
grew Careless.
“Excuse Me,” said the
Umpire. “ None of My Busi-
ness. But if you go on Like
This you will certainly Knock
Down your Own Wicket.”
“ There is No Danger,” said
the V >man.
“ There is Always Danger,”
said the Umpire. “ And the
Other Woman is very Keen
on getting an Innings. Her
Bowling is Feeble, and you
can stay In pretty well All
your Life if you care to Try.
But if you Knock Down your
Own Wicket you will cer-
tainly be Out.”
However, the Game con-
tinued As Before.
“ Why is the Crowd Laugh-
ing?” said the Woman at
length.
“ Because you have Put
Yourself Out as I Foretold,”
said the Umpire. “ And the
Other Woman has been Having
her Innings for Some Time
without your Knowing.”
“Then it is a Sin and a
Shame 1” she said. “ Also it
is Not Cricket.”
Moral: What is Worth Winning is Worth Keeping.
« * • *
The greatest white slave of the day is the
lady-help. Here is a list of every-day duties
which one lady-help found posted in her room in
a Melbourne suburban house :
Up at 6 a.m. After lighting fires, sweep breakfast-
room, dust and prepare breakfast for 7.45. During break-
fast-time sweep verandah, clean and lay dining-room
fires, sweep carpets and dust, wash dishes [glass and
silver first]. Tea-towels to be washed and put online
each morning, after each washing-up. Empty slops and
thoroughly clean basins and baths. All towels in the
house to be put on the lines. Knives to be cleaned after
every meal. After making beds, sweep and dust all the
rooms every day. Bath-rooms and halls to be
swept and dusted every morning. Alternate Mon-
days the washing to be damped and folded. Scullery,
sink, cupboard, boiler and troughs to be thoroughly
scrubbed every day. Windows and Venetian blinds
cleaned and washed on Mondays. Ironing on Tuesdays.
Bedrooms and kitchen washed out thoroughly on Wednes-
days, and the stoves and ovens cleaned. Halls and
bath-rooms, press, pantry, knife-boxes, safes and dressers
washed on Fridays. Kitchen stove and ovens, scullery
and vegetables prepared for Sunday. All brass-work
cleaned twice a week, also out-houses.
Then followed this elaborate note as to how the
washing was to be done:—
On Fridays clean boiler with bathbrick and vinegar.
Soak clothes Saturday morning. The sheets, pillow-cases
and tablecloths in No. 1 trough. Skirts, shirts, and all
under-linen in No. 2 trough. Towels, dusters, etc., in
No. 3 trough. The clothes in No. 2 trough are to be
washed on Saturday afternoon ready for boiling on Mon-
day morning. The clothes after being boiled must be
rinsed in No. 1 trough, blued in No. 2, the wringers to be
used always, the washing-hoard only for towels. All
flannels must be washed in three waters. When the
washing is finished, draining-board, sink, cupboard,
under-sink, troughs, boiler-lid, floors, &e., to be all
thoroughly cleaned and scrubbed. The bath, sink, etc.,
to have two bucketsful of boiling suds.
All this work bad to be carried on along with the
household cooking and ordinary household duties.
The rate of wage was ten shillings a week. Part of
the moral is that every girl, if she has to go into
the domestic circle, should go there as Bridget
Murphy, pot-walloper, with plenty of sass for the
missus, instead of as poor, trembling Miss Smith,
lady-help. The catalogue above printed is also a
powerful illustration of the good time which
awaits hundreds of nice girls when the Women’s
Christian Temperance Union shall have succeeded
in abolishing the barmaid (who now gets £1 a week
at least, and the best of food at the hotel table,
likewise presents of gloves and theatre-tickets,
and a reasonable chance of showing herself off to
advantage among marriageable men) and forces
her into the hideous gloom of sweated domestic
service in a pious and underfed family of Ranters.
A good gratitude story is told by
Mr. W. Steedman, formerly owner of
Williamstown (Melb.) baths. One day,
he saw a man, manifestly unable to
swim, dive into about 12ft. water.
Steedman plunged in to the rescue, and
eventually both were brought ashore
much exhausted. Steedman soon re-
covered, and helped in restoring the
other to consciousness. At last, the
stranger was able to swallow some of
Steedman’s brandy, and, after being
warmed up with hot bottles from
Steedman’s house, was dressed in a
suit of Steedman’s clothes, and led to
a cab paid for by Steedman. “Well,”
he said in farewell, “I’ll take good
care never to come near your blanky
baths again ! ”
A letter from Vereeniging, S’Africa,
states that peace was signed at one minute to mid-
night on Saturday, 31st May. This one minute
meant a good deal, as wagering was very warm on
the event. The odds were stol on Peace —even
money it would not be signed before the Ist June.

A correspondent :
For some murky reason the Brisbane dailies omit all
mention of the fact that there are two separate trials
going on at Mitchell in which two men are charged as
being accessories (after the fact) in connection with the
Kenniffs. This suppression of good copy wants account-
ng for.
Recently an Adelaide servant was delivered
of twins in the back yard of her mistress’s house.
Then she went inside, wrapped them in paper,
and, going to the Salvation Army shelter, asked
for a bed. They sent her to the Destitute
Asylum, where she was taken in, and at latest
both babies were alive and well. The curse of
Eve evidently weighed but lightly on that poor
mother.
* • » *
“P.C.0.0.” on Victorian police :
It’s a thousand to one that that Vic. police inquiry will
prove a fizzle. Narrowed down, the charges don’t amount
to anything like what they appear on paper. One
regarding the alleged conviction of men known to be
innocent is several years old ; and, as to it, whether true
or not, evidence will be impossible to obtain. Other
charges are trivial, others are merely word against word.
But while the investigation is proceeding, some inquiries
might be made re the Orange and Green factions in the
Vie. force. The public would be paralysed to hear the
evidence at the average police inquiry board. The usual
procedure is for a senior officer of one color to lay a charge
(say) of drunkenness against a junior cop of the other,
whereupon six or seven other policemen of their respec-
tive colors turn up cheerfully to swear— one side, that
the man was hopelessly and fearfully drunk, the other
that he was as sober as the presiding officer of the
inquiry. If the public only knew the class of evidence
tendered by constables about one another, they would
marvel at the comparative truthfulness of the bobby
when he testifies against strange civilians in Court.

A correspondent:—
The state of the grass in most Sydney parks is simply
disgusting, from the filthy habits of loafers. Except in
the Botanic Gardens, there is no grass-space in all Sydney
where a child can play in safety. Now in Germany
children have places reserved for their use, and are pro-
vided with clean sandheaps as well. Why cannot a small
portion of each Sydney park be railed off for children,
and provided with a heap of clean beach sand ?

“ Jonesey”
writes from
Pal merston
(M.L.) :
Very notice-
able about here
at present are
the Mormon
elders kicking
round. They are
easy to pick,
and no good
judge of char-
acter would
choose most of
them to accom-
pany his sister
home on a dark
nigh t. They
have a style all
their own of
getting' hold of
the gilt in this
country. Their
work lies chiefly
among the
Maoris, and
they hold a
larger sway with
these people
than all the
other denomin-
ations put to-
gether. (I am
speaking for the
North Island
and from per-
son al experi-
ence. What few
Maoris there are
in the South
Island need not
be taken into
consideration).
All the other
sky - pilot cad-
gers go to the
native and ask
straight out for
his money in
cash. Not so the
Mormon. He
sees with what
reluctance the
Maori lets go of
the all - power-
ful ; so he be-
gins by teaching
his stomach to
stand dried
shark, mutton-
bird, raw pawa,
&c. In fact, he
lives with the
native, and does
all he can to
please in order
to effect his
purpose, and,
after getting
the run of the
ropes, he “ kids”
the native to give him a horse to go round and preach.
This nag the Saint sells at first opportunity, then pads the
hoof to the next pah and goes through the same perform-
ance. These elders have a hang-dog expression, but
have their eyes keenly open to biz. About three years
ago, when Te Whiti prophesied the end of the wofld on a
certain date, his followers believed him and did their
best to put away as much “waipiro” as the time would
allow. Monev running short, they sold their horses,
cattles, and pigs at next door to nothing. I knew
one Mormon elder pretty well, and about a fort-
night after Te Whiti’s prophecy had fizzled, he said to
me : “ Say, mate, how did you come out of that ‘ gineral
resurrection’business?” “I bought five horses out of
the pound,” I said, “ and will clear about £20.” “ Well,”
said the Saint, “ I reckon I got on the right side of the
Almighty over it too.” He had bought jewellery, horses,
clothing, and all sorts of property from the poor devils.
Yet these Mormons have a bigger hold among the
Maoris to-day than ever, and I don’t suppose 20 per cent,
of Maorilanders know that there are Mormons working
among them. I have seen, at a native pah, other
preachers of the Gospel accost these Saints, supposing
them to be Maori pakehas. In fact, I myself never see an
undesirable-looking white knocking round among the
natives without asking if he is a Mormon elder.
