The Bulletin notes with pride and plea-
sure that at the meeting of the Commonwealth
Lodge No. 12, Royal Ancient Order of Buffaloes,
Balmain, the Grand Primo, Sir George Some-
body, made a presentation to the Primo and
raised him to the rank of “ City Marshal. ” It is
pleasant to know that the Ancient Buffaloes can
—like King Ned and the Pope -knight a man
and make him a “ Sir,” and can also raise a man
—a Balmain man, too—to be a city marshal.
When the Royal Order of Modern Goats, in
addition, becomes addicted to creating dukes and
earls and marquises at its monthly meetings, and
showers G.C.M.G.’s around the suburbs between
drinks, it ought to take some of the curl out of
John See’s K.C.M.G-. tail and impress upon the
people generally the ludicrousness of the title
business.
* * • *
At St. Arnaucl (Yic.), recently, a man
burst his left eye whilst blowing his nose, and the
injured optic had to be extracted to save the right
one. Which recalls the famous Italian tenor who
broke his collarbone in taking a high note.
The St. George’s Hospital (N.S.W.) chain-
letter scandal is still unsettled. It is an eloquent
argument in favor of some stringent regulation by
law for the auditing by public officials of all
charity collections. No one, however pure his
or her motives, should be allowed to appeal to tha
public on behalf of a charity without some pro-
vision for a stringent audit of accounts.
* * * *
The most daring of fictionists would
hardly venture upon presenting such an episode
as that in which, the other day, three men held
desperately on to the ankle of a lunatic, hanging
head-down wards from the window of a carriage in
a train running at 50 miles an hour on a Victorian
railway. The maniac, who was in the custody
of a policeman, had managed to slip -off the
handcuff by which he was attached to
another prisoner, and it was this prisoner
who was barely in time to get a grasp on
the other man’s trousers as he dived out to
destruction. Then the constable and another
passenger laid hold also, but so furiously did the
madman struggle that he got clear, all but one
foot, to which his rescuers held grimly on until
some line-repairers signalled the engine-driver,
and the train was pulled up, barely in time to
prevent the man from being smashed against a
post which stood close to the line.

Adelaide has four or five bibulous old
women who are competing for a most-convictions
record. Under the idiotic system which awards
these diseased cranks for each “offence” just
enough gaol to make them thirsty, it has been
possible for the youngest of the competitors to
pile up 100 convictions since 1884. An older
hand has reached the 200 mark.
SACRIFICIAL MELBOURNE.
PARALYSED ABRAHAM (in Bourke-street): “ What a holy people these
Melbournites are!”
Justice Cohen, president of N.S.W.
Arbitration Court, taking his vacation on the
stroke of the clock, despite the pressure of busi-
ness, strikes a number of workers as a serious
grievance. But that is really a workmanlike prin-
ciple : down tools when time is up. The way
over the difficulty is either to pay Justice Cohen
overtime rates, or put another hand on the Bench
to cope with the rush of work. It is true that in
the days of Forbes and Dowling, N.S.W. Judges
had no settled holiday season. They worked from
year’s end to year’s end ; but in those days work-
no men had no eight hours and overtime rates.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
~ _. a Sydney, July 22, 1902.
Mr Dear Arini.—
• 1?“? sorr 7 Scheme of Things isn’t much sorrier
in Sydney itself than when I wrote several days
ago. State Ex and family are quite well • and
Mr. vy. H. Howe, vice-regal private-sec., is
recovering from his attack of typhoid. Strange
what a number of persons who live in Govt
Houses get typhoid ! Every vice-regal residence
in this continent and in M.L. has had its share of
the disease. -Mr. Rowe’s attack supervened upon
influenza. Doctor Fiaschi is doing the pulse-
feelmg and prescription-writing at Cranbrook,
where the patient is being nursed.
Most persons’ dancing-shoes are worn as thin as
sixpence, but, fortunately, the ball, charity and
otherwise, is rolling more infrequently, and their
Exes, are getting a rest from that form of duty to
the Empire. But, as a counter-irritant, they are
being played and sung silly. There are concerts
m the afternoon, and concerts at night by well-
known artists ; and recitals by someone quite
new on every washing-day.
v .Max Schliiter, with his Beethoven face and
big head, closed his season at the Centenary
Hall on Tuesday, just as Sydney society, en-
lightened by Danish residents, was about to
realise that the violinist’s family is personally
friendly with Danish royalty, and that one of his
uncles is an admiral. These things are better
than many sonatas to persons who have gone re-
cently to Heralds’ College to find a grandfather.
When Max was younger he used to rob the cab-
horses on the stand near his school of their tails,
to weave into violin-strings. Old cigar-boxes
supplied the wood work.
In sheep and cattle lands in this State, and
beyond it, the Scheme of Things is sorrier than
ever. A squatter writer (his young head that
was sable, slightly silvered in a few months) has
fled from his cattle lands to an obscure little
coast, town, simply to get away from the cruelty
of witnessing the Potter thumping his Clay so
heavily.
Excellent Rawsons were at the ball in aid of
Balmain Hospital last Wednesday. It was a
brilliant moonlight night, and as tliey drove over
the (new) Pyrmont bridge His Ex. and partygave
several delighted bleats. The wide bay, the ships
that seemed to dream at anchor over their inverse
shadows, the long, quivering filaments of light on
the water, the silence, the slight white mist that
shut out unsightly details. It was a good sight.
The Balmainers had decorated their hall beauti-
fully, and the supper-tables had the first
all-wattle adornments of the season. Fol-
lowing night a juvenile fancy-dress ball' was
held, and a “Pillar-box,” an “Old English
Gentleman,” a “Peace” (white wings and olive
branch) were awarded prizes by the judges. A
lovely little three-year-old girl, “Snowflake”
(chiffon over green silk, strewn with flowers),
danced with perfect precision and gravity in the
Lancers, doing her “steps,” and, when she had
to bow to her partner, kneeling on one chubby
knee.
“ Youth’s sweet-scented manuscript ” (edited by
playwright Esmond) presented (closed and open)
under the name of “When We Were Twenty-
one,” by the Broughs, will be probably a great
success.. Men of 40 will rush it, if only to learn if
“the trinity” were the same kind of fools that
they themselves were at 21. And boys and girls
of 21 will seek it to snatch the fearful joy of see-
ing a scarlet woman (in training for marriage into
the British peerage). Before “this person”
appeared, and all the time that the poster which
the players could see on the hoarding outside the
stage setting was being described, an elderly gen-
tleman seated in the aulienee near me was irri-
tatedly saying to his wife, “Why can’t we see
the picture, too ? Brough should have managed
to bring the poster into view !” His wife must
have been of the order that is above rubies, for
she tried to soothe him with “But you’ll see the
woman herself, presently.”
The play is full of points that appeal to the
vanity of certain persons. ‘ ‘ My mother, ” remarks
the ingenue, “says that quiet fair men are always
like that” (lady-killers and other things); and
straight every fair man in the audience, even
though he be louder than a steam siren, blushes
like a peony and looks down at his shoes, and his
darker friends nudge him knowingly, saying
“Ah, ah !” The writer of “W.W.W.2I’-’ is keen
on the theme of the 20-years-old girl in love with
the 40-years-old. man, and the man of 40 in love-
on the stage—is adorable; he does make such a
new kind of idiot of himself. The girl, one must
confess, is a very Rosalind in the courage with
which she does the running, and the initiative she
takes in the love-making.
The Broughs mostly have a nice breakfast-
table, at which none but the stout mother eats
anything, and a nice (I suppose, tvpical) English
girl skirmishes round for fresh toast. Then there
are men in correct evening dress and dainty
modern rooms, with high, ruby-shaded lamps, and
a portrait of the ingenue on the wall. She is so
sweet, so good, and wearisome. Now the red
woman—well, “moral qualities rule the world,
but at short distances the senses are despotic.”
Mrs. Brough, as the somewhat scarlet woman,
makes a splendid picture in her rose-pink satin
and jewels, and she acts the soulless daughter of
the . horse-leech admirably. A packed and
fashionable house greeted her. One of the
fashionables had a black bird fastened to her left
eyebrow. It may have been tethered to her hair,
but it certainly roosted on her eyebrow. Nellie
Stewart got a spontaneous burst of applause when
appeared in the managerial box. Mrs. J. C.
M iihamson, back from the Mountains that are
called Blue, was also present.
The Ladies Educational Council at Leeds is
training young women for the positions of wives in
the colonies, yet, according to our latest local
statistics, if they come out here they will only
have seven-eighths of the chance of a husband
which they would have enjoyed had they stayed
in England, Anyhow we shall welcome the
trained-in-household-work young woman, for if
she doesn’t get a man she’ll have the refusal of a
dozen good situations. Other day a Sydney lady
advertised for. a “general” and amongst her
replies was this one: “Madam.—l am willing
to accept the position. Yours affectionately,
A.Z. R.S.V.P.” If the Leeds E.C. would
only . send out some cooks! Cooks have an
especially good time in Australia, even to the ex-
tent of possessing visiting-cards with (in one in-
stance of which I know) their employer’s address
and their nights at home printed thereon.
Speaking at annual meeting of the Cleveland-
street Working Girls’ Club awhile ago State Gov.
Rawson suggested that, if some of the ladies
present who had cooks of their own at home
would send them down to the club once a month,
or so, to give lessons to the girls, an immense
benefit would result to the future husbands
and children of those girls. South Kensington
(England) School of Cookery has opened a
new branch for the purpose of training ladies
to take posts as housekeepers and matrons
and cooks. The school is also receiving girls of
gentle birth, but scanty means, who wish to
become professional cooks.
The First Lord of the British Admiralty has
asked, so the cables tell us, whether the Colonies
fully realised what the Royal Navy meant to
them, and we answer—if the colonial men don’t,
the women do. Naval engagements don’t neces-
sarily mean two armoured ships trying to sink
each other. Naval husbands are very nice ; naval
hops are very nice ; naval bands are delightful;
afternoon tea on board the warships charming;
naval men are exquisite flirts, because of the
practice they have. Oh, yes ! We know what
the Navy means.
Commandant F. H. Brownlow, the new head
of N.S.W. Naval Brigade, turns out to be a
brother of Wallace 8., the baritone. He is 43
years of age, and biographies of him don’t say
whether he is married or not. His father was
an officer in the Coldstream Guards, and served
through the Crimean war, and his mother was
associated in the campaign with Florence Night-
ingale. He came to Australia many years ago,
and his portraits show him to be very like his
melodious brother.
Miss Myrtle Meggy, a fresh girl of nearly 15,
on whose behalf friends of the late Sydney Moss
are exerting themselves, was a favorite pupil of
the deceased musician, who, had he lived,
would have personally guided her on her upward
musical way. She was 12 when her late master
introduced her to the critics at a pianoforte
recital, where she played among other numbers, by
heart, a Mozart Concerto and the Sonata “Pa-
thetique.” He again brought her forward last year
at a concert, and in her programme on that occa-
sion were two Beethoven Sonatas, a Concerto by
Mendelssohn, and several of Bach’s Preludes.
Mr. Moss thought highly of his pupil’s sympathy
and promising technique. At the meeting con-
vened at Paling’s last week to provide funds for
the completion of Miss Meggy’s musical education,
it was decided that, in place of a concert, subscrip-
tions be raised, and the sum of £lOl was donated
on the spot. Messrs. J. Lemmone and H. N.
Southwell are hon. secs, of the fund, and Messrs.
Paling and Co. hon. treasurers.
The Highland Society ball, to which the State
Governor and family came about 11 o’clock, was
very poorly attended. Dancing space was so
great that the women need not have held up their
trains, which the majority did all the evening,
whether walking or dancing—held them high, as
if out of ankle-deep mud. There were not many
kilties, and no bagpipes. No one danced the
Highland Fling or the Sword Dance, and there
was but one Highland Reel—hid far down the
programme. There was a total absence of brown-
heath and-shaggy-wood atmosphere about the
frisk, and no haggis at the supper, and no one
looked like Helen, the Lady of the Lake, and
Patron Normand MacLaurin didn’t look a bit
Fitzjamesy. One there was of noble bearing and
tartan_ (and black knees) who might have been
Roderick Dhu. Presently he glanced up at the
gallery and displayed a truly Cockney counten-
ance.
So I went away to see the last of “ Sweet Nell
of Old Drury.” What a hairy period was hers !
We are sorry to see the last of those lovely wigs,
flaxen, gold, auburn, and red. Harcourt Beatty’s
Charles II has mellowed to the acme of what we
think the gay, kind, careless, dignified king ought
to have been. The swoon of Lady Olivia (Miss
Minnie Vernon) remains one of the best stage
faints I ever saw. She drops like a feather
cushion. We said good-bye regretfully to that
beautiful stage setting—the room in which Lord
Jeffreys dispenses injustice, so softly red and redly
soft it is, until the lighted tapers send bars of
amber athwart its floor. Its stained windows, oak
panelling, armour suits, and real old oak furniture,
make it a, room for inspiration. As for Miss
Stewart, she has the tenacity of a beaver. For
the last fortnight she has been really ill of influenza
and yet. she played with undimmed brightness
Friday’s two performances. It was in a very
broken voice she spoke a few farewell words to the
packed enthusiastic audience. The matinee per-
formance was in aid of the poor children of
Sydney and the Maternity Home, and realised
£l5O.
Australian artists, Streeton, Minns, Fullwood,
and Thorpe, all had their pictures rejected by the
Royal Academy. Streeton’s picture, on which
the other artists say he intended to stake his
reputation, is said to be the finest thing he has
done. Spence got in with some small work, but
there’s a difference between hanging a little
picture and a huge one. The Sydney local
art societies, which are said to have amal-
gamated, will hold an Exhibition next September,
but in which set of rooms has not been decided.
If the senior society is wise it won’t select its own
eyry, for only wealthy people buy pictures, and
wealthy people are mostly fat and won’t walk up
stairs.
A feature of Sydney Orchestral Society’s
fine concert at the Town Hall last Thursday
was the audience’s reception of the magnificent
Wagnerian “Funeral March,” played in memory
of the dead Sydney Moss. When the last note
had died away, and the waving arms of Hazon
were still, one solitary, timid clap was heard,
and then a silence that was tremendous in
its intensity as the crowded house waited with
bated breath, not knowing that the end had
come. When it woke from its reverie Hazon had
gone, and Morley was coming back to the piano,
and the first notes of Litolff’s Scherzo for piano
and orchestra were sounding down the crowded
galleries.
Mrs. John Jaquet, wife of the IST. S. W. assistant
Government Geologist, died at Petty’s Hotel
early on Saturday morning, aged 21 years. She
had, sad to relate, been married but a few months
and was ill only a week. Mrs. Jaquet was a
daughter of Mr. Hugh Campbell, of Belaringa
Station, and sister to Dr. Alfred and Dr. Robert
Campbell.
The ball in aid of St. Margaret’s Maternity
Home takes place next Tuesday, July 29.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The marriage of Speaker Mason’s daughter
with an Amsterdam gentleman, Mr, Van Cooth,
was the occasion of a large corroboree last
Wednesday. The marriage vows were breathed
at the side altar of St. Patrick’s Cathedral—the
centre one being under repair. Onlookers crowded
guests to the back. Three clergymen gave an
extra-tight tug to the true lovers’ knot. After
they had finished, Archbishop Carr added to his
blessing some pearls of wisdom as to why and
wherefore wives should honor and obey their
hubbies, and so on. The guests fidgetted incon-
tinently, with vulgar visions of a delayed recep-
tion._ There was more dallying at the registry-
signing than usual, the Archbishop taking Gov.
Clarice in tow to see the wonderful Mosaic
altar that he takes so much pride in. Miss
Marie Mason, an unaffected, charming girl,, made
a fair picture as a bride, her raiment running
to soft pearl-toned silk, with borderings of
applique, chiffon ruchings, and good point-lace
additions—a simple toilette, losing nothing for an
absence of hyperbolous decoration.
“There was no barring of St. Patrick’s Cathedral
against any would-be worshipper at the marriage
of Speaker Mason’s daughter. Tramps and blind
beggars strayed in to pray or snooze, with their
dogs. One tyke, a grimy fox-terrier, jumped into
the laps of fashionable guests till the joke lost its
freshness. Then he called three other curs to
mount the scaffolding in his company so as to
get a better view of the hitching-on. The
quartette got into trouble up there and were
chased, but came to the front again when a matted
poodle jumped on the train of the new Mrs. Van
Cooth as she swept down the aisle on hubby’s arm.
Someone threw a brolly at the brute, and gamp
and pup travelled some paces on the snowy
draperies, the bride fancying, perhaps, that it was
her new dignity which weighed her down so
heavily.
“Gov. Clarke gave a solemn dissertation on the
estate of ‘ mixed couples ’ at the wedding-banquet
in the Exhibition. He delivered this marriage
homily in his best public manner, was quite fluent,
sounded no sentiments, you know, but voiced
some solid every-day truths in good style. But
the groom made the hit of the day with his pithy
speech, which abounded in humor. He infused
some foreign grace into a ceremony that John
Bull ever makes a bore. The conventional
wedding-toast is probably the dreariest thing on
earth.
“ Mrs. (Premier) Irvine looked well in blue at
the Mason wedding. One of the most graceful
girls on hand was a willowy Melbourne niece of
Roly-Poly Reid—whose junior relatives, fortun-
ately, don’t resemble Nunky in figure. Mrs.
Butler, in a furry, black coat, and all her mass of
flaxen hair piled up as usual, brought along quiet,
elderly hubby to behold the matrimonial sacrifice.
“ The city had sleep still in its eyes when Gov.
Tennyson and his life-partner arrived on Thursday
morning. A tarpaulined garden on the platform,
with ‘ Welcome ’ signs writ above and about in
flaming red letters, greeted their view as the
Adelaide train steamed in to the Spencer-st.
platform, at the very spot where. Gov.-Gen. Hope-
toun and some ancient sinners had wept a fort-
night before. The square heavy face of the poet’s
son remained impassive, while he distributed
mandarin nods along the road. But the amiable
and perky lady by his side sat forward and nearly
nodded her head off.
“A staid assemblage of social and political big-
wigs sat round the Queen’s Hall in a circle,
waiting for the oath and the chosen chief to
arrive. To a clanking accompaniment of spurs
and swords, Gov. Tennyson solemnly walked bn
and took his place on the dais, with the statued
Queen looking down upon his shining crown-piece.
The most surprising part of the ceremony was the
vigorous and impassioned delivery of the newly-
elected deputy G.G. The speech took on wonder-
fully, the tired air of theearly gathering disappeared.
Society, rather repelled of old by the Sphinx-like
Tennyson during his occasional visits to this side,
went away with a big opinion of the person who
could throw a fair muster of good ideas off his
chest in such rousing style.
“Did you notice in Lord Hopetoun’s photo., in
last week’s Bulletin, how wondrously his over-
coat is built? That is the very latest freak of
the smart tailor, to dispense with arm-hole seams
altogether, and, in some occult way, to cut sleeve
and body in one piece, like the Holy Coat of
Treves, joining the top of the sleeve in a stitched
seam running right up into the collar. An Aus-
tralian man, lately returned from London, says
that the first man he saw with such a garment on
was Mr. Chamberlain, so it must be all right.
“Young Frank Clarke’s son (born at Oxford,
where boyish pa pursues his studies) is the late
Baronet Clarke’s grandson, and so far the heir,
provided by the third generation, to the baronetcy!
S’Rupert has no sons ; his brother Ernest and his
stepbrother Russell are, as yet, unmarried. Baby’s
mamma was formerly Miss Cotton, of Daylesford,
a cousin of dowager Janet.
“An exodus to India’s coral strand is com-
mencing. One of the Melbourne girls who was
wedded there this year gives glowing accounts of
the preponderance of the marrying man in that
golden land. She invites her friends to come, and
pray for judgment meanwhile, for their choice
will be stiffer than ever was that of Paris with his
three willing goddesses on the counter. Her rosy
views seems a bit De Rougemontish, but her own
prosperous match rather gives some foundation to
her rallying cry. Next month the exodus com-
mences in real earnest, when a party of charmers
sets out. And others, dolefully contemplating
their arid prospects in Melbourne, will follow suit.
“The other night, at ‘The Chinese Honey-
moon,’ Better-Not Wallington sat with three
English new-chums. Wally had his glasses most
of the night focussed on the four Pineapple brides-
maids, and he laughed all round the ears at Fi
Fi’s digs at the haristocracy. Wally was jovial.
He had got Hopetoun off the mark, and kicked *
free leg.
“One couldn’t sample ‘The 0. Honeymoon’
any night without confronting Mrs. Norman
Menzies (Dorothy Vane) in box or circle. Mrs.
Menzies avowedly loves May Beatty, and watches
for her new business, her fresh smudges, and her
latest wiles with an appreciative eye. The ex-
actress declares that she loves the theatre more
and more every day, but that she wouldn’t be
back on the boards for even a Melba boom. But,
then, nobody quite believes what an ex-actress
says about her Lost Paradise.
“Miss Maud Hartington, who played the
Princess in ‘ Aladdin, ’ and won some of the laurels
of the show, has retired to private life, resumed
her old name, her family ties, and talks
with pathos of her trials and tribulations
in stageland. Miss Jenkins found the theatrical
life irksome in the extreme, the moving of
goods and chattels a frightful fag, and applause
and such, poor reward for the amount of work
and nerve strain involved. The bizarre ways of
the stage were peculiarly trying to a sensitive
little novice, not long from her desk at the
Presbyterian Ladies’College and the Wiedermann
Conservatorium ranks. She cut herself adrift
from a stage career after the ‘ Aladdin ’ run, and
now shines modestly in her mother’s circle.
“The Victorian artists recognise that the pub-
lic’s taste for art has to be tickled with musical
morsels and jam sandwich, so sing-songs have
been switched on to the present exhibition, and
amateurs tootle while visitors meander around the
pictures. Conspicuous at almost all the receptions
is Pastor Bevan. That divine is so constantly
piloting people over the Gallery where his portrait
hangs, that he probably pitched his tent in the
district for the Exhibition. The good man’s vanity
would awaken the laughter of an owl. He can’t
keep away from that luminous sketch of himself
in oils. He squints at it ever and anon, and
during one concert, when he had to sit in an
adjoining gallery, took a position which com-
manded a view of his other sell.
“The eldest daughter of ‘Napoleon ’ Fitzgerald
has a pretty Sandringham scene in this year’s
exhibition. Society credits the industrious girl
with all the virtues of a saint. Her domestic
qualities run into a lengthy list. And domestic
qualities are becoming fashionable. The smart
girl of the day finds that lovers look askance on
extravagant living. t
“The immense crowd which welcomed the
‘Circus Girl’ to Her Majesty’s (Saturday,
19th July), now label said stranger as
‘not the sort of girl they care about. Ken
ningham gets no singing, Hugh Ward no
dancing, and Florence Young no songs worth her
mettle. Blanche Wallace, as an English Some-
body in Paris, has a streak of luck in her
two costumes. The first—a grey walking
toilet —recalls a silver cloud with a rosy
lining. Sparkling bands, like interludes of rain-
drops, glisten on its surface of grey panne. It
would be a sartorial ‘ symphonie pathetique ’
only for blushing under-frills of coral color. A
surprise band of same cheerful hue intrudes
into the hat reverie—a Puritan poem, based
on plumes of dove-color and white. Blanche’s
othe- toilette draws its inspiration from pea
cocks rather than doves a princess robe of
dark-green velvet has an Empire front of glitter-
ing device like peacock-feather ‘eyes.’ Masses of
green-spangled gauze foam round wearer’s feet and
wrists, like a frenzy of jealousy, swirling to the
surface in spite of j the soft persuasions of the
fleecy cloak of cool primrose, vainly trying to
allay the emerald fury seething below.
“Elsie Moore, like a Watteau shepherdess in
pink, snapped most of the presentation flowers,
thanks to the loyalty of ex-colleagues of the
chorus, who, now havened in gilded married life,
bestow tributes on their old chum, just to show
some of the slie-principals that ‘the Public some-
times differs from the Management in its estimate
of a girl’s ‘ form. *
“A magnate’s wife gave the circle a scare.
In the distance she seemed to be wearing only
the coat of nature, but on a prolonged inspec-
tion through strong glasses, it was found that a
flesh-colored silk and lace gown was pasted in
smooth fashion over her buxom figure. Two ex-
mummers and their elderly hubbies, another
ex-mummer and her ‘fiasco,’ and a racing mag-
nate’s wife brought down the glasses of galleries
and circle on their respective heads.
“The steeplechase ‘Follow the Man from
Cook’s was the most exciting feature of the amateur
‘ Runaway Girl ’ show. The highly-proper Lady
Coodle scorned thehurdle-clearingand disappeared
for that high-jumping turn. The other girls showed
nimbler legs and a greater cascade of petticoat
laces than even the professionals did. The pretty
chorus girls were more rakish than any old stage
hands, and carried on lively communications with
the Johnnies in the audience.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Next to the Insurance fiend ranks the
Typewriter man for cast iron nerve and per-
severance. A man has only to breathe that he is
going in for a machine when the salesmen swarm
round him like flies over beef. Brown will tell
him that a machine printing with a shift for
capital letters is only used by persons with weak
intellects, and that if he buys a double keyboard
instrument his troubles are for ever at rest.
Smith, however, scoffs at the double keyboard
notion and prophesies disaster of the most awful
description to the unlucky possessor. Then up
comes Green with a pad machine and anathema-
tizes all machines printing from a ribbon. The
bewildered buyer, driven to distraction, curses each
and all. The safe and comfortable course is to deal
with a house offering a variety of each principle ;
and anxious to give the buyer the type of machine
best suited to his special wants. The United
Typewriter and Supplies Co. of Sydney,
Melbourne and Perth sells the Yost, New Century
and Densmore, shift key, double keyboard and
pad machine, and they give their advice free, and
also a free trial of each kind of machine.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Peacock makes the third knight now sitting
in Vic. Assembly. The others are doddering
“Jock” Mclntyre and the hopelessly conserva-
tive Gillott.
Died, at Melbourne, Miss Elphinstone
Dick, the eminent gymnastic teacher for ladies.
She once swam six miles, in a rough sea, off the
English coast.
N.S.W. ecclesiastical aprons attract some
of Queensland’s best men. Barlow, Stanton and
Stretch did duty “up above” but leaped when
N.S.W. called.
Looking at N.S.W. Treasurer Waddell
now no one would suppose that seven years ago he
fathered a Bill, all by himself, to abolish the
pensions of Judges of the Supreme and District
Courts.
Lord Hopetocm has not entirely left
Australia. Some of him still remains—in the
shape of several formidable corns from which he
was divorced by the eminent Sydney specialist
Barclay.
Acting-Premier Deakin is vastly proud of
his eloquence and takes much pains to polish it
up in Hansard proof. Said that to correct the
proofs of his speeches often costs more than the
original setting-up of the matter.
A certain prominent N.S.W. squatter,
whose chances of “ ennobblement ” looked more
or less rosy a few months ago, has so altered since
the Coronation-list was published that his friends
hardly recognise him at a first glance.
Woollahra (N.S.W.) Presbyterian Church
is “calling” Rev. Dr. Meharry, the actor-
preacher of London, at a salary of £lOOO. Dr.
M. s present stipend is £lOOO with quarters, but
he desires a change to Australia, of which he
became enamoured.
Twenty-six out ot the 95 present members
of Vic. Assembly have held office as Ministers, a
very fair proof of the mediocrity of Govts, in that
State. Also, Turner, Best, Isaacs, M‘Lean,
Salmon and M‘Coll (in Federal politics) were
former Vic. Ministers.
David Syme has no further use for Kip-
ling and Tommy Atkins now the war is over.
Atkins is off, and Kipling is abased as the
apotheosis of blatant swagger. David reviles him
a whole column long for exhibiting the “ volcanic
braggadocio of his own soul.”
Apropos George Reid’s systematic San-
dowing for his stomach’s sake : The craze for
physical culture has taken on among Sydney’s
fat men ; and dropping in at one of the “parlors”
where the system is taught, you’d think the
Legislative Council was in session.
Admiral Sir Henry Keppel (who, as a
larky post-captain, caused the 30 years’ estrange-
ment between Hori Grey and his wife) has just
returned to England from a journey to Singapore,
made as a director of the British North Borneo
Co., and is hale and hearty at the age of 94.
A SEMI-CONCEALED COMFORTER.
EVANGELIST: “The Bulletin, sir, is a paper
which should never he read.”
THE UNREGENERATE : “I quite agree with you,
sir. If it were not so red it could not he so
plainly seen sticking out of your coat-tail
pocket every Thursday .”
Gravitated to gaol, an old Stawell (Vic.)
identity—the octogenarian Mary Honan, who
made good money as a bullock-driver, carting
provisions to the diggings in the early days. The
Female Penitentiary at Coburg makes a poor
ending for one who has seen, and helped to make,
Australia’s “roaring days.”
“Joey” Bell, newly-elected chairman of
Committees in Q. Assembly, is eldest son of the
late Joshua Peter Bell, who owned Jimbour (the
show-station of the Darling Downs) in the “good
old” squatting days. “ Joey ” went to Cambridge,
years back, and came back with a degree, and a
numnah and an accent. Both he and his brother
William are barristers, and, when at Alma-mater,
rowed in the champion eights.
“ Rit ” : “ Van D.” speaks of a clergyman
he once knew in Tasmania as being the original of
Thackeray’s Revd. Chas. Honeyman in “ Pen-
dennis.” There is better authority for the state-
ment that it was the father of Kyrle Bellew
(teacher of elocution to King Edward 40 or 50
years ago) who sat for the portrait.
Mrs. Lance Rawson accuses Rev. Dill
Macky (high priest of N.S.W. Orangeism), in the
columns of John Norton’s paper, of believing, in
common with all Christians, in the Immaculate
Conception.” John, who is “a High Church
Anglican ” with a deep knowledge of Saints’
Days, ought to know better than that.
George Black, who hasn’t had any love for
A. G. Stephens since that little Burns squabble,
devotes a column or more to slating A.G.S’s.
“Oblation” in last week’s Worker. It con-
cludes :
The pamphlet is dedicated to the late Brunton
Stephens. He did not long survive the insult.
Lord Salisbury, when he was, in a sense, a
“ struggling ” journalist living in a poor quarter
of London, “ sat under ” Rev. James Moorhouse,
afterwards Bishop of Melbourne. When Salis-
bury formed his first Government one of his
eagerly-seized opportunities was to promote
Bishop Moorhouse from Melbourne to Man-
chester.
S.A. Leg. Council is annoyed because ex-
President Baker, for whose portrait it paid, posed
to the artist in a wig and a resplendent “get-up”
such as was never yet seen in the Chamber, where
presidents do not don horse-hair or knee-breeches.
Attorney-General Gordon has appealed to the
artist to paint out the wig but the result is not
yet known.
Queensland Speaker Arthur Morgan
(elected unopposed for the second time) is editor
and proprietor of Warwick Argus. Both
sides testified to Morgan’s fairmindedness and
lack of bias—which must have been gall and worm-
wood to ex-Speaker Cowley. Morgan’s little
brother “ Wally ” is probably the biggest man in
Q. —Oft. high and 28st. in weight.
There are rumors once more that ex-
Premier Peacock is “full up” of Vic. politics.
His mine-managing business cannot expand, and
his better half entreats. But then, the meteorio
Treasurer Shiels is also believed to have been
domestically implored for many years to quit
politics, and his well-advertised malady persuades
him most persistently, yet he grip ly hangs on.
Appointment of Rev. G. H. Frod-
sham, M.A. (of Durham University), as Bishop
of North Queensland, is another victory for the
High Churchites in England, who regard Queens-
land as a good field in which to plant young
adherents. Mr. Frodsham was, in 1896, sent to
Brisbane as incumbent of St. Thomas’, South
Toowong, and secretary to the Bishop. Amongst
the Low Church clergy, of whom there is but a
handful in Brisbane, he is known, because of his
undecided manner, as “Flotsam and Jetsam.”
Alex. Montgomery, the Maorilander, who
goes over to be Westralia’s first State Mining
Engineer, is a good sample of
the Southern Scot. He was
one of the sort that had to take
a degree if he did it on por-
ridge ; as it happened, he was
able to do it on scholarships,
and, no doubt, found that more
comfortable. He was B.A. at
19, and M.A. at 20 ; but as the
Arts course couldn’t keep the
greedy Otago youngster going,
he filled in with metallurgy
and engineering. Afterwards,
having done a little railway
work, he was drafted out with
Prof. Black to organise the
M.L. Schools of Mines. He
was Govt. Geologist of Tas-
mania when Zeehan and Mt.
Lyell were opened, but got
back to M.L. and mine-manage-
ment in ’96. On top of all his
scholastic and scientific attain-
ments Mr. Montgomery is a
good athlete and a mighty
walker ; and when he was in
office in Tasmania he didn’t
keep a frill on the premises.
Old Dr. Nicholson is en-
thusiastic over photos, of a
statue in the Nicholson Museum
in Sydney University. The
statue is that of the wife of
Tirhakah, an Egyptian ruler
whose name figures in the Old
Testament. The nonagenarian
baronet lives a great part of
his time in memories of his
museum.
“Melb.” : Prof. Mudd, F.R.G.S., lectur-
ing here, states that he accompanied King Edward
as botanist, on the Indian tour. He says that a
jeweller named Isaacs was in the suite, his busi-
ness being to value all the gifts. Isaacs had
charge of them, in all kinds of rubbishy recep-
tacles, as if they were things of no value. He
showed Mudd jewellery to the value of £20,000,
in an old kerosene-tin.
“How they apologise,” according to Dan
O’Connor, in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly:—
The O’Connor : Every man has that right (the right of
addressing the House) no matter, Mr. Chairman, whether
he is as courteous as yourself, or as rude and brutal as
the member for Monaro.
The surprised and outraged Miller : “??????”
The O’Connor : I apologise, Mr. Chairman. I apologise
for the honor of the House, and for the dignity of an
ignoramus. I fail to notice any insignificant interjection
from any little creatures who depend mostly on the
length of their hair, or the strength of their voices.
Which apparently quite mollified the ruffled
Miller.
A SUSPICIOUS CIRCUMSTANCE.
“ What made you think I was a married man? ”
“ Well, last night you seemed in such a hurry to
get home.”
Wagga’s retiring Police Magistrate, Mr.
George Martin, is a brother of the late burly
N.S.W. Chief Justice. Mr. Martin was ten years
younger than his eminent brother, and picked up
no little of what law he administered by following
his brother’s career. The ex-P.M. is a N.S.W.
native, and filled several Crown billets ere going
on to the Bench. He was early identified with
the Vernon training-ship for boys.
Among nil the professions of weepful
regret at his departure, the testimonial given to
Hopetoun by the Melbourne unemployed stands
out bold and unconstrained. They resolved :
“ That, no matter how many divergent views we
hold as Royalists, Republicans, or Socialists, we,
the unemployed, all unite in agreeing that the
late Governor-General is a Man ; and as we have
known him as such, we give him three hearty
cheers.”
A female ex-convict has just died in
Northern N.S.W., aged nearly ninety. She was
sent out labelled “ noted garotter ” and took great
pride in her early profession, explaining that a
love of garotting, once acquired, grew with indul-
gence. “I long for the feel of a good throat
betwixt my fingers and thumbs,” she used to
say in her cups. She could not read, but treas-
ured her deed of pardon, asking that it might be
placed on her breast when in her coffin.
Someone who has been analysing Debrett
finds that Oliver Cromwell stands as ancestor to
more British peers than Charles IT ! This is not
due, however, to any miscellaneous affection on
Oliver’s part; there must have been something in
the blood to help on so many of his breeding to
earn titles for public service rendered. Anyway,
there are two marquises, eight earls (Ivo Bligh
among them), and five barons, all claiming descent
from the Protector, and all through his fourth
daughter.
Captain Miles, of Strahan Marine Board
celebrity, the kinked link that condemned
Braddon’s chain of Tas. Ministerial office, is back
in his old haunts, and bids fair to again loom
large in V.D.L. affairs. Strahan Chamber of
Commerce unanimously elected him a member,
t’other night, and the odds are not too long about
him once more securing the seat on the Marine
Board, from which he fell with such velocity a

From an Australian in the Transvaal about
Steyn (who, through the charity of the women of
Capetown, is now going to Europe for medical
treatment): —
Poor Steyn is a wreck. He is almost blind, and is sup-
posed to be suffering from sunstroke. lam afraid this is
only a charitable interpretation of something which the
strain of the last few years has brought about. When I
saw him nearly three years ago he was in the full flush of
his manhood, passionately addressing an assemblage of
burghers. I saw him a few days ago being led about by
an assistant and apparently helpless.
So Kipling has refused a knighthood, or
something of that kind. Was it in anticipation
of some such offer that he wrote his “Last
Rhyme of True Thomas ? ” It will be remembered
that the King wanted to make a knight of True
Thomas “ all for the sake of the songs he made.”
and Thomas indignantly answered :
I ha’ harpit ye up to the throne o’ God,
I ha’ harpit your midmost soul in three ;
I ha’ harpit ye down to the hinges o’ Hell,
And ye would make a knight o’ me 1 ”
The only Victorian Premiers who ever
showed any recognition of literary merit were
John O’Shanassy and C. Gavan Duffy. Big John
made James Smith Parliamentary librarian, and
Finn (“ Garryowen ”) clerk of the Parliamentary
papers. Duffy appointed Marcus Clarke sub-
librarian at Melb. Public Library. Succeeding
Vic. Premiers were not able to see any points
about scribblers. Smith was sent adrift by the
M‘Culloch Govt., and his place was eventually
taken by the late Jas. Farrell, ex-publican and
M.L. A.
London papers are full just now of stories
to show what a good fellow Lord Hopetoun is.
A sample :
His friends well remember a delightful incident which
occurred at Government House late one night after a
rather unusually dull and formal State entertainment.
The official guests had departed, and the Governor, still '
arrayed in full uniform, relieved his feelings and delighted
his house party by walking round the big ball-room on
his hands with his feet in the air!
The meanness of Australia in refusing to double
the salary of a Governor-General who could walk
on his hands !
Australia lost its best woman-preacher the
other day—in S. Aus., of course, where every
third man is either a local preacher or a J.P., and
often both. She was Mrs. Lake, wife of the Bible
Christians’ minister, and grand-daughter of the
founder of that offshoot of the Methodists. The
other Methodist bodies didn’t take kindly to
women-preachers (Wesley’s own experiences of
women were not too pleasant) ; but the Bible
Christians put all it could into the pulpit. This
one had “travelled” Queensland, N.S.W., and
S. Australia.
Howard Spensley (whose eldest daughter
has become engaged in England to her cousin,
Capt. S. T. Staughton, M.L.A., Vic.), was a
Melb. pressman— Herald —in the sixties. He
was afterwards admitted to the Bar, and was
Solicitor-General in the Duffy Govt. When
returned to Vic. Leg. Assembly for Warrnambool,
he beat his opponent, Richmond Henty, by five
votes. By a singular coincidence, after he had
taken up residence in England, his election to
the Commons for a London electorate was
secured by a majority of five votes.
Mary Gilmore writes from Fremantle,
W.A. (on board the G.M.S. Karlsruhe) about
Henry Lawson, who has just returned to Sydney:
Henry Lawson is with us. We went down from Liver-
pool and stayed 10 days with him and his wife in London.
He was looking fagged and ill, and sent Mrs. Lawson and
the children out with us. He stayed behind to go over
the proofs of his last book—said to be his best. While in
London, I saw the manager for Blackwood’s. He told me
it would be madness for Lawson to leave London for a
year or so. “He has the ball at his feet, and there’s
nothing to stop him,” he said. A big pubiishing-agent
told me the same thing. However, as far as I can see,
judging by the opinions 1 heard and the quality of the
reviews, it doesn’t matter where he does his work, whether
in the Sahara of London or any other Sahara. It will go
on his name, let alone on its merits. He came on by the
Gera, and, owing to our having gone to Bombay for re-
pairs, landed at Colombo, and caught us as we re-passed.
“I had about twenty minutes’conversa-
tion with De Wet,” writes a Victorian living at
Vereeniging, the scene of the Peace Conference.
“As I looked into his face, I tried to read what
there was in him to make him such a successful
man. Except for the fact that he is far and away
above the average in intelligence there is nothing
to distinguish him from the ordinary habitue of
Flemington sale-yards. He was a very successful
farmer, and the man who can be that can easily be
a successful soldier. . . . When DeWet was
leaving I endeavored to express the pleasure I felt
in meeting him, and (as I felt, clumsily) tried to
pay him the compliment which everybody will
admit is his due. He laid his hand on my shoulder
and said: ‘ I may never see you any more. I sup-
pose you’ll hear a lot said against me—you will
hear a lot said in my favor—l care nothing for
either ; but I am satisfied in the knowledge that
I have done to the end what I considered right in
the interests of my people who put their trust in
me. That end has now come—so good-bye, and
God bless us all! ’

“ 6x8”: Be drovers’dogs. I must em-
phatically contradict “ Cyniphilos ” (B. 5/7/’02) I
have had much droving experience with both sheep
and cattle and with all varieties of drover and I
have found them nearly always kind to their does
Boss-drovers, in fact, treat good dogs with much
more consideration than their wage-shepherds I
once went with “Old Riley ” from Carinda on
Merri-Merri Creek, N.8.W.. to Gunnedah, via
Combolong and Come-by-Chance, behind a 9000
mob of Warren Downs sheep. Riley’s favorite
dog knocked-up after a few days out, and the old
drover carried the animal on his saddle for over a
week, and at midday shared his lunch with him.
l Why not leave him with the cook’s
cart? Wouldnt trust the cook,” was the
answer; he wouldn’t look after him well
enough. ’ This is one of many instances of the
kind which have come under my personal notice.
Ihe only dogs I’ve known drovers to be unkind to
were wastrels, “sooner-dogs,” not worth tucker,
and such animals are annoying on the road.

“F.M.” : The way all trees grow, from
the crown upwards or outwards, was settled
long ago. Anybody may test this by stapling a
wire to a line of growing saplings and seeing if it
lifts as they grow. And yet there are exceptions
and puzzles. One of the strangest is a red-gum
slab which marks a grave at Yerong, Yarra Flats
Vic. It is gripped and held by the bark of a
willow, and stands about 4ft. from ground. It
has been in the same position for more than 30
years. The history of the tree and slab is queer,
and runs back nearly 70 years, to the time when
the first Victorians settled about the Upper
Yarra. It was Macpherson, of Heidelberg, I
think, who planted a willow-twig from St.
Helena, and a few years later his stockman yarned
with a girl at Yerong and carried a switch cut
from the Heidelberg willow. They stuck the
switch in the ground to commemorate something
or other, and it grew. The stockman married the
girl, and 20 years later, died and was buried under
the tree he had planted. What followed is what
appears to-day. There is no evidence of human
agency in the matter. The big folds of the willow
bark are right over the edges of the slab, which
the tree has carried up, and the lettering on the
part visible is as plain as the day it was cut.
* * « *
“ Albo ” ; Be “Hippo’s” notion that
trees do not carry marks upwards with their
growth. Let “H.” find a sapling with a fork
say 5ft. up, formed by two limbs of about equal
size, and let him bore a hole through each limb
just above the junction. Then, when, in course
of time, this sapling has grown so that the fork is
10ft. from the ground, would “H.” searcli the
trunk 5ft. from the ground for these holes or
would he try higher up ? Not till “ H.” manages
to convince me that trees grow higher without
growing upwards shall I believe that marks
thereon remain stationary.

A i'>rize item at a Coronation bun-struggle
pro session at Rochester (Vic.) recently was a
tableau, entitled “The Starved-out Mallee Far-
mer.” Upon a crazy old dray, drawn by a
wretched neddy scarcely able to crawl, appeared
the aforesaid farmer and his wife, both gnawing
at a shin-bone, while the “ woman ” crooned a
lullaby to her stuffed baby. It was a grim com-
ment on the prevailing sad state of rural affairs,
yet a crowd of cockies laughed uproariously at
the presentment of a condition not far removed
from their own. Strange animal, the Vic. cockie !

“ Vul.” : Be recent Bulletin items about
what cows will eat. From “ The Life of Millais,”
the artist:—
Mr. Gould, the naturalist, when out fishing one day
landed a fine salmon, which he concealed behind a small
bush in a meadow. When he came back to look for his
fish it was nowhere to be found, and after a lono- and
unsuccessful search he began to think the keepers had
purloined it. They, however, laid the blame on the cows
suggesting that they had eaten it. The idea was scoffed
at by every sportsman in the house, and to prove its
absurdity, a fresh salmon was brought from the larder
and put into the same field, when, to the astonishment of
the scoffers, the cows promptly marched up and devoured
it. Also, not generally known that fish of any kind is
readily eaten by all ruminants.

“Rizpah ’’ : Re par. (B. 21/6/’O2) :
The Oentralian drought is a recurrent phenomenon,
much as are our great coastal floods. In '63 flood-time I
arrived in Bowen just as news came of the Burdekin
flood, 17 miles wide at Clare, and the obliteration of the
Burdekin landing—a cargo-receiving and discharging
outlet for N.Q.
That flood has not repeated itself, but it will do so, on
a much larger scale, for the river has silted up greatly,
owing to the heavy stocking of itself and its watershed,
and the lessening of the channel’s depth has caused the
wearing away of the banks. When the Suttor and its
watershed, the Clark and its watershed, and the heads of
the Burdekin become flooded simultaneously—good-bye
to Charters Towers iron railway-bridge, Clare township,
and all sugar-planting Ayr !
When the drought ends the floods begin, and they will
wind up with a fresh-water sea from the Serpentine to
Euri Creek—loo miles—and everything on that strip of
country will go. Also, take the cases of the Herbert
River and Ingham sugar township (low lying); the
Fitzroy and its watershed and Rockhampton (very low
lying) ; the Mary and its watershed and Gympie, Mary-
borough ; the Brisbane River, &c. This last has already
shown what it can do, and the repeat will about settle the
low-lying portions of Brisbane. Over 40 years ago I
heard the Brisbane blacks speak of great prior floods, and
it is evident, upon examination, that the business,
governmental, and botanical part of the city is but a low
second bank, the true one being Wickham Terrace.
t Curious how long-continued immunity begets that
“ tired feeling” that will not move from danger’s circle.
In the case of the sugar towns, it is not hard to foresee
that Nature’s action in the near future will effectually
solve the colored-labor question. Rockhampton, Mary-
borough, and Brisbane are excellent places of abode for
intending martyrs; and, if we can’t be taught in any
other way, a criminal waste of life and goods must be our
instructor.

Aborigines-mission reports read worse and
worse; in one sense perhaps better, for black is
melting into white. Long ago Rev. Hamdt, a
German missionary in Brisbane gaol, was chal-
lenged to convert a blackfellow. A grant of land
was the Govt, prize for every black turned
to Christian ways. Hamdt was an honest man as
well as being a clergyman, and though he wrought
for years he never applied for a single grant.

“P.C.C.”: After the trees on soil of
Silurian formation have been ringbarked “ water-
gushes ” make their appearance on
the weather side of the hills, and
gullies often wash out into small
creeks, thus showing that there is
more water about the ground than
there previously was. This, because—
though the same amount may have
fallen before ringbarking—the roots
of the trees sucked much of it away.
The supposition that trees draw rain
is a confusion between cause andeffeet.
Trees indicate rain because trees are
the result—not the cause thereof.
Droughts prove nothing. [But what
has this cocksure correspondent to
say to the abundantly-demonstrated
fact that forest-denudation is invari-
ably followed by diminution of rain-
fall ? If the rain is merely the cause
of the trees, it should not be affected
by elimination of its own effect .]

would be to risk detection. A prize Jersey heifer

of Young. Outback the whole beast is generally
carried off.

Prof. Gregory, of Mel-
bourne, pours cold water on the half-century-old
project of fi ling the Lake Eyre basin from the sea
at Port Augusta with the object of moistening the
climate of central Australia. He holds that the
cost of a sufficiently-capacious canal would be
about 700 millions sterling, and that the results
might be altogether disappointing when the in-
land sea had been created. S.A. will not borrow
that 700 millions yet awhile.

The Owe’Sullivan lias given notice of a
proposal to build a railway from Cobar to
Wilcannia (N.S.W.). After leaving Cobar it will
pass through three counties—Werunda (with 87
people,) Rankin (population, 150,) and Booroon-
dara, (population, 165.) There are 90 inhabited
huts in these three counties, two of which will
probably be visible from the railway, and thus
help to brighten the landscape. Finally the
line will touch the county of Young with
1290 people. If it gets the trade of two-
thirds of these people (some of them will
be 60 miles away from the line) there will be 1128
people to support 120 miles of railway—that is the
mileage after Robinson county, in which Cobar
is situated, is left behind. If the line is built and
equipped for £4OOO per mile it will cost £420 per
inhabitant served by it, including the baby, or
over £lOOO for every adult male. There isn’t a
town or the semblance of one in all Booroondara.
Werunda and Rankin counties, and when last
heard of there were only 140,000 sheep and 1700
cattle in all three counties. Picture the possi-
bilities in the way of naming the stations along a
route like that: Bare Plain, Brown Wilderness
Dead Finish, Dry Tank, Sandstorm, Lost Ox’,
Godforgotten (change here for Godforsaken) and
Mount Hopeless are a few that suggest them-
selves.

Writer passed through a N.Q. township
some three months ago, and could discover no
visible means of support in the place beyond some
200 goats. Now the goats have all gone—starved
out, and the surviving population L> subsisting on
what it euphemistically calls “salt mutton.”
Lot a sheep or bullock for 50 miles around.

“0.K.” : Re sorghum-poisoning (“Hag-
gram,” B. 7/8/’O2), “F.M.” (B. 21/6/’O2) says
after eating greedily,” etc. Now, a little unripe
sorghum or planter’s friend will kill a beast, and
immediate dissection shows the paunch to be
slightly distended with foetid gas. Horses and
bulls, though not immune, are not so susceptible
as cows. Glover, when eaten by cattle, generates
a great quantity of inoffensive gas, which distends
the paunch so much that the heart’s action is
interfered with and the beast dies; and I have
frequently found the paunch burst and the
abdominal cavity filled with the gas. Have often
saved a beast by sticking a knife between the ribs
into the paunch, thus releasing the gas. Running
the animal has the same effect, if done in time, but
not so with sorghum-poisoned animals.

“ Tinkan ” : To snare crows—“ Young S.'
(B. 28/6/’O2). “Wii. ” a crow, and peg him down
on his back. In this position he will hang on to
anything that comes his way, and when his friends
arrive to see what lie’s making such a noise about,
he’s sure to get hold of one of them and be “stick-
ing closer than a brother.” Take No. 2 half-a-
mile away, peg down, and get back to No. 1 lively
to relieve him of liis second capture. Then, by
the time you get back to No. 2, he will have
caught his No. 2—and so on till your “peggees”
have caught about six each. After that they lose
their grip, and you’ve got to peg down a fresh
pair.
* * « *
“ Oktis" : “Scotty W.’s ’’ fox (B. 5/7/’O2)
became the property of “ Silly Billy.” Blackstone
lays it down that animals ferce natures become the
property of the first person who secures them—
per industriam. This qualified ownership only
remains while the animals are captive, and unable
to escape, or, becoming domesticated, have the
habit of returning (animum revertendi) to their
possession. Thus the fox in question, being able
to escape, became the property of the first one who
re-captured it (unless the former owner instantly
pursued it on its escape). The fact that it was
making off with “Scotty’s” trap does not alter
the case.
Also, as regards the disappearance of rabbits
after some have been poisoned with phosphorus.
The rabbits do not migrate and return. Hearing
the squeals of their poisoned companions, they lie
in hiding in the burrows, and do not venture out
for days. Have proved this by placing traps over
the burrows. Same thing happens on the advent
of their natural enemy, the fox.

Thus a Casino) N.S. W.) paper :
Over a week ago a Shellharbour paddock fired beneath
the surface, and has been burning ever since. It appears
that while a stump was being burnt the ground caught
fire below, and occasionally shows on the surface. Every
effort to extinguish the fire has been baffled. In several
places the earth has been burned for several feet down,
and as the bulk of these places are covered by only a thin
crust, a series of dangerous pit-falls awaits anyone un-
warily entering the paddock.

“ K.” : N.S.W. Railway Commissioners
profit by the drought at the expense of the
already-distressed men on the land. Some of the
railway gates in the vicinity of Young are con-
tinually left open, encouraging stock in the
neighborhood to stray on to the line in search of a
blade of grass. Trespassers are impounded and
Is. per head damage is charged, besides driving
and other fees.

“ Cassa B.” : lie the case of the Western
district (Vie.) farmers: Mountain ducks, being
unable to scratch, take only the grain lying on the
surface, But the brolga scratches for his grain,
and works sad havoc in newly-sown fields. The
drills now so much used by Vic. farmers are a
blessing to him, as he simply follows up the drill-
marks and finds the wheat lying handy, in lines.
Malleeites coming West will find the brolga not
the least of the new evils facing them, hut they
can, at least, have the satisfaction of killing and
eating the marauder. Many worse dishes than a
young ‘‘ native-companion.”

According to a M.L. paper, “a trapping
competition for a wager of £5 between two
trappers on Earnscleugh Run (Southland), was
won with a tally of 426 rabbits for one night’s
catch. The loser’s tally was 344 rabbits. The
winner beats his previous record of 360 rabbits for
one night.” Earnscleugh is one of the stations
around Lake Wakatipu abandoned entirely to the
rabbits a few years hack. It is yielding more
money as a rabbit-farm for the frozen-rabbit
export trade than it ever did as a sheep station
since bunny came.
* * * •
“ Merool ” : When the Paroo country
was first stocked with sheep, some 20 years ago,
the dingoes, then very numerous, were slow to
discover the choiceness of mutton and did not
interfere with the jumbucks for several months.
“Mick W.” : To “Xerxes ” (B. *l2/7/’O2).
I have often seen the late John Gobi) (long
manager of Bando in Gunnedah district) pick
up the most venomous snakes and crack their
heads off instantly. On the top of Ben Lomond
once, he caught hold of a very big black snake
which nearly beat him. First time of cracking a
large frog flew out of the snake’s mouth, but at
third try the head flew clear. Stewart Wightman,
a well-known Red Creek resident, is also good at
this game—at which I am willing to back him if
“Xerxes” or any other sceptic will put up a fair
stake.
» * * •
“ Bori ’* : We were searching for the
missing man Liinond of Moss Vale, and while
scrambling along a narrow ledge of cliff (1000 feet
high) in the Barrengarry Mountain, one of the
party broke off from an overhanging and appar-
ently solid rock, an ironstone stalactite (of which
there are plenty thereabouts). Immediately a
stream of water fully 6in. in diameter burst out.
Any other occurrence of the kind on record since
the time of the late Mr. Moses-in-the-Wilderness ?

In Sou Queensland the timber is dying
out, in many districts in patches of 20 to 30 acres.
Tiie ironbark on the ridges is perhaps the greatest
sufferer in the vegetable kingdom. Strange to
say, this destruction of timber is not observable
in Central Queensland, where the drought is
raging most fiercely.
• • • *
“Stan. P.” : lie “T. H.M.s” query as
to the correct method of destroying a horse (8.,
22/2/’02): If the pistol-muzzle is placed about
2in. behind the poll, and the shot fired towards
the centre of the forehead, the horse will be in-
stantly killed. Have seen many thus destroyed,
and in no case was a second shot required.
* * * *
Young Australia isn’t always leaning up
against a post waiting for a loan. An infant of 6
—no less—who had thoughtfully burnt-off some
tree-limbs that had fallen across the road, lately
sent in to Whittlesea (Vic.) Shire Council a
claim for payment, which was duly admitted.
That youngster should die a millionaire !

J. S. Horsfall, a brainy though unassertive
squatter, stores a little fodder down in Eastern
Liverina. It was not necessary to buy much in
Moinalong, he writes, as there were 250 tons of
hay left from last year’s crop. If he had ever
attempted squatting beyond the 20-inch average
rainfall, there would certainly have been another
nought on to that sum.' S. M’Caughey, r>f Coo-
nong, too, is never without a good few hundred
tons of hay.
TO HER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TUST a line to greet you
In a distant place ;
Just a word to bring you
Thoughts of someone’s face :
Someone who stood watching
One bleak afternoon,
When the vessel bore you
Out of sight so soon.
Just a word to tell you
Someone’s days are gray,
Someone’s nights are lonely
Since you went away ;
Someone sadly wailing,
Someone winter-cold,
Hopes your nights are starlit,
Days all blue and gold.
Someone, wildly longing,
Hopeful waits for you,
For with your home-coming
Gray skies turn to blue.
Someone’s drawn a picture
From its hiding place,
Someone’s fondly kissed it
With a wistful face.
Someone writes these few lines
Just his thoughts to tell,
Knowing you loill read them
Understanding well.
*1 LB.
R. K. DEE.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned an
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
Agnostic : The Bulletin does not fear the
churches, but is much amused at the whole sectarian
row. All clerical ascendancy it regards as poisonous.
Orangeism is an excellent antidote to R.O. political domi-
nation, and R.0.-ism helps to keep the opposition crowd
in its proper place leonoclastes : Take a terser pen-
name, and don’t be so beastly long-winded.. Also, so
remorseless and bumptious a critic as yourself should be
able to spell better than “amerous” and “humerous.”

to get a living when they go' to work Genung :

in the New Testament to mean the grave. It never liter-
ally meant hell in the Christian sense Awakimo : Both
well-written, bat the prose-sketch hasn’t enough in it
Logos; No: “post meridiem” is correct, “meridiem”
being the accusative of “ meridies”—of which “meridian”

position of a Prince Consort is net that of an ordinary
husband, and his name is not necessarily given, to his
wife’s children. Present Prince-Consort of Holland holds
his position for only five years ; if the Queen has not
had progeny by then the marriage is dissolvable

A Vic. Art Show.
Ten years hence the annual winter exhibition of
the Vie. Artists’ Society should seem quite in
keeping with its domicile in Albert-st., Mel-
bourne. Victorian Art comes along steadily,
not expecting much encouragement on the way,
nor getting any to speak of. The efforts of 120
various men, women and, casual girls are just now
hanging in the V. A.S. galleries for all they are
worth, and many of these exhibitors ought not to
be warned off the premises. Indeed the propor-
tion of preposterous pictures is inconsiderable.
A hundred others have something of the art that
cheers but does not inebriate. A few can be
honestly admired by people who despise the cant
of the craft.
* * * *
Because the subject is popular, and the artist is
a new arrival and a valuable acquisition, Signor
Nerli’s portrait of Pastor Bevan has been unani-
mously elected the “star” picture this year. A
three-quarter-length figure, standing book in hand,
the pose not stiff or stagey, the face a striking
likeness of the pastor as he would be, if he were
a little more thought-worn and not quite so well
fed. A severely flattering porti’ait, calculated to
lift the garrulous Doc. out of his Freetrade
fallacies. John Forde Paterson shows at his
best and his second-best in two pictures—“On
the Coast’’and “After Rain”; the one delicate
and charming, the other sickly and overstrained
in its effort to reach the unattainable, but sane
enough. A large, realistic, well-arranged land-
scape—“ Wood Carter”—by Walter Withers, is
attractive in almost all respects save the solid
background of lowering sky.
* * « *
“The Drought ” (by Hal Waugh) two oleo-
graphic men on bony steeds contemplating live
sheep and decayed mutton in a not very thirsty-
looking district is so essentially hard and uncon-
vincing that one or two illustrated papers have,
of course, reproduced it. “ Portrait of Lady Sar-
good,” by A. Handel Gear, is an inhuman treat-
ment of an amiable subject, but “Florence”
(Leslie Wilkie) promises well forthe young painter.
“Florence” is a full-length figure in a creamy
white silk dress. It stands at ease, and the
general effect of the picture is satisfactory.
Miss Violet Teague achieves some showy
failures in girl figures. “The Toilers,” by Miss
Helen Peters, is a large canvas—young women in
the foreground sewing by lamplight: ill-drawn,
misty old woman doing ditto in the background.
The picture is chiefly gloom, and the amount of
visible art doesn’t justify the reckless expenditure
on paint and frame. Bernard Hall shows an
accurate nude female figure of Grief, weeping
against a water-tank, or something, and another
nude figure of an artist’s model, seated on an un-
pleasant cane ohair, nursing a cup and saucer on
the place where her lap would be if she were
clothed. Any amount of careful work in the
flesh painting, and the stiff, dry, leopard-skin rug.
Again, Bernard Hall’s capacity for faithful re-
presentation of things is wonderfully displayed
in his “ After-Dinner Still Life.” On the
other hand, his “ Fantasia di Carnevale ” is
but a poor attempt at a poster-puzzle-picture. It
suggests a staring advt. of “ Illusions ” for a six-
penny Palace of Marvels. The funniest illusion
is a pair of pale purple legs, placed entirely “on
their own,” like an extract from the dissecting-
room of a fairy hospital.
* * * ft
Portia Geach has a fascinating work of decor-
ative art to her credit. The picture, unnamed in
. the catalogue, is a portrait of a girl in the Burne-
Jones saintly style, the bust draped in dull red
against a flat, background of peacocks’ feathers.
The composition has the charm one associates with
stained-glass windows in old cathedrals. It
remains in the memory. The same brainy artist
has failed to score even a good likeness in her cold
portrait of brother Edwin Geach, the young
theatrical worldling. No girl ought to paint her
brother, anyhow. Miss Sutherland is respectably
represented at the exhibition, likewise J. H. Schel-
terna with his familiar cattle. Norman Macgregor
earns respect among the very young artists for his
“Ti-tree Panel.” N. Carter, too, is fairly suc-
cessful in “An Old Bush Kitchen,” a warm
cottage interior, with a couple of well-placed
figures in it. A worthy thing in its way is
Dancey’s cheerful study of “ Dawn,” an allegorical
female displayed in an aesthetic frame, which
raises the tout ensemble to a glorious altitude of
10ft. or thereabouts. Other worthy things, not
being 10ft. high, are overlooked for want of space.

THE SENATE.
The most interesting question at present
being discussed by Senators is, Will the
tariff be through the House by the end of
this week? With fair progress it should
be, but the probabilities' are that the
greater part of next week will also be re-
quired to, complete the schedule.

PROTESTANT UNION OF
VICTORIA
The committee appointed by the meeting
held at -the Assembly-hall, Collins-street, on
June 30, for the purpose of organising the
Protestant vote in Victoria, met at the As*
sembly-hall board-room yesterday evening,
To obtain the return to Parliament
of representatives who pledge them-
selves to support the above objects
of the union."

END OF THE ‘‘ BAIL Y MAIL ” OFFER.
The Daily Mail (London) announces in our
columns to-day the final withdrawal of its
“Encyclopaedia Britannica” offer in New South
Wales. This sale was undertaken a few weeks
ago by permission of the London Times, and the
period of the contract having been reached, no
further books can be sold after Saturday, August
2nd. We understand the sale has been most
successful, exceeding that even of the Times
distribution of the same work two years ago. A
novel feature of this new offer is an extra volume
compiled especially for the use of students and
others, and entitled “A Handbook of Systematic
Readings in the ‘Encyclopaedia Britannica.’”
This volume forms a 26th and is sent gratis with
all orders for the “Encyclopaedia.” Much com-
ment has been caused by the characteristic manner
in which this offer of the “Encyclopaedia Britan-
nica” has been placed before the New South Wales
public. This no doubt accounts for its marked
success. See announcement elsewhere.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
33 of this issue *

find the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances at Head Office, and to the Balance
Sheets furnished by the various Branches, the latter being certified to by the respective Managers and
Accountants. ' ’’

bane on the 30th ult., and hereby certify that we have found them correct.

exhibits a true and correct view of the affairs of the Bank as slH»wn by the Books on the 30th,
June, 1902.

N.S.W. PATRIOTIC FUND.
REPORT OF EXECUTIVE COMMITTEE
For the Year 1901-1902.
Presented to and adopted by the General Committee, Wednesday,
16th July, 1902.
Your Executive Committee have met fortnightly throughout the year now reported on.
Although Peace has happily been declared, there will be need for the continuance of these meetings for some
months to come, as there are still a considerable number of New South Wales men at the seat of the late War;
indeed, since the Declaration of Peace, several deaths have been recorded, and a large number of invalids have
returned whose applications had not reached us at the date of closing this report.
During the year 539 New Cases have been dealt with, and 341 cases previously considered were again reviewed on
further application for relief. .. _
The appointment of a Medical Examiner on behalf of the Fund has materially assisted the Executive Committee
in arriving at their decisions. ....... . ... . .
The total amount of Donations to the funds since its initiation is £52,419 7s. 5d., and interest received
£2734 17s. sd.
The amount distributed in Relief, from
Ist July* 1901, to 30th June, 1902, is £8,946 10 0
Up to 30th June, 1901, it was .. .. .. .. .. •• 12,440 0 0
Making a Grand Total, since initiation of Fund £21,386 10 0
This amount is made up as follows :
Widows and Children .. .. .. .. .. .* •• £1,659 15 0
Other Dependents.. .. .. .. .. •• •• •• 8,611 0 0
Wounded and Invalided 16,115 15 0
£21,386 10 0
The Executive Committee have to express their thanks to the authorities for their courteous assistance in giving
Reports in first cases, and especially in second applications where the applicants reside in the country^
The Executive have to report, with regret, the retirement from the Executive Committee of Sir William
McMillan, and Messrs. J. S. Brunton and Richard Teece. The vacancy caused by the retirement of Sir William
McMillan was filled up at a Special Meeting of the Committee on 16th October, 1901, by the election of Mr. F. Bridges.
It remains for the Committee to elect two of their number to fill the vacancies caused by the retirement of Messrs. J.
S. Brunton and Richard Teece.
Mr. Richard Teece has also forwarded his resignation from the Committee.
As provided for under the By-laws, a Statement of Receipts and Expenditure has to be published in the month
This 1 Statement! duly signed oy the Trustees and audited by Mr. David Fell, 0.A.A., Public Accountant, is now
placed before you, and, on adoption, will be published, as required by the By-laws.
AUGUSTUS F. ROBINSON,
Chairman of Executive Committee.
Sydney , N.S.W., 30th June , 1902.

Our Australian Black Burden.
“New Guinea” writes
Australia may have one day to face serious trouble in
New Guinea, caused by the missionaries. The position
has of late become so strained that in certain parts of the
Possession the question has resclved itself into one of the
missionaries v. the Government and the white men. In
Samarai district the most sweeping charges have been
made against small Government officials by the mission-
aries, and so far is this animosity on the part of the
missionaries against the executive officers of the Govern-
ment displayed, that quite recently the London Missionary
Society, at Vinato, near Samarai, actually charged a high
judicial officer with gross partiality in dealing with cer-
tain capital offences. The charges were entirely un-
founded. In the early part of this year several magis-
trates in the eastern division were hounded down and
persecuted by the missionaries. The L.M.S. station is at
Kwato, a pretty little island about two miles off Samarai,
where the mission has the finest houses in New Guinea.
The missionaries don’t go amongst the natives in the
interior, and many of them are actively engaged as
traders. They receive tons and tons of copra from the
natives as “donations” to the mission, and this goes down
to Sydney in the L.M.S. steamer, John Williams, freight
free. The competition of missionaries with the regular
traders has been so keen that, at a public meeting
in Samarai last January, a resolution was carried with the
utmost enthusia-:.' 1 , to the effect that the Governor be
asked to prohibit the missionaries from trading of every
sort in British Papua, and'pointing out that the practice
was becoming so prevalent and extensive as to seriously
interfere with the living of the licensed traders and busi-
ness people. The L.M.S. and Anglican missionaries also
appear in the police and criminal courts, and assume the
role of prosecutors. In every one of the trumped-up
cases of rape and murder—they always put it as rape and
murder—which they have brought against white men on
behalf of the native, dismissals have been granted. But,
still, this is poor satisfaction to the person charged, as
when an odious—even though it is false and malicious—
charge of that description, is once brought against
a man, a certain stigma will always attach to
him in the eyes of a section of the public.
In every part of the Possession the missionary stations
occupy the very best sites, and have the handsomest
buildings, gardens, etc. The missionaries run no risks,
and yet pose before the Australian public as martyrs
amongst the savages. The fact of the matter is, New
Guinea has not yet arrived at that stage where missionary
effort, as a Christianising influence, can be of any service.
The popular conception of a missionary is an earnest,
self-sacrificing, fearless man, who takes his life in his
hands and goes amongst the savage races to make friends
with them, and try and teach them. But the missionaries
of New Guinea never do this. They let the Government
parties go first, and a ter these have spread death
and destruction amongst a hostile tribe, the holy
men come behind and tell the injured natives that
such outrages were necessary as an example for their
future guidance. Then they establish a station.
The missionaries, instead of being the peacemakers and
friends of both white and black, are the active enemies of
the former, and the spoilers of the latter. Their hand
is against ever}' other white man’s in the Possession, and
every white man’s is against them. They quarrel with
the white man, and they quarrel with each other, and
between the lot of them the natives get a very lovely
time. The whole system wants thoroughly reformimr.
In the first place, missionaries should not be allowed to
trade with the natives, or compete, with native-grown
products, with white business people. Secondly, they
should not be allowed to acquire freehold lan Is —and
these the very best in the Possession. Thirdly, they
should not take an active part in laying paltry charges
and prosecuting white men in the criminal courts. They
should leave this to the Government, which is well able
to look after it. Fourthly, they should go amongs' the
natives in the interior, instead of living a life of idleness
on their comfortable stations.
