POLITICAL
POINTS
Mr. Barton speaking in London, July 16 :
“ In Australia they would find the Empire’s butcher.”
One of ’em, anyhow.
“ The Empire’s Butcher,” Barton says ;
Unfortunate conceit—
Rather a happy hunting-ground
For Empire’s human meat.
Now that King Ned is on the high road to
renewed health, one may be pardoned for asking
—ls not Royalty itself the vermiform-appendix
of the body politic ?
George Reid’s silly anxiety to have Lord
Hopetoun created High Commissioner for the
Australian Commonwealth is another instance of
the triumph of Hope over experience.
In face of all the talk about the hard times
and the need of Parliamentary retrenchment, no
reformer in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly is heard
demanding the abolition of the office of Sergeant
at Arms or Usher of the Black Rod.
Mr. G. H. Reid, at the N.S.W. com-
mercial travellers’ banquet, complained that can-
didates for Parliament, and therefore members of
Parliament, “don’t represent the higher intelli-
gence of the constituency.” The statement coming
from Mr. Reid carried its own proof.
Another “ snag” for the Queensland State
Treasurer this year is the Police Superannuation
Fund, reported by the Investment Board as hope-
lessly insolvent. Estimated receipts from all
sources for the year, £6000; expenditure, £20,000.
The board asks for £14.500 this year, and last year
it got £12,600.
Strenuous efforts (evidently countenanced
by the daily press) to raise a Protestant—R.C.
row for next N.S.W. general election will be
much aided if Cardinal Moran returns. That
indiscreet personage never knows when to keep
his mouth shut, and mulishly responds to every
leg-pull by his enemies.
The average income of each# inhabitant of
N.S. Wales is estimated at about £44 a year.
Consequently, the See-Owe’Sullivan Government
borrowed enough last year to support 160,000 of
the population—about 12 per cent, of the com-
munity. Think of 12 per cent, of a self-reliant
English -speaking community living on I O U’s !
Westralia still hasn’t enough women to go
round. If every two were joined by a third there
would still, in fact, be a shortage. To be doubly
accurate, the figures at the end of May were 63J
females to 100 males, which is a lower proportion
than there was in ’OO and ’Ol. Returning cohen-
tingenters. account for some of this; a swollen
stream of immigration westward accounts for
more.
Members of S.A. Assembly, finding that,
since the enlargement of their electorates, cadging
applications for subscriptions for cricket, football
and other clubs have largely increased, are trying
to combine to refuse all such applications in future.
As a matter of democracy all such subscriptions
should be treated as illegal bribes. A member’s
allowance is granted to him to live on, not to cor-
rupt his constituency with.
Q. Premier Philp, who couldn’t bear, last
year, to think of reducing Q. Governor’s salary
below £5OOO per annum (Ontario, with over
2,000,000 people, only pays its Governor about
£2000), is now about to make an onslaught on
poor old women who make £5O per annum by
cleaning out Govt, buildings. These unfortunate
women are to suffer a 5 per cent reduction as a
counterblast to the deficit.
A letter from Shepparton (Victoria) :
Same old trip—G. H. Reid, the Argus, and the cus-
tomary lie. This day’s (17th) Argus says the Dry Dog
person was met here, en route to Numurkah, “ by a large
body of farmers and townspeople.” Went specially to
Bee the reception. The “ large body ” must have been
Reid ; the crowd numbered 16, including one farmer.
About that number is liable to meet the train any day.
Nine of the crowd cheered when they saw G.H.R. The
other seven laughed.
“ Kiwi ” : Be the pow-wow as to the
King’s Coronation oath. Curiously enough, it
never seems to strike anyone that, according to
the principles of English law, the Oath is not
binding. An oath, to be binding, must be volun-
tary ; and, as the King takes the oath under
orders, no lawyer could consider it valid. [Surely
not right. What about a court-witness, who is
forced (subpoena) to give evidence on oath?—
Ed. B.]
In the last (1901) Budget, the Q. Govt,
deprecated any reduction of Civil Service salaries
by way of meeting the deficit. Now the Philp
party is reducing (or proposing to reduce) the Civil
Service wholesale, even knocking 5 per cent, off
salaries under £lOO. This bitter pill is also to be
sugar-coated with an income-tax over and above.
And it serves two-thirds of the Queensland Civil
Service right—their cowardice and selfishness has
met its reward.
Irvine’s proposed amendment of the Vic-
torian Constitution, giving a Minister of one
House the right to appear in the other for the
purpose of explaining’ a Bill fathered by him, will,
if carried, _ have decided advantages. The right
already exists in a number of Parliaments, and
the need for it in N.S.W. was lately shown by B.
R. Wise resigning his Leg. Assembly seat so as
to be able to explain the Compulsory Arbitration
Bill to the Council.
Land alienation in New Guinea should be
closely watched. The information given in the
Senate leaves several large loopholes for the enter-
prising land-shark. “Selectors actually in pos-
session can obtain 10 square miles.” To own an
acre or two, or, for that matter, a few square
yards, is to be in possession. Transfers to others
afterwards ao« freely talked of. In view of the
probable mineral future of the country, £lOO worth
of land might turn out an excellent gamble.
Queensland Premier Philp makes a half-
promise to take a referendum on the subject of
religious teaching in State schools. Just now,
the money that referendum would cost is much
more urgently wanted to reduce the deficit. Still,
if the present Government is a product of “god-
less education,” then there’s a good deal to be
said for religious teaching. On the other hand, if
the present Government had a religious training,
then godless education should be earnestly culti-
vated.

During a recent deputation to Mr. Irvine on the unemployed question, Mr. Hannah said that by thoroughly grappling with the labor question, Maoriland had
practically solved it and made herself prosperous. In Victoria there were 30 families who held 3,000,000 acres of land, employing only 500 people. If cut up into 320-
acres blocks that land would employ 51,000 persons.
At recent Farmers’ and Settlers’ Conference in Sydney the need for an effective Closer Settlement Act was emphasised by one speaker, who instanced the Peel
River estate, with its 45 miles frontage to the river, and supporting about a dozen people looking after a few thousand sheep, while adjoining was a 40-acre block
supporting a family of 13.

and intensely gratified at the spread of Freetrade
principles in Victoria, and at the present rate of
progress, &c., &c. The Freetraders, the. Bible-i-
n party, and the crowd which wants to restore

which are always amazed and gratified at the re-
markable spread of their principles. If Freetrade

Reid himself when he was in office, then there

of.

The detailed report of last year’s census of
S. Aus., just issued, explains some of the “Con-
servative reaction ” over there. Naturally am in
is most Radical between, say, 25 and 35, and the
further away he gets from 35 the more Conserva-
tive he becomes : of coarse, there are exceptions
to this, but the generalisation stands firm for all
that. Now, though S.A. gained 2014 per cent,
of people in 10 years, it actually wound up with
4-37 per cent, fewer males between 25 and 30 than
it began with, and 3'58 per cent, fewer between
30 and 35. On the other hand, it had 4012 per
cent. more between 40 and 45 than it had in ’9l.
The only other actual decrease was of children
under 6
The true inwardness of the Kyabram
“ reform ” movement, so far as Melb. Argus is
concerned, has at last crept out. After combining
with its hated Protectionist rival for months to
prove the u ter impoverishment.and instability of
Victoria, the Argus comes along with a subtle
suggestion that the real cause of all the trouble
is the policy of Protection, and advises people to
insist upon a trial of Freetrade, just to see whether
it isn’t a better idea. Kyabram has been the
Argus game all along, and through it Syme’s
Age has forfeited its political grip appreciably.
The Irvine Ministry is said to be the first to
which Syme has not acted as accoucheur during
nearly 20 years past.
Sydney D.T. in a leader, July 21, badly
gives itself away on the aliens immigration restric-
tion question.
We shall not attempt to uphold the administration of
Argentina as a model, for it has had very many short-
comings. But (italics ours) with a territory about one-
third the size of Australia, it has of late years consistently
done its best to attract immigration, and to settle immi-
grants on the land rapidly. It has no writing test, no
prohibitions; and the population and the immigrants
must rely on their own efforts, unassisted by anything
beyond a ready access to the land.
So “ no writing-tests and no prohibitions ” against
aliens constitute the chief virtues of Argentine
Government in the D.T.’s opinion !

Labor-member Griffith’s Bill to. abolish
fees in N.S.W. State schools should be taken up
in earnest this time by every Democrat in the
Leg:. Assembly, and ushered into the Council
with such emphatic backing as will scare hostile
fossils. The Bill has never got a square chance of
settlement on its merits. Members who cheer-
fully sanction tens and hundreds of thousands of
pounds for royal visits and empty theatrical cele-
brations, oppose the remission of the children’s-
threepennies as if it involved national bankruptcy.
And it has been fought bitterly from motives of
sectarianism. Yet free education is the born
right of the children of the State, and the logical
complement of compulsory education. By the-
way, one cheeky feature of the opposition is S.M.
Herald’s long-ago leader stating that the country
“ couldn’t afford ” free education—though it could
afford to carry the S.M.H. and all the other news-
papers free through the post at a huge sacrifice.
Howl of rage from London Spectator,
reproduced by Sydney Telegraph as a protest
against Australian industry:
The perverse ingenuity of the Victorian and South
Australian Governments in particular has indeed been
desperately active in vain and costly efforts to develop
forms of industry for which the two States are either
unripe or absolutely unsuitable. Under cover of a high
tariff, woollen mills have been erected, and labor urgently
required for useful purposes has been employed in a
feeble attempt to ape the West Riding of Yorkshire.
What would be thought of the West Riding if it tried
to constitute itself into a sheep-run, or took to working
and mining for gold ? However, the microscopic results
of these expensive foibles have not seriously distracted
the attention of Australia from its great staple industries..
How indignant these far-off people get because-
Victoria and S.A. choose to manufacture some of
their own wool, and thus find employment for
about 1100 of their own people, instead of sending
the wool to England in Bull’s ship, and paying
1100 English people to do the manufacturing,
and bringing back the finished product in Bull’s
ship ! Also, consider the perverse ingenuity of
the United Kingdom in keeping 31,000,000 sheep
—twice as many as Victoria and S.A. put together
—and thus remorselessly cutting into our wool-
growing business. And if theWestßidingof York-
shire, with 700,000 sheep (250 to every square
mile), isn’t a sheep run, what is it, and why does
it try to ape Riverina in this fashion ?

Dear Sir,—About 18 months ago I was forced to give up
my position owing to an attack of congestion op the
brain caused by excessive study and work. For some
time I was very ill, and was under the care of four of the
best doctors in New Zealand. I found that although I
had recovered from the cerebal congestion, the nervous
system had received such a shock and become so weakened
that my medical advisers had informed me that I would
have to take perfect rest for a long time, and that it
would be from two to three years before I would regain
my strength and be what I had been prior to my break-
ing down. Knowing of the wonderful results which had
followed the taking of VITADATIO, I resolved to go in
for a course of it, and after drinking 12 large bottles I
rejoice to be able to say that I am now well and strong
again, and enjoy the best of health. I feel that I cannot
but let young men who struggle through the weary
days of their office work, feeling so weak and ill that life
has very few pleasures for them, know what your
medicine has done for me. To all such I earnestly re-
commend VITADATIO, as I am perfectly sure it will
thoroughly tone up the system, and drive all impurities
out of the blood.—Yours, very sincerely,

A certain kiosk (refreshment) in the Bris-
bane Botanic Gardens was leased out lately at
£4OO per annum, or £8 a week—lessee to erect
buildings, etc. Previous lessee had a beautifully
soft thing on—ten shillings’ rental per week for
ten years.
Not generally known that present Sydney
Pitt-st. electric tram-line is not the first to run
along that thoroughfare. In December, 1861, iust
40 years before the opening of the electric line, a
horse-tram service was started. That was the
first Sydney tram-line, and it was torn up in 1866.
It is just 40 years ago since a N.S.W.
Parliamentary Select Committee, with Parkes as
chairman, reported to the House that the unem-
ployed m Sydney alone numbered 3000.
Attorney-General Wise’s Bill for the em-
ployment of licensed shorthand-writers to take
down the evidence in courts has the balance of
argument and commonsense in its favor, since it
wm shorten the duration of trials. Yet most
-N.b.W. Judges and barristers are against it, from
no other motive, seemingly, than that of in-
stinctive conservatism.

Ourious ad. from the Adelaide dailies just
after what should have been Coronation Day :
The Lady Sarah Moreo CJoppinger has fought a big
battle for the King. He will be spared by the Lady Sarah
Ooppinger.
Stated that Russia has 10 horses to every
sq. mile of its European area, or one fourth of all
the horseflesh on the face of the earth. The whole
British Empire is supposed to possess about
7,000,000 horses, the United Kingdom holding
2,000,000, Australia 1,600,000. Germany has
4,000,000, and Russia 22,000,000 !
That strange and demoralising insect
whose presence in the tenement is so much
objected to, was the subject of a substantial law-
suit in Maoriland a little while ago. An old lady
sold a house, and the purchaser went back on
his bargain, and refused to pay or take delivery,
alleging that the building was infested by the
undesirable animal above hinted at. The old
lady wanted damages. It was pointed out that
the noctural marauder wasn’t mentioned in the
agreement, therefore the law had to decide,
supposing the stealthy parasite was there, whether
he was sufficient cause for rescinding the bar-
gain. Evidence was called at great length,
some witnesses swearing that they had seen the
objectionable beast walking on the premises,
and others swearing that they hadn’t. Two ex-
pensive lawyers addressed the court at great
length about a small reddish object that isn’t
generally mentioned in courts or in polite society.
Then his honor reserved judgment, probably so
that he might sleep in the disputed house, and see
if he was bitten by the defendant in the night—
the animal being the defendant, to all intents and
purposes.

savagery of Flemington steeplechase course
has now been recognised even by the Melbourne daily
papers, and the Argus remarks No one wants to see
the steeplechase course at Flemington made as easy as
some of the suburban ones, but there seems something* in
the nature of a trap about the stone wall. Its abolition
would still leave the course a good jumping one. Race-
goers can have no objection to ‘The Cathedral,’ as this
obstacle is called, going. Very little of the jumping at
this part of the course can be seen from the stands ” So
now it is pretty certain that the superfluous stone wall
(which is too far distant to give the Standites any horror
for their money) will be taken away presently.

Last ’Murkan mail brought the famous Yankee
trotting stallion Owhyee, and a 4-year-old mare, Zenam-
bra, for Victoria’s enthusiast, Mr. John Robertson, of
Trugamna—the home of Osterley and other top-notchers.
Owhyee’s purchase was the result of three months’ search
A few years ago he put up 2.11. Authorities agree that
Owhyee is among the finest types of trotting horse Cali-
forma (if not the U.S.) owned. His breeding* is : Sired by
Chas. Derby, 2.20 (sire of Much Better, 2.7 J, Derby Prin-
cess, 2.81, and Little Better, 2.11), dam Ida Wood (dam
of Babe Marian, 2.171, by Simmons, 2.28). In nine starts
■during 99 Owhyee won seven times, and finished second
and third on the other two occasions. Three races saw
jhim account for a majority of the best trotters America
boasted in his class. Following year he went to the stud,
and some handsome colts and fillies are said to have been
the result.

Sydney’s best-known citizens and turf-meii. Heart

entered for “ young ” races.

* * ■* *
The present week makes the first bad piece of
railway travelling in the Australian XI. tour, otherwise
so well arranged. To rush up from Taunton (away in the
West) to London, thence to Manchester by night, and
back through London to Brighton, is about as bad an
arrangement as could be worked for consecutive matches.
Australian supporters in England are urging strongly
that all test matches should begin on Mondays, to give
the team the chance of a quiet journey and some rest.
As it is, the only one so arranged is the fifth, and the
other four have meant Wednesday-night journeys from
London to Birmingham, Cambridge to London, Edinburgh
to Sheffield, and now London to Manchester.
* * * *
“The weather was brilliantly fine, but the
attendance was only moderate.” Cricket cables report
bad business for the Australian XI. as the result of their
consistent walloping of the ordinary English county
teams and the weakness of the opposition in two or three
cases where they might have been given a chance to
shake up local interest. The third test, which they won,
is the only great match of the tour up to time of writing.
Their defeat by the strong Yorkshire team was a mere
mud-incident; and their revenge was discounted by the
fact that Jackson, who represents fully 30 per cent, of
the Yorkshire strength, didn’t trouble to play for his
county. The ’aughty amateur ought to have been
there to assist in putting up a good fight against the
Australians for the sake of both sides. Now that the
visitors are pretty sure to win every time, they have
reason to complain of what looks like mean treatment by
the English cricket bosses, except in the selection of test
players. The M.O. C. eleven was not nearly as represen-
tative as it might have been, and the “ England ” eleven
had no pretensions to make a hard struggle and draw a
big “ gate.” Years ago, when the tests were limited to a
series of three, the Australian programme was made more
attractive than now by the inclusion of at least half-a-
dozen matches in which the visitors were put on
their mettle, Men like A. G. Steel, in his day, followed
them from place to place. W. G. Grace never missed an
opportunity of meeting them. The crowd rolled up with
a reasonable hope of rejoicing over the supremacy of
their side, and the Australians had a good financial time.
Now that the English play is comparatively weak, the
Australians get “ only moderate attendance,” because in
match after match their victory is only a question of
weather permitting. The crowd would go to see them
bustled by a carefully-picked, even though it were not
called an “All England” team, but the recognised ex-
cellence of the Australian play, in this year 1902, forbids
any hope of seeing it fairly beaten by commonplace county
“ form.”
* * * •
Jessop last week had two more chances to
punish Australian bowling in a “ true ” Jessopian style.
Result, 13 and 21. All the talk about the little man not
showing his proper English form in Australia was mostly
nonsense. He is a fairly successful paster of poor bowl-
ing, and can keep it down more often than not. When
the bowling is tricky he hits it up and trusts to luck, or
misses it altogether. In the Gloucester match he was
clean bowled once and stumped the other time. Surely
they will not want him, or Fry, in the next All England
Eleven. Lilley is another consistent failure who may be
expected to drop out. He was a serviceable batsman as
long as he was a brilliant wicketkeeper, but ever since
that first test match in Sydney last year Lilley has been
“off.”

Australia- Somersetshire match began Wednes-
day last week. Somerset were first at the wickets and
gathered 274 ; best scores being 52 (twice) and 44. Saun-
ders bagged 5 for 109 and Jones 3 for 68. Australians
made 348 in their initial attempt. Duff (183) did best,

innings of Somerset produced 315. Most graceful bat of
the day, L. O. H. Palairet, scored 90 ; next to him were
62 and ex-N.S.W. man S. M. J. Woods’ 30. Jones sent 4
back for 104, and Hopkins 3 for 46. In Australia’s second
innings (with 242 runs to make) Trumper went out early
(1.b.w.), and Duff had scored 11 when rain stopped further
play with the score at 1 for 16.
* • * *
Fourth test, Australia v. England, begins to-
day (Thursday) at Manchester. Weather conditions
promise well, and as Australians have been operating
brilliantly inference is they’ll show a bold front. Fact
that Noble and Trumble haven’t been shaping with the
ball lately leads to the presumption that they are being
“saved” for the big thing to come. Summed up, Aus-
tralia’s prospects of scoring the “ rub.” are by no means
bad. Old Trafford ground at Manchester has been the
locale of six tests in the past, and of these England
gathered up two and Australia one ; the other three were
drawn. The ’96 Australian invaders won there by three
wickets ; then Iredale made 108 and Ranji 62 and 154 not
out. Three years back England’s first effort produced
372 and Australia’s 196, but the latter came very strongly
in its next essay, piling 346 for 7 wickets, and closing at
that. Three of the Britishers were out for 94 at the end
of play. Noble’s memorable stone-walling feat occurred
in this engagement.

Return Q.-N.S.W. toe-ball match, Saturday,
was the toughest ever played by those States. Upwards
of 16,000 people thronged Sydney ’Varsity Oval and faced
drizzling rain to see the struggle. The game
saw sound defence prominent, and the manner in
which the advantage flashed from one to another,
and then back again rendered play unusually exciting.
At half-time N.S.W. was in front, 4' to 3, and soon after
resumption added 3 more ; then Q. converted a “ try,”
and brought its points to 8. Though work seemed even
from this out, Q. failed to notch any more, while N.S.W.
accumulated another 6 per medium of a penalty goal and
a try, which enabled it to finish 13 to 8 best. Q. team
was different from that of the week before. Dennis played
full back in place of Christensen, while Oxlade (specially
brought over) and Colton filled the breach among for-
wards caused by accidents to O’Connor and Anderson.
Home 15 was different, too, in that Verge and Allan
replaced Maund (full-back) and Halloran (three-quarter),
and Coomber took Wright’s place. Again Finley (N.S.W.)
shaped ahead of any other in the field. 'Wickham
(N.S.W.) was splendid in both attack and defence.
Spragg (Q.) had the high distinction of building up all his
side’s points as the result of two goals and a try. Half-
back Evans shone brightly. Verge (N.S.W.) played his
first big match, and, as full-back opposite, Dennis (Q.) did
a heap of good for the team. Bananalanders never
previously shaped so well in Sydney. Some top-form
tackling and line-kicking occurred at different times.
• * • *
Queensland football team walloped N.S.W.
Second Fifteen by 15 to 5, on Sydney ’Varsity Oval,
Wednesday week. It was a fast game throughout,
played in mud and water, with considerable dash and
good all-round work during the first half. Second half
was marred with scrambling roughness, and a succession
of mild accidents brought about frequent interruptions,
but nothing worse than a badly-fractured jaw and some r
strained sinews happened, although many confidently
hoped, right up to the last five minutes, that at least a
broken neck would round-off the list gracefully.

* » * •
The cable tells that there is to be a speed-
contest between the flying-machines of Santos Dumont
and Hiram Maxim, at St. Louis, U.S.A. Neither of these
implements is an air-ship in the proper sense of the word.
The Brazilian machine is merely a half-navigable balloon,
and the Yankee contrivance a sort of mechanical flying-
fox, which can only keep going as long as the impetus of
the original start off the elevated railway lasts. Imme-
diately that impetus begins to fail, Maxim’s machine
begins to glide earthwards till, by the time it is exhausted,
it has settled down helplessly on the beastly common
globe again. When the ideal air-ship, the real “ Clipper
of the Clouds,” does come, it will be like Jules Verne’s
fanciful creation, heavier than the air, supported with
aero-planes driven by engines of small bulk but enormous
power. At present there is no engine which, in propor-
tion to weight, can generate the same power that a bird
does when it flies, and apparently till that engine comes
along the aerial navy will not materialise.
• * * »
Big table-tennis tourney ended at Sydney
Queen’s Hall, Saturday night, and prizes were worth
£l5O. The two absolutely best players—Horace Elliott
(champion) and D. C. Ward—won one each. Neither
could compete in both, or in all human probability Elliott
would have won both. Elliott’s style of play is as good as
a pantomime, and was a big draw, and although ping-
pong is a nice, harmless, sheep-like game, Elliott puts all
the possibilities of broken limbs, utter smash, and sudden
death into it. He swoops into corners, he flings himself
on the floor to return low drives that seem impossible,
and whether standing on one hand, lying on his back, or
standing on his head, all his strokes are clean, well-calcu-
lated, and admirably placed. He flings himself from one
side of the table to the other, and smites the ball with a
mighty swipe that would propel a 40-ton locomotive, and
if his opponent can return it he has Elliott done, as that
worthy gets so much steam on he is generally flattened
against the opposite wall. The way he gets back from
that wall to stand on his forefinger and thumb in order
to meet the return that never comes is truly wonderful.
It is a boon and a blessing to many alleged pugs now holding
their heads fairly high that Elliott didn’t take to bruising.
A bruiser who could fling himself about at such a rate of
speed would be a dangerous object to get into any sized
ring with.

N.S.W.’s weighty premier scratch puller, Larry
Ryan, who scales somewhere near 17st., recently went
under to the big Dubbo (N.S.W.) Teuton, Bob Hastardt,
in W.A. Ryan sits on his haunches, toe to toe, and pulls
square, but Hastardt insisted upon a cleat and got down
to work sideways. R.van tried to connect again t for £5OO
a side, but t’other wouldn’t come. Explained that Ryan
hauled the German up almost perpendicular in the initial
strain, but wasn’t quite ready when the order to pull
came again. The third trial extended over 40m|n.,
through a succession of false starts, and when they did
get off the champion slipped. Ryan now challenges all
scratch-Dullers in Australia, and says he 11 back himself
to pull the best of them out of them skins.
Australian swimmers, George Read and “Dick
Cavill, went under to Britisher J. A. Jarvis, Saturday
last, in the English long-distance championship, some 50
or more yards over 5 utiles, from Kew to Putney.
Jarvis occupied lh. 12m. 27sec., Read lh. 14m. lOsec.,
and Cavill lh. 16m. 50sec. The record (lh. 6m. 35sec.)
stands to credit of ex-Australian premier Percy Cavill,
who accomplished it in July, ’97. Prior best was A.
Ibbott’s lh. 12m. 27sec., which stood six years. No
really .reliable line can he had regarding the relative
merits of these performances, as the question of strength
of current for the time being needs some getting' over.
Percv Cavill’s figures are, however, so much better than
the others that it seems safe to assume he swam faster
than those winners who went before or came after him.

News that swimmers Dick Cavill and George
Read were second and third to ’Ome champion “ Dave
Billington in the English 500 yards supremacy (July 14)
was disappointing, as little danger was feared from that
quarter, though both Australians recognised that he had
improved and would improve considerably. Time
(6.25 2-5) indicates that Billington has come along re-
markably, for it easily wipes out previous best amateur
performance (Jarvis’ 6.35) and is but 1 l-ssec. slower than
world’s professional champion “Joey” Nuttall’sspeediest.
Dick Cavill (3see. behind the winner) also out under the
then existing amateur best, while Read (6.35) equalled it
to the fraction. Contention that neither Australian has
been long enough in the country to show his true form is
pure bunkum. It isn’t recorded that Cavill or Read ever
swam as well here as the figures for this race indicate they
are doing in England. Interesting that their conqueror
(now 19) has been swimming as “ boy” Billington since he
could move his legs, and winning, with few exceptions.

Australian rifle team in London appropriated
the M'Kinnon Cup, shot for at Bisley (Eng.) Friday last
week, with 1328 points. Canada was second (1308),
Rhodesia (1267) third, and England (1265) fourth. M‘Kin-
non Cup was first in evidence 11 years ago. Conditions
have been altered this year. In lieu of five volleys at
800yds. in one minute at a 6ft. x 12ft. figure target, the
competitors were to fire 10 shots each at 800, 900, and
1000yds. The teams consist of 12 men, as against 10
in previous years. Also cabled that best eight scores
of Australian rifle team under Kolapore Cup con-
ditions averaged 94J. W. Sloane (Vic.) made 102, and
A. Ferguson (Q.) 101. This average is satisfactory, and
leads to the impression that the Commonwealth team
will make a big fight for the prize. Three weeks since
they averaged 96 per man, tieing the record for the com-
petition. Famous Bananaland shot, Mr. Ferguson, then
made 102; his 101 in the second practice showing con-
sistently good form. Mr. Sloane went “on his own,” and
not as a member of the team, but that 102 may make
room for him among the eight. On M‘Kinnon Cup day
M.L. reps, (with 145) won fifth prize in the Mappiri Cup,
the winners making 176.

Black and white pug struggles ’tween negro
Tom Mitchell and Melbourne Jack Willis, at Sydney
Gaiety A. 0., Monday night, disappointed a packed house
that smacked its lips in pleasurable anticipation, for
both principals (admitted good ones) shaped in prime
trim, and brimful of confidence. Mitchell, lank-limbed
and clever, had weight, reach, and skill ahead of his
opponent, and, though floored by a stiff punch in the
mouth ere the first round closed, was up quick as a flash,
dealing out stoush freely and heavily. Midway through
the second 3mins. it was all over ; a heavy jolt on the jaw
settled Willis beyond recall, and the son of Ham won a
fight that was all his bar one blow. Australia hasn’t a
cleverer middleweight than Mitchell, who looks to possess
the physique, though some doubt exists regarding
stamina and heart; but for this there’d surely bo rocks
ahead for champion “Snowy” Sturgeon.
* » * *
Much-boomed fight ’tween ’Murkan, Scanlan,
and West Indian negro, Starlight, at Newcastle t’other
night, was uneventful and all one-sided, till Scanlan
drove a stiff punch on the chin, sending the coon out on
the boards amid a storm of jeers from disappointed on-
lookers. Time now that Starlight gave the game best
and tried something more suitable to his waning physical
powers. He is now a year or two on (he road to the
second half century, and for some time has plainly
reached only for the loser’s whack, or an equal divide
whoever won. No one minds this so long as matches are
genuinely fought; but all lovers of the game must regret
the hard knocks such occurrences as that at Newcastle
give its popularity. Though never a champion, Starlight
waS for years above the average pug in point of skill and
fighting merit. He’s about due for a rest now.
* » * «
There has been some tough fighting in Sydney
lately, but the toughest and most willing of the lot was
when negro Barnes and Lynch, said to hail from Wagga
(N.S.W.), went for each other like tigers. Right through
15 rounds they tried to deal out sudden eternity with an
utter disregard for rules or the exhortations of the
paralysed referee. Eventually they were at a standstill
and had to be called off, each looking a pitiable spectacle
and wearing a sick and sorry appearance that a hundred-
fold the small “gate” wouldn’t compensate. This writer
never saw such an amount of thump served out with such
freedom, and cannot understand how either survived so
long. There was no skill on either side ; neither knew
the rudiments of the business, and it was simply bash
and stoush, grip and throw, while a sweating, snorting
crowd howled like a collection of wild beasts.
FIRST-CLASS CONSTABLE DENIS GJJIL-
FOYLE, MURDERED AT SYDNEY.
There was a grim and most unusual tragedy
of crime in Sydney last Saturday. In the social
grades of lawlessness the distributor of counter-
feit coin, as a rule, takes a very low place
—among the pickpockets, the robbers of children
and the sneak-thieves: he hasn’t the brains and
nerve to be a confidence-man, nor the brute
courage to be a footpad or a burglar. When,
therefore. Constable Guilfoyle got on the track
of two false-coin-passers, out in a mean suburb
of Sydney—men who were passing counterfeit
half-crowns on small shopkeepers—lie got a
mate, Constable Maher, to go with him, but
of other precautions took none. It was only
a matter, to his mind, of catching the men,
or of failing to catch them through lack of running
power; and he accosted them without thinking of
his revolver, or even drawing a baton. But -the
criminals were of a desperate character, which
their mean calling did not match, and, without
word of parley or thought of putting their
chance of liberty to the test of a running-
match, drew pistols and opened fire on
Guilfoyle and Maher. The former received
four bullets, the latter three. Guilfoyle was
mortally wounded and died within a few minutes;
Maher, more lucky, escaped with slight injuries.
The murderer and his mate got safely away ; the
Sydney police, however, think that they know
the identity of the criminals, and, in that case,
arrest and punishment should be only a matter of
time.
The parson who rises above the ruck is
the one who is always ready to take a point when
he sees it. During the recent illness of the King
one clergyman made an immense hit. On Sunday
morning he got from the office of a daily paper
the latest (and most encouraging) cable news.
Then he started for. his church, and when the
special prayer was raised for King Ned, uttered
fervently :
We beseech Thee that our King may be relieved, that
his wound may give him less trouble, that his sleep may
be sound and, on awakening, he may be permitted to
smoke a little, for sucli is, under Thee, of great comfort
to a mail.
When the flock opened their papers next morning,
they regarded that parson as a prophetic soul.
“Westralian” on the new Groperlaud
railway boss: —
The appointment of W. J. George, M.L.A., to the posi-
tion of Railways Commissioner in Westralia has raised a
howl in Perth press, apparently' for just the same
■reason that the moonrise raises a howl among the dogs in
the back yard. Press and public have long been clamour-
ing that the railway' management was rotten; and the
system disorganised, and that a strong, masterful hand
was needed to set it right. Late Premier Leake fell foul
of George in the House over matters relating to Leake's
brother-in-law, Judge Parker. And Leake said some
nasty things. The public generally is now wondering how
Leake, of all men, should have appointed George to this
important post in face of his previous bitter attitude.
The troubles affecting the railways are chiefly labor
ones, and the management hitherto has been utterly
unable to grasp the situation. A big increase in ex-
penditure was incurred by an all-round increase in
wages—an increase strongly demanded by the men
and conceded by public opinion. Classification of
the service is also badly needed, and the efforts of
officers to classify only increased the trouble. The old
’Groper management was reared in an atmosphere of two
mixed trains per week. The hurry and bustle of the new
order of things set wheels buzzing in its head, and it sat
down and let the service drift. What was wanted
was a man familiar with Australian labor and
who understood the Australian workman. Leake
approached George and offered him the chance.
George has had many years’ experience in Australia
with big contracts and crowds of workers. Eleven years
right-hand man for Neil M‘Neil in railway construction in
Victoria. Went to W.A. to finish contract for Perth
Water Supply. He then took up the moribund Jarrah-
dale Timber Co. and put it on its legs, bringing it from
nothing to a big concern. Ten years ago, in partnership
with another man, George founded the Black- Swan
Foundry and Engineering Works, and developed it into a
fairly extensive business (for W.A.), and throughout his
whole career George has never had a labor dispute. Leake
recognised this, and by dropping a live, vigorous man into
the service has spread alarm and consternation into a
sleepy hollow of let-it-slide. The main howl appears
to have been generated because a man has been selected
from everyday surroundings, and not imported from
England. To bring a man from England—no matter
what his railway qualifications might be—familiar only
with English labor conditions, and to dump him down on
the W.A. railways in their present disorganised state,
was to court trouble and disaster. But to appoint any-
one without a British eye-glass and a Britishhaw" was
to rouse the ire of Jingoism, hence the hostility of tha
Perth press generally.

The fool honorarium magistracy of Victoria
seems to be trying to disparage the Aliens
Restriction Act just as it did the Factories Act.
The other day a Williamstown (Melb.) Bench
decided that three Portuguese stowaways —who
had failed to pass the test were “there through
no fault of their own,” and dismissed the cases.
Only a Melbourne beak could hope to arrive at
the state of mind that considers a stowaway a
I compulsory passenger.
“ Loco ” : Re the Vic. “ Spark-Arrester ”
Commission which has just sent in its report:
The Commission declines to recommend any of the con-
trivances submitted to it. Various invento.s and their
agents buzzed around while the Commission was taking
evidence, but the Board had the honesty to resist all such
pressure. No plan, apparently was submitted for keeping
the sparks in the locomotive firebox. With a good
“brick arch” and “baffle-plate” very few sparks will
leave the firebox. Good coal also emits few sparks ;
these generally proceeding from scraps of wood and rub-
bish in the coal. Unskilfulness of engine-drivers also
causes sparks. Many of these work the steam ‘ wire-
drawn,” instead of expanding it till there is little or no
blast.

night, in conveying a woman (a ferocious biter and
kicker) to a Sydney lock-up.

The Irish Education Board has decided to
have the Erse (Irish) language taught in the
National schools. This tongue—inevitably doomed
as a speech-medium—has yet been carefully con-
served in the literary form by a small number of
appreciators of its philological and historical
value.
“ Cornstalk ” to The Bulletin :
I notice that Bulletin, like other Australian papers, is
under the impression that there are no unemployed in
Maoriland. Now, I left Sydney at beginning of March,
and did not find employment till end of May, though I
can do aim any kind of outside work. I have been in
a store also for several years and have good references,
but I spent £3O roaming all over M.L., seeking “ collar.”
I know of hundreds of unemployed. There was a meeting
of them in Wellington while I was there, and I saw an
advt. about that time offering first two weeks’ pay to
anybody who could find advertiser a billet. Again, store-
keepers here don’t consider Sydney or Melbourne people
as up-to-date Maorilanders ; in fact, it repeatedly hap-
pened that the heading “Sydney” on my references
caused them to be promptly handed back, unread, with
—“Very sorry; but you Sydney people are not ‘up’
with us, and wouldn’t understand the trade.” This
amused me at first, but the matter soon got serious, and,
when one employer gravely assured me that Dunedin was
larger than Sydney I gave it up, and am now working in
a coal-pit.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
Sundry recent items in The Bulletin give
interest to the following, from Cloncurry Advo-
cate (Q.):
During the past three months there has been great
mortality amongst the blacks in this district; nearly every
week one or two have died. Last week two died, and the
week before, one. During the period mentioned at least
20 must have succumbed to sickness and disease. Queens-
land at the present time has two or three highly-paid
officials who are supposed to be looking after the blacks,
or, as they are called, “Aboriginal Protectors.” During
their appointment, one of them—Dr. Roth —visited this
district once. He had a casual look round the blacks
here, but beyond that has done nothing. There were
many of them at that time suffering from disease, but no
effort on his part was made to alleviate their sufferings.
They were allowed to lie and rot away in their camps—a
danger to their fellows as well as to those who resided in
the vicinity. It would be far better for the blacks and
the settlers out in these parts if the “ Protectors” were
retrenched out of the service, and the salaries and the
allowances they receive were put into flour, tea, sugar
and meat, and, with a well-supplied medicine chest, sent
to the different police stations, with instructions to give
all unemployed blacks a certain ration.
“ F.C.” : Encountered Melbourne's peri-
patetic swaggie, who used to wallaby round the
city day after day, t’other afternoon, and hardly
recognised him. Had it been three months hence,
I might have thought he’d been to the Coronation.
He was in his right mind, and clothed in a black
flogger, light pants and bell-topper—all somewhat
dilapidated but still passable. Also, he had ex-
changed Matilda for a robust cane and a paper-
bag, which was being used to collect butts, lunch-
remnants, and other scraps. Melbourne’s city
swagman is a mystery. A faint legend has it that
he was once a duke, but fled owing to a shortage
in his cash. Another theory is that he is the lost
Austi’ian archduke.
Lord Charles Beresford’s frank statement
of the truth that “the colonies are of far more
value to the ‘ Mother ’ country than the ‘ Mother ’
country is to the colonies” will strike thoughtful
critics as being the wisest of Imperial Jingo
utterances. It is only the dull official British
Tory of the ancient Barnacle brand who nowadays
adopts a tone of haughty parental patronage in re-
ferring to the Greater Britains. The up-to-date
Beresford pats them on their brawny young backs,
saying in effect “of course-you’ll-never-desert-us,”
and taking an affirmative answer for granted.
That’s the right cue for the political John Bull.
Weave some strong threads of flattery into the
silken bond material and it will last for years.

Constance Clyde writes on some social
matters: —
Various printed remarks on various small women’s
questions now before the public have brought me to the
conclusion that the 20th century man will not understand
woman any more than the 19tli century man did. Take
the Melbourne Women’s Hospital trouble. It began with
the committee’s desire to differentiate between married
and unmarried women in the maternity wards. I
do not think there should be any differentiation,
but the she-committee is not necessarily a joint and
several cat for thinking otherwise. Because man does
not care particularly whether he is considered respectably
married or not, he thinks women should be broad-minded
also._ The only way for man to understand feminine
sentiment on the matter is to imagine that an Institution
were formed in our midst as a Refuge for wounded
soldiers. He must also imagine Chat for some reason or
other this, the only Refuge for Atkinses, came to be largely
filled by Atkinses who had flagrantly disgraced themselves
by cowardice or treachery in the open field (supposing
it were possible that enough Atkinses were discovered
to have thus disgraced themselves). In such a case,
the few honorable Tommies forced to take refuge in this
institution would certainly want to have a different uni-
form, or something to show that they had not failed in
what is considered man’s chief virtue. It is possible,
likewise, that the honorable men outside would want to
uphold the honorable men inside in their desire for
differentiation, and would not be at all responsive if the
outside woman came along and told them to uphold their
fallen brother Tommy and not to break the bruised reed.
The Legitimisation Bill is another matter which shows
how little man understands woman—woman, who has the
making of social laws. I understand that the Bill is to
raise the social stigma from, &c. Man always talks of the
social stigma as if it could be raised by machinery or
dynamite, and raising it would supply useful work for
the unemployed. The stigma will remain after men have
passed forty bills telling it to go.
Man, the section of him that wears a monkey-
beard and puts cold water in himself by way of
dissipation, is being very much surprised just now
on anti-barmaid platforms because quite nice girls
prefer the bar, with moral temptation, to the kitchen
without. He does not understand that the occasional
disrespect with which the bar-lady may be treated as a
woman is nothing^.o the continual disrespect with which
Mary Jane is regarded as a human being. Mary Jane is
not supposed worthy of being allowed to join in the con-
versation of people who are not Mary Janes ; she is the
only woman that a man permits to bring in the coal-
m Una '^ e< ?' 18 a ' so conscious that she escapes
Moral Temptation (when she does escape it) mainlv because
her hands are large and red, and she doesn’t look
picturesque when travelling down the passage on her
knees with a large pail of soapy water and a scrubbing-
brush beside her. Consequently she is not at all grateful
for her escape. She has not even the consolation of feel-
mg' that man admires her for her superior virtue, because
nowadays man considers bad women just as good as good
ones. In Pope’s day he considered good ones as bad as
bad ones, which sounds the same, but is really quite

Guilty?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
lAM to die to-morrow. The Law says so. He
who judged me said that never had he tried so
vile a wretch, inasmuch as I had wilfully killed a
pood and just man, blameless in his life and
respected by all.
Let a score of years fall from my life. Now I
am happy again in an everyday sort of way a
good home, steady work, and a girl-wife, of whom
I think the world.
One night when I came home it was to find her
gone, leaving no word, no trace behind All
search proved useless. Well, I started to go down
then in the ordinary dull, commonplace way.
Oh, yes ! I know what any person of well-
balanced mind would say : “ How weak !-how
foolish ! Very likely. Anyhow it was a case
with me of down lower yet and deeper still, till
I struck the level of those who hunt the cows from
their lair at night to lie warm in their place, who
feed as the pariah dogs feed.
We met then. In the sink of vice and misery
that stagnates beside the current of social life, we
came together. Husband and wife. We met at
the police-court; I for drink—l forget how on
earth I found the means for that orgy; she “on
the vag.” She was not used to it, I could see
that, watching her across the barrier that divided
men from women. The cry of “ Silence !” the
rustle, and th.en the sudden hush of the place as
the magistrate entered and took his seat, seemed
to rouse her, for she raised her head and looked
straight at the Bench. Then—the look in her
eyes as she shot galvanically to her feet, swayed a
little, finally remaining stiff, a speechless figure
of horror. They called her name —the name I’d
learnt to love her by. The charge was read, but
no sound passed her lips. The magistrate spoke
How do you plead? Guilty, or not guilty?”
His voice seemed to stir her strangely. Once,
twice, her lips parted without sound ; then, with
one more effort, the words came—“Guiltv ves •
both of ml” ’
For a second his face betrayed him. Watching
both, I saw his look of recognition—and hers. I
had found my enemy. The next moment his
voice, impassive as usual (I had been there before
and knew that voice), gave her sentence—three
months !
I was at the gate to meet her when her time was
up, and low as we had both fallen, battered as we
both were, I think we were nearer happiness then
than for many years. While she lived, the devil
of revenge was quiet, but, with her gone, it woke
again.
For weeks I shadowed him—this man of justice
who had sentenced as a vagrant the woman he had
made one—l shadowed him night and day till my
chance came.
And so to-morrow I die.
E. S. H. PARSONS.

WITHIN 2 WEEKS
or NEVER!
THERE is going to be a big change in one of the conditions of Colonial
life after two weeks more. A staple article, which used to be re-
garded as a luxury because its price put it into the luxury class -
a staple article nowadays regarded as a necessity because every
man requires it —and because for the moment it is within every-
one’s reach, will undergo a great change.
PSV'TT® ,

T\)\S Cbai)gs li)tcreStS Vo(i.
ALTHOUGH the “ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA” has not long been sold by the
DAILY MAIL upon the half-price and sixpence a day plan, so many people have bought
the book on these easy terms that we have all come think of it as a cheap book that every man
can buy. . .
Unhappily, that condition of things is almost at an end. The contract, m virtue of which the
DAILY MAIL offers the “ ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNIC A,” expires in two more weeks. If
you do not order the book at once you will soon find the original publishers’ terms again in force
when you do order it. It is hard to realise this, for, although the “ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITAN-
NICA” is only a book after all, you find it as indispensable as your daily or. weekly paper when you
have once begun to use it. Your newspaper is, indeed, incomplete without it.. You cannot
really enjoy reading the news of the day if you have no means of obtaining full light upon the
various subjects with which the news of the day is concerned. Unless you think about
what you read, the newspaper is wasted. If you think about what you read, all sorts of
questions present themselves to your mind, and the “ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA” is the
one exhaustive and comprehensive library of reference that answers all questions. If you were told
to-day that the price of your favourite paper was going to be raised to a shilling, but that you could
still obtain it for a penny a day if you subscribed for it in advance, you would make haste to book
your subscription before it was too late. In the case of the “ ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA ”
you are offered the 25 volumes at half the regular cash price charged by Messrs. A. and C. Black, the
publishers of the work, and you are not asked to pay for it in advance. As a matter of fact, you are
not even asked to pay for it on delivery ; you pay ss. now and the Daily Mail sends you the 25
volumes at once, and the monthly payments at the rate of 12s. a month by which you complete the
purchase will not begin until you are actually using the books. There.is no question about the value
to you of such an offer. The man who fails to accept it will not lose his chance, because he thinks the
chance is not worth taking, but only because he postpones too long the taking of the simple step
which brings the books to his door. You are safe if you apply to-day —that is the only sure way.
Carriage free as far as Sydney. Immediate delivery.
THE FINAL DECISION.
"THE TIMES-” Printing House Square,
[COPY.] London, E.C.
«« D ear Sir,—l regret to say that we cannot renew the arrangement under ivhich you sell * The
Times’ Reprint of the ‘ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNIC A’ for monthly pi yments. Our contract
with Messrs, A. and C. Black, is itself limited by pneise restrictions, and I may recall to your mind
the fact that when you first suggested that the * Daily Mail’ should be enabled to offer the * Encyclo-
paedia,' you were informed that if any such arrangement were made it must necessarily be of brief
duration. Yours faithfully,
“ The Editor, * Daily Mail.” “THE MANAGER.’’
The issue of the “ ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA” now offered by the Daily Mail is the Times’
Reprint of the Ninth and latest edition, as issued by its original publishers, Messrs. A. and 0. Black.
The work is divided up into 24 volumes of 850 pages each ; the 25th volume contains an elaborate index to
the whole, and a detailed list of contributors. The handsome volumes measure Ilf inches in height, 9J
inches in depth, and 2£ inches in thickness. The binding is, in every case, the best of its kind. The dark
green cloth is as strong as cloth can be, and the morocco used in the leather bindings is so rich in color, so
soft at once and tough, so fine in grain, that no one will regret the small, extra payment which secures
an added dignity and durability due to so noble a book. The Encyclopajdia contains 388 full-page plates,
671 maps and plans, and over 9000 other illustrations. For the MS. alone of the 30,000,000 words, which
occupy the 22,000 pages of letterpress, more than £60,000 was paid to the 1100 contributors, and the style in
which all this valuable matter is got up makes the work the most costly ever offered to book-buyers. It is
this most costly of books that is now, for a time, within reach of everybody.

Alex. Peacock chuckles delightedly over
Irvine having collared his Reform Bill. Mean-
time, how is Irvine, with scarce a visible majority,
going to keep his political legs ? There is always
hope for the weakest, politically or physically,
in that strength-giving beverage, D.C.L. Whisky.
Judicious appeals to that genial stimulant work
wonders in sustaining the appellants through
periods of storm and stress. The wise—in politics,
business or elsewhere—never neglect their D.C.L.
Once taken, it is never forsaken.*
Wagga Wagga Show this year takes place
on the 20th and 21st August. £925 is offered for
competition, and entries close on the sth August.
A. F. D. White, late of Echuca, is secretary this
year, being elected in May last out of 124 appli-
cants. Wagga has long been celebrated for its
Sheep Show, and this year the schedule is well up
to the mark.*
The Plague of London.
There are few events about which greater
misconception exists than the plague of London
—that terrible visitation which caused 100,000
deaths in the. year 1665, almost depopulating
England’s capital. Comparatively few people
know what manner of sickness that plague was,
for happily it has for many years been quiescent
the world over. Yet London is not without a
plague to-day, though it be less deadly than that
of 1665. A great many Londoners regard the
tax-collecting fraternity as a plague; others so
describe influenza; while some (and these with
strongest reason) apply the term to indigestion.
But indigestion is not only the plague of London ;
it deserves the greater distinction of plague of
the world, for wherever there are people there is
indigestion. Some people suffer from it the
greater part of their lives; others wisely fight
and conquer the malady, as Miss C. Halton, of
New-street, Brighton, Victoria, did. Miss Halton
writes thus, on January 15th, 1902:—“I was
fairly robust from my infancy until the age of 18,
when ! became a martyr to indigestion and
anaemia. A hard, dry cough settled on my
chest, and my friends feared that I was sinking
into a decline. My aunt, with whom I lived,
took me to Daylesford to consult a doctor, but I
went from bad to worse under his treatment.
Afterwards I .was placed in the care of a cele-
brated Victorian herbalist, who undertook to
restore. my health in three months. But just
when his medicines seemed to be doing me some
good I had a relapse and was worse than ever.
My stomach become so delicate that I was
obliged to subsist almost entirely on broths, dry
toast, and a few soda biscuits ; and even this light
and scanty fare occasioned me intolerable pain.
I remained in this wretched condition for three
years. During that period I must have consumed
gallons of various medicines, and all to no purpose.
Then my uncle, advised by a friend, bought a
bottle of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup from
Allen’s store in Daylesford. I had very little
faith in its virtues when I first began to take it,
I had been so frequently deceived by other
patented preparations. But, to my intense delight,
after a, few days I observed a change for the better.
I continued to take the medicine, and in a few
months it completely restored me to health.
When first I began to take Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup I was very thin and pallid, and
weak as an infant. Now I weigh eight stone
nine pounds, have a hearty appetite, and can
work as well as any woman in Victoria.”*

For the last half-year, comparing it with its predecessor
this looks pretty g00d—3250 tons more ore (which isn’t’
worth mentioning) gave £11,943 more profit, although
lead averaged 12s. 2d. less, and silver 2 9-16 d. less. And
it looks all the better work still, when into the picture is
taken the further item of a small decline in the metal-
value of the crude ore. The profit is equal to £2 2s. 3d.
per ton of the lead output, as against £1 17s. per ton in
the preceding half.
About the most interesting item in the B.H.
Prop, report is the announcement that the co. "has
decided to adopt the Huntington-Heberlein roasting (de-
sulphurising) process, the object of which has been ex-
plained in this column once or twice. On account of the
sulphur in the concentrates, the smelting is very slow ;
last half year the concentrates so outpaced the furnaces
that about 18,000 tons of concentrates had to be sold. The
new process will make the concentrates a better smelting
material, and it is believed that the co. will not sell any
concentrates at all. Also, the sulphur being got rid of,
the smelters will be able to get out more of the silver and
lead. Nevertheless, though it was hoping for better
things, the Sulphide Corporation was told by its chair-
man recently that the process (which has been installed
at Cockle Creek) was only just paying its way. Anyhow,
whether this process answer or not, there are enormous
savings to be made around this point; and as there is
naturally therefore keen competition among experimen-
talists in the matter, the B.H. Prop, has not tied itself
down very fast to this particular patent, and is merely
working on a royalty basis. »
* » * *
What are B.H. Prop, shares worth? One can
only answer that by asking another —What are silver and
lead worth ? On recent prices shares are not worth any-
thing like the 345. which is the market quote. In twelve
months 2s. has been paid, and in the same time the sur-
plus of liquid assets over liabilities has gone from £385,551
to £389,657, which shows that the co. has been distribut-
ing all it has been earning. Two shillings a,year—17
years to get your money back in—that is not good enough
by a long street. Of course, improvements are always
being made in extraction processes, but hitherto they
have not even kept pace with the declining value of the
crude ore. Also, of course, in the last 12 months metal
prices have been much below the average ; and, presum-
ably, it is this fact which keeps share-prices up. In that
case people who “ fancy Brokens when the metal-markets
harden ” had better paste-up somewhere the fact that the
hardening is already very liberally discounted.

Advices from London state that a prospectus
has been issued for a co. to work the Kiandra alluvial
deposits. Cable states “ capital 400,000,” but does not
indicate whether it is in pounds or shillings. The report
probably applies to a project initiated by Mr. David
Marks and his sons, who have applied for leases for 500
acres in the locality.
* * • *
Royal Standard Co., Stewart’s Brook (N.S.W.),
after years of prospecting, seems to have got on to the
continuation of the lode for which they have been search-
ing. Where discovered the lode is 2ft. thick, and is
showing gold freely. Capital exhausted and reconstruc-
tion probable.

Bulletin has it on undoubted authority that
negotiations have already been entered into with a view
to the amalgamation of Mt. Lyell and N. Lyell mines.
London directors now in Australia may affect to pooh-
pooh the statement, but they are challenged to deny it.
* * • *
Mount Morgan G.M. Co. holds its 17th annual
meeting on 28th. The reports of the working of this
unique “ show ” for the last 12 months—in the face of
more intense drought trouble than prevailed even last
year—are interesting enough. Gross tonnage of pav-
stone mined and treated for year 213,907 tons ( a decrease
on 1901 of 1554 tons). Yield, 144,5370 z. (a falling-off of
29,2960z5.) ; the approximate average of 13'46d\vt. per ton
with a cash value of 525. per ton, is also a big drop from
previous year when it stood at 16'13dwts. and 65'28 shil-
lings respectively. Average value per oz. of bullion this
year £3 18s. 7d. per oz. In addition to ore mined, 18,569
tons of old tailings were treated for a yield of 31020 z. and
a profit of 12s. Id. per ton on the treatment. These tail-
ings have been lying in a huge stack at the lower works on
the side of the creek, and thousands of tons of them have
been washed away for miles and miles with each successive
rise in the river. They were the refuse from the old
barrel process of chlorination—used in the days of the
million-a-year divs. The tailings which have thus floated
away at various times are now providing about 50 men
with a good living. They have taken up the bed of the
river for miles down as ‘ ‘ sand claims,” have put a series
of wooden troughs in, and, under the very noses of the
co., are saving the copper with which the tailings are
impregnated by the simple method of precipitation. The
co. has now its own plant on the job, so very little more
is going the way of the sand claims. The actual cash
handled for the year was £574,572, of which £570,337
came from gold. (Figures for 1900 —187,3520 z., £748,919
cash ; 1901 —173,8330 z., £691,040 cash.) Out of this
£204,166 was paid in divs. This was a shrinkage on last
year of £145,835. With the last dividend the number
since the company started has been 176, and the amount
£6,129,166.

The special meeting of the Wallaroo and Moonta
Copper Co., to be held on 28th, ought to be a lively affair.
The meeting has been called at the instance of several
shareholders who wish to sack the directors. The charges
formulated against the directors are red-hot.
* * * •
Great Fingall (W.A.), has at last topped the
five-figure mark, the return for June reading 10,4690 z.
from 6534 tons. The full 60 head of stamps are now Tun-
ing, and it is anticipated that from this out 7500 tons will
be treated monthly for about 12,0000 z. Latest reports
from mine are very satisfactory.
* * *
The June returns from the Lancefield and
Childe Harold, Laverton (W.A.), only confirm predictions
that the district had been greatly over-rated by local and
other skiters. 1772 tons for 7190 z. and 1710 tons for
700 oz. are the respective figures for these two mines. As
the average in each case is only Bdwt. per ton, including
the cyaniding of tailings, readers can easily guess that
prospects, when sulphide ore has to be dealt with, will he
anything but rosy.

Great Boulder Main Reef (W.A.), another
Moreing-bossed concern, also shows up badly for last
Revenue realised from treating 23,938 tons for
33,8600 z. was £124,028. Although this works out £5 3s. 4d.
per ton and total cost of mining and treatment is given as
±.2 3s. 6d., the only div. paid totalled £13,000, or 10s. sd.
per ton. This shows that costs are understated by no less
than £2 9s. _sd. per ton. Of course, this is for deprecia-
tion, which in W.A. is excluded from costs. As treat-
ment plant is now only fit for the scrap-heap, the finan-
cial position of 00. is considerably below zero. £2OOO has
i n J^£ ended for en S ine capable of winding to 1500 ft.,
only 500 ft. below present depth. Owing to bad roasting
in dilapidated furnace, cost of cyanide varies as much as
os., equivalent to 51b. or 61b. per ton treated. Water
costs 6s. to 7s. a ton. Ore in sight, of ounce value, on

position of co. is far fro o rosy. Below 600 ft. level no

asks shareholders to believe that it refused
£260,000 for the property. This co. also consigning gold
to London at an extra cost of £7O monthly compared with
Perth mint charges.
* * * *
Bewick, Moreing and co. have gained control
of the Cosmopolitan (W.A.) by leading the recent bear
raid, and so coming in on the ground floor. Now stated
that manager Hayes will be replaced by a more discreet
individual, who can hold his tongue and look wise, and,
as in the case of all mines controlled by the same firm,
debar inquisitive strangers and pressmen. As B.M. and
co. got 78,250 shares for the 50-head mill and 36 acres of
scrub, their power as bears can be well understood. Pros-
pects of property excellent if investors are satisfied with
controllers, as it contains a large free-milling quartz reef
hke Great FingaH, and not erratic lodes like those at
Kalgoorlie. Equipment of mine is being improved by a
new air-compressor and 20 drills, also a new winding
engine and pump. The area held by co. is 455 acres,
more than all the producing Kalgoorlie mines combined.
Main shaft is now down nearly 750 ft.
• » * *
“Cosmo” writes from Kookynie, W.A.: A
Bulletin par (21/6/’O2) states: “Cumberland Niagara
(W.A.), whose cutting of the Englishman lode has created
such a scare among holders of the Cosmopolitan,” &e.——
Well, as Cumberland co. are working the same reef now as
they have from the surface, the Oosmop. holders have
nothing to be scared about in that direction ; and it is
the Englishman lode (which is a quartz reef) all the same.
The recent strike in No. 5 level is undoubtedly the same
reef which has been worked in the higher levels,
but it is much stronger than it was there and
it carries more gold, and it is only fair to mention
that it pinched from 12ft. thick to 2ft. before the
level was finished. You can walk up the underground
northern boundary of the Englishman in the Cumberland
from No. 4 level, and in places step across and see the
same reef being stoped in the former show, as has been in
the latter. As both shows are down about same depth,
and are pegged end on to each other, and their main
shafts —both underlays—are about a quarter-of-a-mile
apart, and the reef is stronger and better in the bottom
of the Englishman shaft than it is, or has been, on any
other part of the property, it is difficult to understand
why there has been any scare at all. Development work
is not far enough ahead in either mine. While No. 5 level
was being driven in the Cumberland, under-hand stoping
was going on at No. 4, and that is where most of the
stone which was crushed in April came from. The reefs
here pinch out and make again, but the walls are always
there—which can’t be said of most W.A. reefs—and
the country is solid granite. Some of them improve at a
depth, and that is what has occurred in this one. Owing
to its flatness, the stone has to be shovelled down the
passes—it won’t run—and the difficulty is the same when
mullocking, but, judging by present appearances, the
Cosmop. can pay a good deal for extra shovelling, and
still have a bit left for divs. as well, but tipping is an
uncertain game while the stopes follow the bottom of the
shaft so closely.
* • * *
Oroya (W.A.), after wasting time and money in
diamond-drill boring from No. 8 level, has now started
cross-cutting for the continuation of the rich Brown Hill
chute. This chute, it was anticipated, would be cut in
Pomeroy shaft at a depth of about 700 ft., but it appears
to have been thrown 100 ft. to the east, which is very much
better for the Oroya, but a facer for the Kalgurli. Driv-
ing, upraising, and sinking on the chute, where already
located, are exposing very rich ore. Trewartha Jones,
consulting engineer of this and various other W.A. cos.,
is expected in Kalgoorlie shortly, so that the mines are
being well inspected, if badly handled, by’Ome authorities.
* * • •
Westralia Mt. Morgans has purchased at
auction, for £7700, adjoining Guests mine, containing 69
acres. This property was sold to J. Bull in May, 1899,
for £lBO,OOO in £1 shares in a 240,000 share co. The flo-
tation didn’t catch on, with result that only £14,000 was
subscribed, which has long since disappeared. Mine is
equipped with 10-head battery, and from all accounts, is
a cheap purchase. The new 30 heads of 15001 b. stamps
are in course of erection, and when they start will more
than double output.
At Poverty Point.
“ T.D.” : Gerardy has aged an apparent decade since he
was in Melb. little over twelve months ago. The ’cello is
the coffin of its master, as a rule. It demands such
infinite attention, so many hours upon hours of the
closest application daily, and as the virtuoso has to lean
caressingly over his instrument all the time, pulmonary
trouble is common. Strauss, the clever young Sydneyite
who died in the States three or four years back, is a case
in point. The only perfectly-healthy front rank ’cellist I
can call to mind is Hattenbach, beloved of Melb.
students. An artist to the digit-tips (where it is of course
essential), Hattenbach seems to thrive and physically
expand, instead of withering away with the rest of them.
Fitzmaurice, formerly of Rickards’, is off to India this
week with a vaudeville co., including Priscilla Verne and
about ten other people. There is always money to be
made in India by a manager who takes a fair amount of
capital and a careful selection of Australian girl. But it
is necessary to draw up the girl-contracts with a sub-
stantial saving clause. The story goes that when
Willard’s opera co. was at Rangoon, not so very long ago,
two of the lady principals seized an opportunity to get
married when the boss wasn’t looking. He had been
suspicious of the intentions of a pair of languid white
johnnies who were haunting the show, but he hoped to
get away to the next town ere it was too late. At the
last minute, however, the johnnies came along and
claimed possession of their new-made brides—couldn’t
think of letting them remain on the stage, bai jove,
doncherknow. Willard explained that the blooming
brides would be subject to a penalty of £3OO each if they
left the co., whereupon each husband in turn wrote out a
cheque for the required amount, and bore his goddess
away in triumph. £6OO seemed satisfactory damages at
the time, but Willard had to suspend business for seven
weeks before he could replace those two too-attractive
girls.
“ S.S.” : Seeing May Beatty letting herself go and re-
velling in comic ugliness, I reflect upon the rarity of the
theatrioal lydy whose art rises superior to her vanity.
Maggie Moore is, or was, wonderfully funny in a broad
low-comedy character, but I never saw her do broad low-
comedy under her own management. The only time I
remember seeing her in a truly farcical character was at
the Princess’s, in a comedy called “Our Flat,” I think,
when Mag played an influenza-smitten slavey, her make-
up being a most generous piece of caricature, and the
actress drew heaps of choice humor out of the grotesque
slattern. The world has stacks of money for the woman
who can and will do that sort of thing, but the sex would
rather live plainly than look plain.
Harry Rickards, in order to take hold of good things as
they chance along, has resolved to live half his time in
London, and keep in touch with U.S. and the Continent.
Experience teaches him that the best thing pays best,
despite his having to pay so much- better- for the best.
The variety business is a heap more reliable and safer
than the running of plays. A variety turn that gets
home effectively in London, Paris, Vienna, or N. Y., can
be depended upon almost to a certainty to get home in
Australia, too; but managers who deal in operas and
plays cannot reckon the greatest success elsewhere any-
thing of a cert. here. Their biz. is an eternal game of
chance.
May Beatty’s sister Maud at one time gave indications
of being the finest principal boy in Australia, but the
wear-and-tear of Pollard’s opera told on her voice, as on
many others, including, for that matter, the one-time
pretty mezzo that May now devotes to reciting the ambi-
tions of the slavey in “ A Chinese Honeymoon.” Also,
Maud married, and no principal boy is quite the same
when she takes to domesticity.
“Melb.”: Never saw a big audience more intensely
delighted than at a lecture by one Prof. Mudd, in Wesley
Church, July 10. He was botanist with King Ned, n&e
Prince of Wales, throughout the Indian tour 1875-6. This
lecture has been given scores of times round the back-
blocks of Australia, etc, Mudd, a bluff, blonde man,
probably a Boer, or lialf-Boer, sworn admirer of the
Prince, of course, but a genuine humorist withal. Every-
body but His Maj. came in for thumps, which faintly sug-
gested what Mudd could do with a Republican audience.
Says he—“ Perhaps, friends, you think I’m unduly exalt-
ing myself in this contact with Royalty, but I bear in
mind I’m only Mudd ! ” Like Mawworm, he likes to be
despised—at a hob a head.
Queen Alexandra is rapidly getting a London reputa-
tion for frisky tastes. She went to see “ Sapho,” and
then to “ Zaza ” (described by the press as simply “ the
impudent realism of the French comic papers”). But her
subjects should in charity remember that the Queen is
quite deaf.
“Deadhead”: Your par. re the lost allusions in
“lolanthe” omits, perhaps, the most striking of all.
“ To-night,” says one character, “ Strephon introduces
his bill to throw open the House of Lords for competitive
examination.” “ And he’ll carry it too,” replies another.
“ Yes,” assents the first, “ he’s a Parliamentary Pickford;
he carries everything.” From the first production of
“lolanthe” in Australia, no mere local saw that joke.
(Pickford is the great carrying firm of London.)

Mention The Bulletin.
The Maoriland military authorities ave
just killed off a profitable industry. The women
aV' 6 s *-ill have the kharki craze rather
badly, and at social hops, assemblies, and
even first-rate balls, men in kharki are simply
rushed by the girls, especially if the uniform
is worn by a returned cohentingenter and has
a tew dull red spots, which are understood to be
rsoer blood—and most returned cohentingenters’
uniforms have those dull red spots, even if they
come from the warrior’s own nose. So the returned
cohentingenter and the local volunteer turned a
good solid shilling by hiring out their butchering
duds to the amorous youths of the countryside,
drunk on the proceeds. It was at Gore
that the bolt fell. One warrior went in a field-
marshal’s uniform, or something equally brilliant,
and the military authorities in the hall booted him
out, and he was fined next day at the police court,
while the individual who loaned the uniform is
to be eourt-martialled. Now, no man who owns
a uniform will “ lend ” it for any price, for to be
from the service means loss of the
blood-stained clobber. And that means ostracism
by the all ged gentler sex, who used to sit with
him m a, corner and listen in breathless admira-
tion to his story of how the brown spot on the
coat was the gore of seven Boer admirals all
impaled on the bayonet simultaneously.
Concerning a female model, a surgeon
writes to The Bulletin
Once had a conversation with a respectable young
woman, living with her mother, touching her feelings on
first being employed as a model for the art-classes at
Melb. Public Library. Said the girl: “It was this
way. The teacher came to me and asked me to stand for
the Altogether. The idea 1 I was horrified, and would
not hear of it. Then he said I should only see him and
another old man ; and, after a good deal of persuasion, I
consented. On the eventful evening I was conducted to
a private room, where I disrobed. Then, as a ‘ maiden
mit nodings on,’ I ascended four steps to the top of a
pedestal draped in black, and was there posed in an erect
attitude. There was nothing before me but a big
curtain. Suddenly the curtain was dropped, and,
to my utter consternation, I found myself facing
30 young male students. My first impulse was
to fly. Then I reflected that, in order to do that, I
must descend the steps, which would put me in a worse
position than before. So I finally determined to see it
out, making up my mind that the tragedy would never
happen again. My feelings were awful. I felt the per-
spiration just rolling down my backbone. By the time
the class had been dismissed I was a little more self-
possessed, and was persuaded to stand again. With a few
experiences I got quite used to the business, and now I
don’t care a rap. Of course, no one is allowed to laugh
or make any chaffing remark ; and if a girl has confidence
in her figure she soon grows accustomed to imitating a
statue. There’s nothing in it; and the pay is very small
—only half-a-crown an hour.”
