Queensland, which brags eternally about
its educational system, spends less per head (viz.,
per pupil) than any other State of the continent,
one excepted. It has no training school for
teachers; it is ruthlessly cutting down its system
of technical education, and it has no university—
and no prospect of one. F. lucational progress
mightn’t assimilate well with syndicatism.
Despite Premier Seddon’s vaunted Im-
perialism there is absolute indifference in Maori-
land re his two favorite projects—an Imperial
Customs Union and the annexation of Fiji.
When a tired House assented to the incorporation
of the Cook Islands, Seddon stood up and started
“Gorsave.” There wasa poor response at first until
the great bass boss cast an enraged scowl at his
staunchest supporters, after which a fairly good
noise was produced. Nathless, a message duly
appeared in the English papers which would lead
anyone to believe that the Bill passed with enthusi-
asm. The designs on Fiji find no response worth
speaking of outside a small Auckland coterie led
by an ambitious lawyer and a handful of trades-
men. As for the Imperial Customs Union, few
Maorilanders seem to understand the question,
and fewer care about it. The absurdity of a
province, which taxes British imports more heavily
than nine-tenths of Britain’s foreign enemies do,
professing to be anxious to give an open door to
British goods hasn’t dawned on Maoriland yet.

Since the Morant-Handcock business and
the hot time enjoyed in Cape Town by 1700 of
our braves last May, the English Radical rags
have taken to calling the Austral gens of Cohen—
“ Britain’s Bashi-Bazouks.”
It is full time honorary justices were
relieved of the duty of revising N.S.W. jury-lists.
The fever certain M’sL.A. have developed in the
past to have their men made J’sP. was not wholly
unconnected with this element of their duty. An
amending bill throwing the revision on the police
magistrate alone is now getting ready, and will
be a boon to many inland towns.

“Like a Girl in a Book!"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wanted to go to, the -bush as a governess. I had
never known any girls who had gone, but had
often read about them. So for a week I carefully
read the “Professions and Trades” column in
S.M. Herald. At last I fOlihd the desired adver-
tisement asking for a young lady who could teach
English, French, music, singing, dancing, draw-
ing, and I determined to call on “Squatter’s
Wife ” immediately.
I put on a clean print frock, with a high linen
collar and a dark tie. The collar and the tie both
belonged' to my brother, arid inade me look very
plain, but I felt they gave me a governess-like
appearance.
When I arrived at the Hdlise inhere the “Squat-
ter’s Wife ” was staying I had to wait nearly an
hour before she could see tile. I believe she was
having breakfast. I filled in the time with
arranging what I should say to her.
Then the door opened, and a tall, grey woman
came into the room. She ttiiffllt have passed for
the Grey Lady, for she waS an entire study in
that color. Eyes, hair, and tltess were all of the
same shade, and When she spoke I felt that the
greyness had got into her very sbul. She looked
down at me, at my neck, my hands, my feet. All
over me. Then she said, “No, you are too
young.” The voice was the Saddest that I had
ever heard, and in an instant I felt that it must be
my mission in life to btin# the joy-note into it.
So I stood up and rapidly explained that I was
older than I looked, and that 1 would like to be
her children’s governess, and to try to bring the
sunlight back into her life. She seemed quite sur-
prised when I said that, and looked at me again.
I wished I had not worn my brother’s collar, for
I felt that this woman would not approve of any-
thing that was not soft and womanly. Then she
asked me a lot of questions, and laughed twice at
my answers.
At the end of an hour she told me that I would
suit her, and that she would give me twenty
pounds per anpuni. I forgot to think what “per
annum” meant, and only thought of the good
sound twenty pounds had !
She said she would pay toy expenses second-
class one way, if I stayed for Six months—if not, I
must pay them both ways myself. As I felt it
would be ridiculous to stay less than that time, if
I was going «o far away, I gladly agreed to the
terms. '
Then we parted, with the arrangement that I
would leave Sydney the following week.
When I went home, I gave my sisters a vivid
description of the Grey Lady, and brought an
envious light into their eyes when I said I felt I
had found my mission in life,.
The days went by quickly, and when, at
last, all the family stood on the railway-station to
see me off, I was half-dazed with excitement.
I got into a ladies’ carriage that was filled with
fat women, with .paper bags. But that did not
matter. I was going to the bush as a governess !
After the train started* I noticed there was
another girl in the carriage besides myself. She
looked moA. and I wondered what she was going
to be! ■- ■ .
It was very hot, and, as a’.few drops of rain were
seen on. one of the windows* they were all closed
at once. The carriage Was Stifling, and the fat
women were settling themselves for the night.
Some of them took their hair off and their teeth
out. I looked at the other girl, and we smiled at
one another. After that* we began to talk in
low voices. She was also going to be a governess,
and had just left school. She told me she could
teach drawn-thread, ribbon-work, and a lot of
other fancy-work that I had never heard of.
She said she had a black velvet ev.ening-gown,
made very long, with elbow-sleeves, and that she
intended wearing yellow roses—real ones, of
course—with it. She asked me if I thought she
would have to dress for dinner ! I wasn’t sure,
but fancied she would. Governesses in books
generally wore black gowns, just a little low at
the neck, with a rose nestling amongst the black
net, and another in the hair.
I wished I had brought a black velvet, instead
of my white book-mUslin. It seemed so simple
now ! My sisters had told toe to always wear on
the left shoulder a cluster of scarlet berries off the
sweet-briar bush; they Said that they knew I
could always get plenty of them on a station. The
other girl said that they would look very sweet,
and that she wouldn't mind wearing them some-
times herself for a change,
We talked on through the night; arranged how
we would meet in OUr long lonely rides and tell
one another our Experiences. Then, as the grey
dawn was breaking* I said good-bye to her, and
stepped out on to the little platform in the
drizzling rain. In the half-light I saw a man
peering about for someone, and I went to him and
asked if he was looking for me. He said no, that
he had cotoe to meet Mrs. Jones’s new governess.
I told him that I was she, and he whistled softly
as he looked me.
After he had got my luggage—l had a good deal,
for I had brought everything I possessed in the
world ; I had been advised to do so, for one in-
variably wanted the very thing that had been left
behind—W6 got into a buggy and drove to a funny-
looking house. The man told me that it was an
hotel, and that I was to breakfast there and rest
till 9 o’clock, when he would call forme to drive
me out to Yowbah Station.
I felt Very like a girl in a book as I went into
that place and heard the man tell a sleepy-looking
girl to get me some tea and to see that I had
everything I wanted. Then he left, and I was
shovfrn into a dark room, where I had my tea, and
Went to deep on the sofa.
Whon-i woke up I heard the rain pouring down,
.artd wondered if the man would think it too wet
to come for toe.
Just theh the girl put her head in and said that
“the gentleman” was in the parlor. He was
{lulling up the blinds as I went in, and I saw his
ace clearly for the first time. He was very
brown, and had no beard—at which I was sur-
prised ; and when he asked me if I was rested' I
noticed that his teeth were beautiful. He then
explained that he was a nephew of Mrs. Jones,
and that she had asked him to meet me. I said
that I supposed we could not start until the rain
stopped. He laughed, and said he was afraid there
was no chance of it doing that for a week. He
wrapped me up in a huge oilskin coat, put an oil-
skin cap on my head that tied under my chin, and
lifted me into the buggy again. And we drove
away through the blinding toin into the bush.
It was the strangest drive I ever had, with
the rain beating in my face —the wind was too
strong to put an umbrella up—and the strange man
at my side.
After we had gone a few miles he began to
talk. He asked- mb questions, and when 1 told
him that I had never been away from home
before, and had never been a governess, he
whistled again. I told him that I felt sure that I
would like it, only somehow the bush looked
different from what I had expected. He said it
was a bad day to see it for the-first time. lhen
he told me. that he couldn’t get over the idea of
me being the new governess. I was frightened at
that, and asked him if he thought.that I didn t
seem clever enough to do the work, hie said it
wasn’t that, but somehow I was quite different from
the governesses that they’d had at Yowbah
station before. Hold him that I was much older
than I looked' -1 would, be nineteen next birth-
day—and that I could be very stript. He laughed
and did fret- say &ny more for a lohg time. _
It was nearly 3 o’clock when he-told me that we
would bo at v YoWbah in about five minutes. I
was glad, for I was very tired and hungry. Just
then I saw a white cottage with a verandah round
it away over in a paddock. I asked the man who
lived there, and he said that was Yowbah.
“ But, ” I gasped, “ that isn’t a station, surely ?
He assured me it was, and just then I saw a
woman come to the door and I recognised my
Grey Eady ! - *
The man helped me out and I stumbled up to
the woman. She asked me if I was wet, and told
a long legged girl who was staring at.we from the
passage to show me my room. I got out of my
soaking oilskins and followed the girl into a tiny
room with a very low ceiling.-. The first thing I.
noticed was that the bed was not made, and the
sight made ine feel homesick. The room .was so
small I could scarcely turn round. There was
nothing on the floor, nothing on the window but
a bit of brown paper to fill up a hole.'
The girl stood and watched me as I washed my
face, and I kept it buried purposely for a long
time in the towel, so that khe would not see that
my eyes were wet. Then I told her that I was
ready, and she took me to the dining-room. The
cloth was on the table, and ‘there was cold mutton
and stewed plums for dinner.
The man was standing talking to the Grey Lady,
and he smiled at me as I came in. I tried to eat
some mutton, but it nearly choked me. The Grey
Lady suggested that I should have some plums.
There was no cream, no milk. I ate them because
I dared not refuse. And outside the rain was
pouring down and the wind was moaning.
I heard the man tell the Grey Lady that he
could not stay the night, but must get back at
once. She went out of the room for a minute, and
I dropped my spoon on the floor, put 1 my head
down and sobbed. The man said “ Ob, little girl,
don’t do that. You must not break down now.
“ I can’t stay in this lonely place by myself. . . .
I want to go home ! ” I sobbed.
“ You’ll soon get used to it, and my aunt isn t
such a bad sort when you know her, but for
Heaven’s sake don’t.let her see you cry. She hates
anything like that.” . And he gave my arm a little
shake. I gulped back the tears, and dried my
eyes, and wished that I "were dead.
Then the Grey Lady came back and the man
went. , , - .
Two tall, thin girls, with long dresses and their
hair up, peeped in at the window, and the mother
told me they were two of my pupils. She said I
might sit on the verandah with them until tea-
time. I was longing to go to my room and lie
down, but was too frightened to say so.
So we three sat on an iron seat and looked*.out
on the grey, soaking paddocks.
The girls were very quiet. They asked me it 1
was engaged, and if I bad any bracelets, and when
I said no to both questions they seemed to quite
lose all interest in me.
The next day-was Saturday, and the Grey Lady
said we would not start school until Monday, but
she wished to have a talk with me in the school-
room. So I followed her there and she shut the
door. She said she had taken a great fancy to
me —I was so bright—so amiable ! I opened my
eyes very wide and wondered what she was going
to do to me. Then she said that her other three
daughters would return home that afternoon, and,
as they were such intelligent girls, she knew I
would be delighted, to teach them witli the other
two. I could n’t speak. Me witli five pupils ! It
was funny! And she called me “Dear!” and
said I was a little treasure. She also told me that
her nephew was a great flirt, and that he was
engaged to be married to a very rich squatter’s
daughter. . . , „ „ . .
The next day—still raining—we all, hve girls,
Grey Lady, squatter and I, packed in a huge
waggonette and went to church. It was about
three miles away in a tiny village.
At the church door I was introduced to the
clergyman—he had a cross eye—who asked me if
I was playing the organ that service. I laughed
and said I had never touched an organ in my life.
He looked surprised, and -I thought Mrs. Jones
did not seem pleased.- There was no music that
day! . .
Coming home I was told that it- was the custom
for the governess at Yowbah to play the organ
and lead the choir.
So for a week, every afternoon after school, 1
walked —with three of the girls—over to that
quaint little church to find out the ways and
means of playing a bush organ. -
When the next Sunday came and I sat m front
of about 40 people and pulled out the necessary
stops, I could scarcely breathe for nervousness.
As it had been suggested to me that a voluntary
would be required, I started, with trembling
fingers, toplayoneof the “Songs without Words.”
It was the 19th and I only knew three lines on
the organ. I played them once softly, played
them three times, four times, five times, six times
■_ wou ld the clergyman never come ? —I dared not
stop for fear of the awful silence that is so
unnerving in a small building. I played those
lines 20 times, until at last I was so perfect in
them that they sounded like a jubilee march.
Then, as 1 was growing reckless, the clergyman
came.
The days went by. My five pupils—two or
whom were older than I—were harmless enough,
but very stupid.
On very rare occasions visitors came, but they
were very few and far between.
Then the autumn set in.
Rolls of flannel were produced, and I was told
that it was time for me to start the girls’ winter
clothes ! Now, this was the hardest thing that I
had yet been asked to do. I had been told many
times that I was the greatest comfort that Yowbah
had ever had in the way of governesses. And I
was beginning to understand why.
I had never made clothes for anyone in my life.
Dare I tell the ,Grey Lady ? She had a queer way
of looking unconscious, as though stone-deaf, if I
ever told her anything like that.
So I held my tongue, and started on a frock for
Liz. Liz Was 6ft., and luould have her frocks
made with revers!
I can’t speak calmly yet of that fearful frock.
The tnemory of it stiil hurts, for I had to unpick
it —every bit of it—before the Grey Lady’s eyes.
I remembered how I had told my sisters that I
had'foutid my Mission in Life. If they could have
seen me sitting at night over that hateful frock,
u ndoing all that I had so fearfully done, would
they have laughed—or cried ?
• * * *
I had never seen my companion of the train-
journey, but had been told that the people she
was with were very common, and only had a small
selection. And she had wondered if she would
Dress for Dinner ! I wondered if the black velvet
had ever been worn —my white book muslin still
lay in my trunk, and was likely to do so till I
went back to Sydney. .
I stayed at Yowbah for six months, and so
earned my first ten pounds, two of which went
in paying for my return-ticket.
Eight pounds for teaching five girls English,
French, to play the piano, to sing, to dance; for
playing a bush organ and leading the choir; for
making five girls’ underclothing, and unmaking
one girl’s frock. Eight pounds for learning by
experience what it meant to be “Like a Girl in
Book.”

“ Westinghouse ” : Chinese luck is pro-
verbial, and the expression “must have killed a
Chinaman ” has acquired much significance in
this district, since, about six weeks ago, a Chow
was killed by a train near Dunedin. Since then
we have had no fewer than seven railway acci
dents —more or less serious—on Dunedin-Oamari
section. The number of accidents on the section
for the previous two years was but two.

Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills heal the kidneys and help them
to work naturally and to remove absolutely all disease-breeding

BUT BE SURE THEY ARE DUAN’S.
The Root of it all.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now this js the cry of the "People - who ; clamor of
Hardship and-Wrong:
“ Save us . the trouble. of thinking— let us not
ponder for long! ; ; •o: '
Let us be fat and be merry—let us be"careless and
glad, 1 i ■
Labor of brain is a burden that maketh the merry
one mad.
‘ Give us a despot to rule us—Minister, Kaiser,
or Czar.
Moulding the State is a trouble—leave us the laws
as they are !
True we may hate the Oppressor, and call him
accursed as Cain,
But worse do we hate we should labor with breed-
ing of thought in the brain.
“ Give us a cut-and-dried system—lend us a code
and a creed ! ,
Save us the trouble of saving the souls of a weari-
ful breed !
Lend us a ladder to lift us—grant us a lamp and a
guide,
And bear us, at peace and recumbent, up where
the Seraphim bide.
“ Save us the trouble of flunking—save us the
doing of deed—
Hath He not fashioned us nidd’ring, backward
and slothful at need ?
Therefore a fetish we bow to, therefore a despot
we crave,
Therefore we fondle our fetters and creep to an
honorless grave.
“Let us be fat and be merry—let us be vacant and
glad,
Labor of brain is a burden that maketh the merry
one mad.
True, we may murmur a moment, and curse at the
King in the car,
But, rather than think and be doing, oh, let us have
things as they are / ”

P.L.
“Out of the Mouths of Babes.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sylvia was deliciously happy. The lane had
been long, but she was in sight of the turning at
last, and could even see a little bit of the happy
land beyond. For weary years she had been
plodding along that dreary thoroughfare, oft-
times footsore and sick at heart. But the weari-
ness was past, and now she was to reap her
reward, for he was coming ! He was the end of
the lane, even as he was the end and aim of every-
thing as far as Sylvia’s loving heart was concerned.
So sister Margaret and everyone else in her little
world said, therefore it must have been true; but
even they would now be forced to own that the
waiting had not been in vain.
How proud and happy she was, and what a
tangle her thoughts were in! What would he
say first, and how would he look, and would he
think her much changed or (and she hesitated)
o&Z-looking ? Crossing the room, she looked
anxiously at her reflection in the foggy mirror
over the fireplace. Since the days of her “ teens ”
she had never analysed her face so closely, for
vanity hactnever been her weakness, blit now, not
a line escaped her, and she was far too-honest to
blind herself to the fact that she was beginning to
show the wear and tear of Time. . Her patient,
tired eyes had a multitude of little marks round
them, and that long line—she had almost said
wrinkle— could that be any relation to the little
dimple that used to lurk in her cheek, ready to
dance forth at every smile? And her chin that
had once threatened to become double, had
become sharp instead. She saw, with a shock,
that there was a certain faded look about her alto-
f ether, that even-the dim, old glass could not be
lamed for. Indeed, if it had not been for its
kindly mistiness she would have looked more
faded jstill, and she knew it. She was much too
human not to give a sigh for her vanished youth,
but much _ too simple to attempt to keep awful,
uninteresting middle'-age'at bay, and would have
thought it almost a sin. She just accepted it, and
was very sorry, more for her belated lover’s sake
than ljer own.
It was fortunate that he would not See her in
the cruel sunlight first, thought Sylvia—not that
he would weigh her shortcomings against her. He
was too generous ; and with childlike faith she felt
had she been quite ugly he would still think her
beautiful. Did he not love her ? Still, she Wanted
to look as much as possible like the Sylvia he left
so many years ago ; and she sighed again 'as she
brushed her still pretty hair behind her ears and
straightened the uncompromising collar that she
wore round her throat, calling attention to its
thin outlines; and mentally reviewed the simple
dress with its fluffy edgings that she had worn
that last evening when he said good-bye. Bessie,
her niece, who was laughing and talking on the
Verandah, was wearing just such a one to-day, but
Bessie was seventeen, and she was thirty-five!
Did she look it ? Again she anxiously consulted
the friendly glass, and this time persuaded her-
self that she hardly looked twenty-five, certainly
not thirty—her cheeks were, for the time being,
almost as pink as Bessie’s own, and her eyes
almost sparkled; certainly happiness was most
becoming. Sylvia laughed nervously. She, who
was always so unruffled and whom people
called sedate, was in a flutter of excite-
ment, and she tried to force herself into some-
thing like her usual repose by reading “The
Percy Anecdotes ” as a soothing dose, but she
could not interest herself in anything but her own
happy prospects; and presently the book slipped
from'her fingers to the floor.
Looking at the clock for the hundredth time,
she was startled to find it so late, and was struck:
by an awful thought. Perhaps he wouldn’t come,
after all—it was so long ago, and he might have
forgotten. He had been away in the world and
might not have kept bright the memory of
those blissful months, as she had done,
she who had stayed at home, with only
that one vivid patch of green in her life
of drab to think of. Could he forget? Surely
not! Had she not prayed for him for years, and
bravely defended him from the sneers of Margaret
when she persisted in prophesying that he was
“married and settled-down long ago.” But
Sylvia had never doubted him, had believed in
him from the first, and now put away a 3 unworthy
a,ny fear.as-to his return. Return he would, and
as teue as herself. ■ T . ■ • .
The>6ld-fasHlohed room,' with low. ceiling and
scanty ventilation, oppressed her until she felt
taint and dizzy, and, going to the window, quietly
pushed it open. The little draught of air and the
pungent scent of ; the. carnations revived her, and
thee ev.qry-day-sounds,' the hum of insects, and
careless chatter of voices outside, calmed her con-
siderably.,, E ven the', parrot, chattering monoto-
no-JfTln - stable, helped to steady her nerves.
-heeling much better, she took up her position
in one-corner of the old sofa, the springs of which
had long since succumbed to the weight of a suc-
cession of lovers, many of whom , had run the
course of true love smoothly, frorn start to finish,
while she had been waiting patiently on the
brink. He and she had helped to take the spirit
out of that ill-used piece of furniture. She had a
tender feeling for it, and blushed to think she
might, m the near future, sometimes dispute its
possession with Bessie, who seemed to think she
was the only one who could possiblybe enthroned
there, with Love to whisper in her ear. And
then Aunt Sylvia blushed again to think she oould
put herself into competition with that chit,
through the open window Bessie’s assured
tones were heard, and s.he was talking lightly,
slightingly even, of the man Sylvia had wor-
shipped and'held sacred for almost half a life-
time :
''Aunt’s old sweetheart is expected to-day,
Martha,’ said Bessie; “He’s been away ages,
making money and getting dried-up and shrivelled,
like the snake-skin in Jack’s collection.”
How sweet! ” sighed Martha, who was noth-
ing if not sentimental—“ Is lie the one, coming
back to his first love ? ”
Ihe listener’s heart was gladdened—others
believed in him, too, then ! He was coming home
to his first love ! But, then, Bessie again : “He
may be her Ideal, though what even she can see to
like in a dried-up stick of a man like that I’m sun
•* “ on know—Jack says he looked over-cooked
when he saw him two years ago. Of course that’s
only his vulgar way of describing aunt’s old boy.
He s so conceited, you know, and thinks everyone
a scarecrow unless they’re as red and beefy as he
is himself.” (Sylvia shuddered.) .“Still he can’t
be handsome at his age. Besides, ma says he
won t have any eyes for aunt when he does come.
He won’t be in love with her now. She’s quite an
and not as pretty as ma is.”
But she’s still passable,” answered Martha,
who was leniently disposed towards the woman
whom hope deferred had aged more than actual
years, “and isn’t he old too, much older than
she ? ”
Oh, that’s of no account at all. If a man is
well-tailored’his age goes for nothing,’’said Bessie
impatiently, “and men never come back to their
first love nowadays—it’s out of date. If a girl’s
m’.sk W as h goes she should get another, never
mind how, as long as she does. For if you don’t
marry, the younger generation looks down on you,
and your family think you’ve disgraced them.
Believe, me, Martha, constancy is at a discount
now-a-days; it’s not valued as much as the
novelettes would have us believe. You’ll see,
when Aunt’s lover comes he’ll be the same as the
rest of them, and overlook her.”
Sylyia looked spellbound, and listened against
her will to her niece’s sweeping opinions conoern-
***!? nian - As in a dream she heard her clatter on.
Most likely he will be snapped up by one of us
younger ones, and if I am the lucky girl I’ll say
yes ’ and be thankful, even if he is as withered
as this peanut, because he is so rich. Ma says
money is everything. You can live quite com-
fortably without love, but you might as well be
dead; as-be poor. As for an old maid like Aunt',
she has no chance. v , ~
An icy hand clutched the old maid’s heart/ Slip
was grey as ashes, and in her eyes happiness had
given place to dull despair. Hertfiopes and her
love were humbled to the dust by the careless,
heartless chatter of these young, creatures glorying
m the possession of youth. For the first time she
saw herself with their merciless eyes—and hope-
lessness made her look more than her age. And
when, a few minutes later, and before she had
time to pull herself together, her visitor entered
the room, she felt that she fitted the part. The
T? aif^a mantle had indeed fallen on her
shoulders. Her heart broke when she saw that he
saw it too—saw in her nothing but a faded, em-
barrassed woman, standing limply before him in
dull, dejected silence.

This is how the picturesque recent casting-
out of Daniel O’Connor, M.L.A., is drearily
described in the “Votes and Proceedings” of
N.S.W. Assembly:
Disorder.— The hop. member for Sydney-Phillip division
having'Jrequently disregarded Mr. Speaker’s ruling,, that
ms remarks were irrelevant, was, by direction of Mr
Speaker, removed from the Chamber by the Sergeant-at-
Arms. .
“Votes and Proceedings ” is a dry .publicatibn-
dry as the dust of old Egyptian dynasties, or as
the bones of the Carthaginian aristocracy. Yet
what a saleable work it could be made if it were
illustrated by capable artists with a disregard of
facts, and had corset-advertisements on the spare
space! The hon. and irrelevant member defying
the Speaker with his beard flying in all directions!
The -Speaker threatening to dot the irrelevant
member with the mace ! The Sergeant getting
his coat off to shift, the member ! The Sergeant
trying to lift the irrelevant member and remove
him ! The Sergeant reporting to the House that the
irrelevant member was too globular and he couldn’t
get a firm hold of him ! The Sergeant reporting
to the House later on that he had tried to haul
the member out by the collar, and the collar had
bust, *and that he had tried to shove the member
and he wouldn’t shove worth a copper ! The
O Sullivan putting a sum on the Loan Estimates
to pay for the removal of the disorderly member
as a permanent and reproductive work 1 And
then, immediately after, an advt. setting forth
that Bildad’s liver-pills remove all obstructions,
and a tonic-advt., stating that if the Sergeant had
only drunk two kegs of Dodblaster’s Ciant Reno-
vator he.could have removed the whole Opposition
with one' hand!
“Pankaroo ” tells of John’s narrow
■escape:—
Cycling down Sydney Road (Melb.), t’other morning
about 5 o’clock, suddenly espied, in the middle of the
tram-track, a vegetable John suspended by a rope around
his neck from the push-bar of his two-wheeled truck, toes
just touching the ground and face the color of watered
ink. Thought at- first it was a novel case of suicide
but further inquiry showed that it was pure accident’
John had started for market with a load weighing nearly
half a ton, and had hitched himself to the cart by a rope
The wheel caught and skidded in a tram-rail, which caused
the load of vegetables to lose balance and slip to
the rear, and up John had to go. Wrigglinn- to get
himself free the loop of the rope got around his throat
and the rest was only a matter of time. Upon being re-
leased, John cursed slowly and effectively in Celestial
language for half a minute or so, then philosophically
re-trimmed his cargo and set off town wards
